APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        This report appraises a project that is the first initegrated live-
stock development program in Yemen Arab Republic. The project is designed to
initiate a long-term program to improve livestock development and production
from a subsistence base for which the infrastructural services such as animal
health, extension, marketing and credit are practically non-existent. As a
source of food, draft power, raw material (wool, skins) and reserve capital,
livestock form a key component of the farming system and rural economy.
Through demonstration and the introduction of modern production and marketing
techniques by Government and investment in livestock related industries, the
private sector which includes the target group would be stimulated to assume
major responsibility for the future development of livestock resources.
Through the erection of simple, efficient and hygenic livestock slaughtering
and meat marketing facilities public health standards would be improved.
Livestock carrying capacities would be improved in range areas through com-
bined pasture improvement, controlled grazing practices and the introduction
of browse trees and shrubs and commercial timber species. Social and rural
development benefits to producer families would be realized through increased
incomes, improved animal health and production services and improved nutri-
tional and living standards. Finally, investments in livestock and range
improvement would ensure long-term development in livestock production.
ii.       The project would provide for (a) the formation of a national Live-
stock Development Corporation (LDC) which would be set up as a semi-autonomous
Government owned commercial enterprise. LDC would establish eight 50-cow
dairy farms, a livestock fattening-dairy production farm, five milk collec-
tion colonies and a poultry-feed mill complex; (b) the development of three
urban slaughterhouses and 154 municipal retail market stalls to be operated
by the Ministry of Public Works and Municipalities. So as to increase foreign
exchange earnings exportable hides and skins improvement would be included as
well as meat inspection services; (c) support of animal health services of the
Directorate of Animal Health of the Ministry of Agriculture with disease con-
trol and diagnostic services, veterinary field stations, and quarantine facil-
ities at Hodeidah; (d) supervised credit to about 900 traditional sector,
small holder livestock and poultry owners, landowners and irrigated fodder
crop producers through the ACF. Additionally, about 84 retail butchers would
receive credit for an urban retail meat market improvement program; (e) estab-
lishment of a livestock and range improvement center in the Intermontane
Plains area. A village range development component whereby 1,250 hectares of
suitable land would be improved for grazing and planted to provide limited
quantities of building materials and fuel. All components under the project
would be supported by technical services inputs, training grants, technical



- ii -
studies and the provision of financing for expatriate technical staff neces-
sary to implement the project. The project would also receive significant
institutional support from UNDP/FAO technical assistance projects. The on-
going United Kingdom Overseas Development Ministry (ODM) program in animal
health services and in livestock and range improvement would be expanded and
coordinated with the requirements of the project and UNDP/FAO would supply
training facilities for local project technicians and staff.
iii.      The project would be implemented by a Project Implementation Unit
(PU) which-would be responsible to a Livestock Development Board (LDB). The
LDB would be chaired by the Minister of Agriculture and composed of represen-
tatives from involved Ministries and Government institutions. In addition
to LDC, a Municipalities Slaughterhouse and Markets Division (MSMD), an
Animal Health Division (AHD), and a Livestock and Range Improvement Division
(LIRD), would be set up under the PU to implement their respective project
components. The Supervised Credit Component would be implemented by the
Agricultural Credit Fund (ACF) in cooperation with the PU.
iv.       The total project cost would be US$32.1 million (YRls 144.6 million)
of which 53% or US$17.0 million (YRls 76.5 million) would be foreign exchange.
The proposed IDA credit of US$5.0 million would finance 16% of the total proj-
ect cost. The Kuwait Fund and the Netherlands would contribute US$11.6 mil-
lion and US$7.5 million respectively toward the project cost. ODM would pro-
vide the equivalent of US$2.4 million in bilateral aid financing. The Gov-
ernment would provide about US$4.6 million and the remainder of US$1.1 mil-
lion equivalent would be financed by subborrowers.
v.        The Government would be the borrower.  It would channel the IDA
credit, the Kuwait Fund loan and the Netherlands bilateral aid through ACF.
ACF would on-lend part of the proceeds (US$9.0 million) for subloans to (a)
LDC for investments in dairy and poultry production, milk collection, market-
ing, livestock finishing and feed processing. (b) Village Development Author-
ities for investments in range improvement and (c) to traditional sector sub-
borrowers for on-farm investments in livestock and poultry production. The
interest rate charged to the subborrowers would be at 9%. ACF would also be
the administrative channel for Government equity grants for the above sub-
projects and for the grant to MSMD for investments in slaughterhouses and
retail meat markets.
vi.       Vehicles, equipment, facilities and services financed by the Nether-
lands, the Kuwait Fund and ODM bilateral aid would be procured in accordance
with their respective guidelines. Procurement under the supervised credit
component and financed by the Kuwait Fund, the Netherlands and IDA would be
procured locally through existing commercial channels over the six year proj-
ect implementation period. Consultant services required for implementing the
project and financed by IDA and the Kuwait Fund would be provided by an inter-
national firm selected on a competitive basis.



- iii -
vii.      The project would provide substantial benefits to parl:icipating
livestock producers all of whom are in the target group and the national eco-
nomv.  It would lead to decreased animal mortality, improved management prac-
ticcs  and increased rural income due to the availability of credit for ex-
panded livestock production. Substantial incremental production of livestock
products would result from the project: 26,000 tons of milk, 4,000 tons of
meat, and 1,000 tons of eggs per year by 1985. This would be equivalent to
20% of projected deficits of these items by 1985, thus contributing to for-
eign exchange savings. The overall economic rate of return for the Project
is projected at 27%; in addition, there are substantial non-quantifiable be-
nefits including improvements to public health through hygenic Livestock
slaughtering and meat marketing facilities, the training of 460 Yemenis in
diverse skills, and through the building of institutions which would make a
positive contribution to economic progress of the YAR beyond this project.
Further long-term progress in dairy production, meat and poultry production,
range improvement, animal health, municipal livestock slaughtering and meat
marketing, as well as agricultural credit, would be achieved the creation and
strengthening of institutions and programs under this project.
viii.     With the assurances listed in Chapter VII, the project would be suit-
able for an IDA credit of US$5.0 million to the Government of YAR on standard
IDA terms.






APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of the Yemen Arab Republic has requested the Inter-
national Development Association (IDA) to help finance a livestock credit and
processing project aimed at increasing the supply of meat, poultry, animal
feeds, dairy products and breeding livestock both on small producer and
larger scale Government-owned enterprises. Additional objectiLves include a
national animal health program, long-term range improvement for livestock
feeding, improvement in foreign exchange earnings through reduced imports of
livestock products and increased earning from hides and skins export, and
improved sanitary conditions for slaughtering and marketing oi- livestock pro-
ducts in the major urban communities.
1.02      The total project cost would be US$32.1 million (YRIs 144.6 mil-
lion) of which IDA would finance US$5 million (YRls 22.5 milllon). The proj-
ect would be implemented by a Project Implementation Unit (PU) which would
be responsible to the Livestock Development Board (LDB) and a:Lso by the
Agricultural Credit Fund (ACF). The LDB would be established under the
Ministry of Agriculture. A Livestock Development Corporation (LDC), a Muni-
cipality Slaughterhouse and Markets Division (MSMD), an Anima:L Health Divi-
sion (AHD) and a Livestock Range Improvement Division (LRID) iwould be estab-
lished under the PU for implementing and operating their respective com-
ponents of the proposed project. The LDC would be a semi-autonomous Govern-
ment commercial enterprise while the other units would, after project imple-
mentation, become part of the ministries.
1.03      The proposed project would represent the fourth IDA credit for agri-
culture and agroindustries in YAR. Previous projects were thes Tihama Develop-
ment Project (TDP) Credit No. 376-YAR, the Southern Uplands Ruiral Development
Project (SURDEP) Credit No. 545-YAR and a Grain Storage and Processing Project,
Credit No. 636-YAR.
1.04      The Government of YAR, in 1974, requested the Kuwailt Fund for Arab
Economic Development to finance a project identification and preparation re-
port of a national livestock development program. The projecit was prepared
with Kuwait Fund financing in early 1975 by C. H. Chisholm and Associates
(C.I.) Ltd. The project was concurrently appraised in November 1975 by the
Kuwait Fund and IDA, the IDA team consisting of Messrs Schertz and Hing (IDA)
and Ashworth (Consultant). Mr. Nickel (IDA) also assisted in the preparation
of the report.



- 2 -
II. The Agricultural Sector
A. General 1/
2.01      Agriculture contributes about 70% to GDP, nearly 90% to the value
of exports, and employs over 80% of the labor force. Cotton, coffee, hides
and skins, and cotton seed are major agricultural exports. In 1974, cotton
and cotton seed accounted for 60% and coffee for 10% of total export value.
Food imports, nearly half of which were cereals, accounted for about 50% of
the total import value.
2.02      YAR, with an area of approximately 20 million ha, has a population
of about 6.5 million and a resident population of 5.2 million. A substantial
number of Yemenis work in neighboring countries. The population is growing
at an estimated annual rate of 2.4% despite high mortality. Only 1.5 million
ha of the total area are regularly cultivated; however, during the 1974-75
cropping season an additional 2.0 million ha of marginal land was brought in-
to production as a result of sufficient preplanting rainfall. Most of the
cropped area is classified as rainfed, with only 230,000 ha (15%) being under
some form of supplementary irrigation from wells, perennial springs or per-
iodic wadi floods. Climatic conditions vary widely, and because of variable
mountainous topography microclimatic differences are locally important. Rain-
fall is erratic, and annual variations influence crop production by affecting
not only per hectare yields but also utilization of rainfed and marginal crop-
lands. On the basis of rainfall, altitude, and crop-livestock pattern, five
main agricultural zones can be identified in the country; (a) the Coastal
Belt of the Tihama; (b) the Western Slopes; (c) the Midlands; (d) the High-
lands; and (e) the Eastern Slopes. Available data on land use, agricultural
production prices and production value are given in Annex 1.
2.03      Land Tenure and Taxes.  The land tenure system in YAR is tradition-
based and complex. The great proportion of total agricultural land (about
80%) remains under the control of large individual owners who generally lease
their land under sharecropping arrangements. State and religious institu-
tions directly own 2-3% and 15 to 20% of the land respectively which are
usually farmed by tenants. The tenant is expected to supply required family
and bullock labor input and the landlord the full cost of hired labor. Pro-
cedures for further cost and income division between landowners and tenants
differ widely and the form of tenancy agreement varies with soil fertility,
the availability of water and the nature of the crop. In most cases, material
input costs, taxes, and net production are divided between the tenant and land-
owner in fixed proportion, with the tenant's share ranging from 25% of well-
irrigated to 50% on rainfed land. These arrangements, in most cases, appear
equitable. 2/ Agricultural production is subject to a 10% tax (Zakat) based
1/   For further discussion of YAR's economy see current Economic Position
and Prospects of the Yemen Arab Republic, IBRD Report No. 840-YAR.
2/   See Annex 1 for details.



-3-
on gross value of output. Livestock activities are generally assessed accord-
ing to the number of animals owned, and crop production on well--irrigated land
is taxed on a reduced rate of 5%. There is no special statutory provisions for
taxation of poultry, milk, or wool, and therefore these items are assessed ac-
cording to the general 10% rate. 1/ Commercial firms whether semi-autonomous
Government enterprises or private are taxed progressively up to 25% on net in-
come after deducting depreciation.
2.04      Although to date no long-run development plan has been prepared,
the Government's major objectives for the agricultural sector are: (a) an
annual increase in agricultural production of not less than 6%, and the dev-
elopment of the necessary agricultural storage, processing and marketing
facilities; (b) achievement of long run self-sufficiency in basic - food-
stuffs particularly cereal crops, oils, vegetables and livestock products;
(c) improvement of the trade balance through the increase of exports espe-
cially cotton, hides and skins, fresh and canned fish, vegetables and fresh
fruits; (d) improvement of the dietary standard of the population; and (e)
provision of credit and supporting extension, marketing and input distribu-
tional infrastructure for agricultural producers, especially the smaller farm-
ers. Beginnings have been made in establishing an extension service and a
credit mechanism under on-going regional development projects, but in spite of
these and other development initiatives, it appears that the first three objec-
tives appear to be based on assumptions which are too optimatic. The target
growth rate for agricultural production as well as self-sufficiency in cereals
are considered unrealistic targets for the foreseeable future. Although the
importance of the agricultural sector is recognized, YAR's capacity to imple-
ment successful programs is hampered by (1) inexperience in systematic program-
ming, (2) lack of institutional infrastructure, and (3) a weak resource base
offering limited opportunities for growth. In an attempt to modernize the
sector and increase productivity Government is concentrating its efforts on
overcoming institutional weaknesses and improving the supply of all basic
agricultural inputs (farm equipment, fertilizers, seeds, extension service).
B. The Livestock Sub-Sector
2.05      Livestock contributes significantly to the resources of the rural
economy, which in 1973 produced 66% of domestic income . In the financial
year 1972/73, livestock products, principally hides and skins, accounted for
19% of total export value. In 1973, YAR imported livestock and meat products
(YRls 0.7 million) and dairy products and eggs (YRls 10 million).
2.06      The estimated livestock population of 12 million sheep and goats,
900,000 cattle, 600,000 donkeys and 100,000 camels is concentrated in the
cultivated areas of the country with the following approximate distribution:
1/   See Annex 1 for details.



-4-
Cattle          Sheep and Goats
Tihama                                 500,000            4,000,000
Southern Uplands                       200,000            2,400,000
Central Highlands and
Intermontane Plain                   150,000            4,000,000
Eastern Slopes                          50,000             1,600,000
2.07      Livestock forms an integral part of the farming system.  Since
farmers produce mainly for subsistence, the plow oxen is first in importance
and the milk cows are second. Typically, a family owns two oxen and one
milk cow. In addition, a farmer may own sheep and/or goats which are often
grazed with neighbor's stock on common lands. He may also own a small flock
of chickens. Livestock represent work power, a source of family food, a re-
servoir of cash capital and the means to obtain needed items through barter
or cash transactions. The output from these animals consists of culled cat-
tle, sheep and goats as needed, sour milk and butterfat, and some chickens
and eggs, wool and hair, and hides and skins.
2.08      Livestock productivity is low.  Contributory constraints include
inadequate disease control, frequent prolonged drought and overstocking on
small communal grazing areas, inadequate supplies of quality fodder crops
due to competition for cereal and food crops and inadequate supplies of ir-
rigation water, lack of trained extension services, lack of organized credit
facilities and the problems faced by the tenant farmer in providing adequate
and acceptable guarantees for loans. With these constraints annual offtake
of cattle is estimated at 9% and milk yields at 200 litres per lactation.
Offtake of sheep and goats including unrecorded domestic slaughterings, is
estimated at 35%, and the milk yields at 17 litres per lactation. This off-
take of cattle and small stock and milk yields are comparable to other coun-
tries in the Middle East and Africa that have similar constraints on product-
ivity. On the other hand, livestock offtake and milk yields range to 300%
higher in countries of the region which have reduced such constraints.
2.09      Livestock Marketing.  Marketing in YAR is poorly developed.  Animals
are sold in primary markets, and outlying villages often make up a small herd
for sale at the nearest market, sometimes two or three days trek away. Cattle
traders are the main buyers although trade is done with butchers on their own
account. Livestock sales are made through independent bargaining between sel-
lers and buyers. Road transport is used extensively to move animals to
larger urban markets. The markets are administered by Government officials
and a tax is levied on transactions of 2-1/2% of the sale price. No sales
records are kept, nor are receipts used. There are no weighing facilities,
bargins are struck on basis of estimated weights and no premium is paid for
quality.
2.10      Meat and Poultry Marketing.  Most locally produced meat, poultry
and dairy products are sold in traditional urban and village markets. Fa-
cilities are primitive and very unsanitary with minimal storage capacity.



- 5 -
Products are sold daily with little or no carry over. Meat is sold in a
package which includes offal without being weighed. Poultry are sold live
at uniform prices with little premium for weight. The retail trade in im-
ported frozen meat, poultry, fish and dairy products is conducted entirely
by a small but growing number of urban retail markets in the main towns.
Sales of livestock tend to peak at certain times of the year, and are in-
fluenced by availability of cash, state of the pastures, timing of tax pay-
ments, market prices for livestock and crops and important religious feasts.
Farmers also use livestock, dairy products and poultry for bartering for
needed items which are locally produced.
2.11      When compared to international prices livestock, dairy products and
poultry farm gate prices are high in YAR; for example beef prices are 30%,
lamb 50% and poultry 100% higher. Government does not control prices on
livestock and livestock products nor are retail and wholesale meat and dairy
product prices fixed. Live cattle, small stock, and poultry market prices
approximate prices of beef, mutton or goat, eggs and milk are YRls 14 per kg,
18 per kg, 6 per doz., and 2 per liter respectively. However, these prices
vary considerably throughout the country.
2.12      Consumption.  By using available export and import data (1973), esti-
mated livestock, and poultry population and productivity levels which indi-
cate a production of 33,000 tons of red meat, 7,000 tons of poultry meat,
6,000 tons of eggs and 140,000 tons of milk equivalent of dairy products,
the implied annual per capita consumption is 7 kg of red meat and poultry
and 23 kg of milk equivalent in dairy products. Cereal consumption in 1973,
was equivalent to a per capita consumption of 292 kg per annum. Approxi-
mately 75% of average caloric and 72% of protein intake is derived from the
consumption of cereal-based food items.
2.13      Livestock Diseases.  Endemic diseases are widespread and are a major
constraint in the development of livestock productivity. Foot and mouth dis-
ease, camel pox, sheep pox, and caprine pleuropneumonia are widespread and
major causes of mortality, reduced milk yields and sub-optimal work perform-
ance. Similarly, internal parasites such as liver fluke, sheep nostril fly,
and the sarcoptic sheep scale are major problems. Rinderpest and Newcastle
disease periodically devastate the ruminant and poultry population, respec-
tively.
2.14      Livestock Health Services.  Services available to tb,e farmer are
supplied principally by the small veterinary unit set up in 1972 by the
Ministry of Agriculture with ODM technical assistance and support. The unit
consists of a central diagnostic laboratory and a field team. The immediate
objectives were to investigate the animal diseases affecting livestock pro-
duction and to establish a national health service. The field service team
has met with favorable producer response and demand for its services is
greater than can be provided with existing staff and facilities.



- 6 -
C. Agricultural Credit
2.15      Background.  Institutional credit to agriculture in YAR has been
limited to seasonal credit for cotton growing. Farmers primarily depend on
their own resources, remittances from relatives from abroad and other non-
institutional sources such as friends and traders for investments in agricul-
ture. The Yemen Bank for Reconstruction and Development (YBRD) finances agri-
cultural production through loans to the Cotton General Corporation (CGC)
which on-lends to farmers at no interest rate for the purchase of seasonal
inputs against the security of their cotton crops. YBRD holds 30% of the
share capital of CGC which has exclusive cotton ginning rights. Cotton market-
ing finance is provided by a Government assembled consortium of private banks.
Parliament in 1975 passed a law establishing a wholly state-owned Agricultural
Credit Bank (ACB) under the Ministry of Agriculture which began operations on
July 1, 1976 with a paid in capitalization of YRls 20 million, and of an
authorized capital of YRls 100 million to be subscribed by 1980. Government
is considering merging the Agricultural Credit Fund (ACF) with ACB and has re-
quested IDA to assist in this effort.
2.16      Agricultural Credit Fund.  In order to provide a mechanism for the
credit component of the Tihama Development Project (TDP), Government estab-
lished a small credit unit (ACF) within the Central Bank (CB). ACF has since
been selected as the financing channel for the Southern Uplands Rural Develop-
ment Project (SURDEP).  Under both the projects short-, medium- and long-term
loans are to be provided to farmers for purchase of agricultural inputs and
capital investments. ACF has established its head office in Sana'a with
branch offices at Wadi Zabid and Wadi Mawr; for the SURDEP, offices at Taiz
and Ibb will be established.
2.17      ACF is headed by a Manager (expatriate), stationed at the Sana'a
head office, who is responsible for the formulation of policy for board ap-
proval and for its implementation. He also formulates procedures for ACF's
operations and has undertaken training and supervision of local staff. The
branch offices would be managed by an expatriate who would be responsible for
training Yemeni loan officers in loan appraisal and other operational duties.
Thus far, ACF has been understaffed and in need of more transport facilities
for loan officers so as to reach subborrowers for loan inquiries as well as
for supervision and collection of loans. ACF credit activities began in 1975
with the approval of a modest number of subloans under the TDP. With the
credit effectiveness of the SURDEP in January 1976, increased lending activity
is expected in 1976.



- 7 -
III. FUTURE TRENDS
A. Livestock Production, Consumption and Imports
3.01      Production.  The present total land area under cereals of between
1.3 and 1.9 million ha depending upon rainfall, will at best remain unchanged.
Livestock, poultry and dairy production, under normal rainfall conditions,
are expected to average an annual growth rate of 1.0% and to reach about 11
thousand tons of beef, 28 thousand tons of mutton and goat meat, 9 thousand
tons of poultry and 160 thousand tons of dairy products by 1990 (Annex 2,
Table 5).
3.02      Consumption.  The most important factors affecting future demand for
livestock products in YAR will be population growth and rising incomes. At
present population is growing at an estimated average rate of 2.4%; this is
expected to rise to 3% per annum by 1990. The expected GDP growth for the
period 1976-1990 is between 4% and 5%. The calculation of demand for live-
stock products is shown in Annex 2, Table 5. The Table takes into account
these figures for population and GDP growth as well as assumed income elastic-
ity for livestock products estimated at 1.0. The resultant demand projections
indicate a deficit of 4,600 tons of beef in 1980 rising to 30,000 tons in 1990.
Mutton and goat meat show a substantial deficit of 41,900 tons by 1990. Im-
ports of beef are increasing and would be substantial if present demand trends
continue. Deficits of eggs, poultry and dairy products are projected to con-
tinue and are expected to rise to 11,650, 11,250 and 237,550 tons respectively
by 1990.
3.03      Imports.  The value of animal products in current terms over the
last several years represented only a minor portion of total imports into YAR.
However, while total imports almost tripled in nominal terms over the 1969-
1973 period, imports of animal products displayed even higher growth from half
million YRls in 1969 to 14.7 million YRls in 1973. Dairy products and eggs
dominated the livestock commodity import category with 2,858 tons in 1973.
Projected supply deficits in livestock commodities by 1990 woulcL result in
significant increases in imports.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Objectives
4.01      The project is designed to form the initial phase of a long-term
program to expand livestock development in YAR and to improve and strengthen
the fragile base upon which it now depends. Through demonstrat:ion and the
introduction of modern production and marketing techniques by Government and
investment in livestock related industries, the private sector would be sti-
mulated to assume major responsibility for the future development of livestock



resources. Parallel with this, about 400 individual livestock producers and
500 private poultry farmers would be assisted with long-term credits. Public
health standards would be improved by the project, particularly through the
erection of simple efficient and hygenic livestock slaughtering and meat mar-
keting facilities. Carrying capacities would be improved in range areas
through combined pasture improvement, controlled grazing practices and the
introduction of browse trees and shrubs and commercial timber species. Social
and rural development benefits would be extended to about 1,000 producer fami-
lies, all in the target group, and 10 villages through increased income, im-
proved animal health and production services, and improved nutritional and
living standards. Finally, investments in livestock and range improvement
would ensure long-term development in livestock production.
4.02      The project would include:
(a) the formation of a national Livestock Development Corporation
(LDC) which would be set up as a semi-autonomous Government
owned commercial enterprise. LDC would establish eight -
50 cow dairy farms, a livestock fattening-dairy production
farm, five milk collection colonies and a poultry feed mill
complex.
(b) the development of three urban slaughterhouses and 154
municipal retail market stalls to be operated under the PU
by MSMD. Hides and skins improvement to increase foreign
exchange earnings would be included as well as meat inspec-
tion services.
(c) support of animal health services of the Directorate of
Animal Health of the Ministry of Agriculture with disease
control and diagnostic services, veterinary field stations,
and quarantine facilities at Hodeidah.
(d) granting of supervised credit to about 900 traditional
sector landowners, small holder livestock and poultry
owners and irrigated fodder crop producers through the
ACF. Additionally, about 84 retail butchers would receive
credit for an urban retail meat market improvement program.
(e) establishment of a livestock and range improvement center
in the Intermontane plain area. A village range development
subproject whereby 1,250 hectares of suitable land would be
improved for grazing and planted to provide limited quanti-
ties of building materials and fuel.
4.03      In addition, all components under the project would be supported
by technical services inputs, training, technical studies and the provision
of financing for expatriate technical staff necessary to implement the proj-
ect. The project would also receive significant institutional support from



UNDP/FAO technical assistance projects. The on-going ODM programs in animal
health services and in livestock and range improvement would be expanded and
coordinated with the requirements of the project and UNDP/FAO would supply
training facilities for local project technicians and staff.
4.04      The Project Implementation Unit (PU) would have the responsibility
for establishing the varius operational divisions and managing the proposed
project. The PU would be responsible to the Livestock Developmnent Board
(LDB) consisting of the Minister of Agriculture as Chairman, representatives
from the Ministry of Finance, Ministry of Municipalities and the Central Plan-
ning Organization and the General Manager of ACF, the Project Manager and
Co-Manage. LDC would be a legal entity formed and capitalized by Government
to undertake commercial activities in dairy and poultry production, marketing,
milk collection, livestock finishing and feed processing. MSM]D would be set
up under the PU to implement and to operate the slaughterhouses and retail
markets in Sana'a, Taiz and Hodeidah. The supervised credit component would
be implemented by ACF in cooperation with the PU. ACF would aLso be the lend-
ing channel for all subloans and the administrative channel for Government
grants and equity for subprojects.
B. Detailed Features
Livestock Development Corporation
4.05      LDC would consist of three production divisions:  Dairy, Livestock
Finishing and Poultry-Feed Processing.
(a) Dairy. The dairy division would comprise eight 50-cow
commercial dairy units located in the Sana'a, Ibb, and Taiz
regions and five associated milk colonies. A consultant
study would assure the suitability of the selected or
proposed sites for irrigation, the crop production pro-
posed and the availability of acceptable water. Invest-
ments would include tube-wells with a water distribution
system, land leveling and preparation, farm machinery,
light trucks, buildings, stockhandling facilities, port-
able 2-cow vacuum milkers either electric or motor operated
and about 500 disease free guaranteed in-calf, commercial
grade Friesian cows and 10 registered pedigree bulls. In-
cluded would be provisions establishing five milk coLlection
colonies each to be located within proximity to one of the
divisional dairy farms, individual participating farmers and
urban markets.
(b) Livestock Finishing Farm. A 250 ha irrigated livestock
finishing farm including a 50-cow commercial dairy farm
would be located in the Tihama where, primarily, forage
would be fed to livestock for fattening. Included in the
subproject are provisions for access roads, land levelling



- 10 -
and preparation, irrigation system, buildings, stockhandl-
ing facilities, farm machinery, vehicles, silage bunkers
and about 3,600 ewes, 70 rams, 60 dairy cows and 2 registered
bulls. A consultant study would assure the suitability of the
site selected for livestock production, irrigation, the crop
production proposed and the availability of acceptable water.
(c) Poultry-Feed Processing. A poultry feed mill complex would
be constructed in the Taiz area to produce about 125,000
broilers annually and to supply pullets for the establish-
ment of about 500 flocks of 50 birds each to individual
farmers. A small feed mill with an annual capacity of
3,000 tons on a single shift basis would be constructed to
provide poultry and livestock feed. Included would be pro-
visions for access roads, land preparation, fencing, civil
works, equipment and machinery, installation, engineering
fees, vehicles and working capital.
4.06      LDC would be a semi-autonomous Government enterprise with an equity
base equivalent to approximately 40% of the total investment. Government
would provide the equity from the loans. Its charter would enable it to
undertake the proposed investment program, to acquire land for its facilities
to operate on a commercial basis, and to make contractural arrangements for
materials, equipment, civil works, supplies and services. Assurances were
obtained that land would be made available for this component.
Slaughterhouse and Retail Markets Component
4.07      The MSMD would be established under the PU for constructing and op-
erating three urban slaughterhouses and 154 retail markets in Sana'a, Taiz and
Hodeidah. This component would consist of:
(a) after project implementation, Year 6 of the project, the
responsibility for operation of MSMD would be transferred
from the PU to the Ministry of Public Works and Municipal-
ities which has the overall responsibility for the super-
vision of all slaughtering facilities and retail meat
markets in the country.
(b)  construction of new slaughter facilities in Taiz and
Hodeidah and the completion of the abattoir in Sana'a
which was initiated under an UNDP/Government agreement but,
due to the lack of funds, has not been finished. The
abattoirs would be simple, closed structures complete with
on-the-rail dressing system, hot water, hide shed, and a
layout to permit efficient slaughter of livestock while
meeting the religious rituals of the community. Included
would be provisions for a delivery vehicle at each slaughter-
house to provide delivery service to convey carcasses and
offal to the retail markets.



- 11-
(c) construction of 154 retail meat market stalls appropriately
distributed in the three urban centers. The retail markets
would be simple enclosed structures with interior-suri-aces
finished with easy cleaning non-absorbent materials. Each
stall would be aproximately 9m2 and would have a surfaced
and drained courtyard. Water and electricity would be
provided.
(d)  implementation of a hides and skins improvement program in
the urban slaughterhouses to reduce losses due to poor
flaying and salting methods.
(e) training of local butchers in livestock slaughtering and
skinning techniques by MSMD divisional staff.
(f) overseas training of eight trainee meat inspectors who would
receive six months supplementary tuition in a Middle ELast
or East African country with conditions similar to those of
YAR. Three suitable trainees would be sent to a Midd:Le
East or East African country for three months training in
abattoir management, plant operation and hygiene.
Animal Health Component
4.08      A small veterinary service entered upon a diagnostic 'Laboratory in
Sana'a has been set up under a bilateral aid agreement with ODM. This unit
would be used as a nucleus for the greatly expanded animal heallh program
under the project. Under a new agreement, the ODM Veterinary Project is to
be substantially expanded and would provide a portion of the staffing and ex-
penditures included in this project.
4.09      During implementation, financing would be included for construction
facilities and for staffing required by the Director of Animal Health to es-
tablish a small but comprehensive field staff, backed by adequate diagnostic
facilities and veterinary investigation staff. A small headquarters exists
and both the Director of Animal Health and the project manager (ODM) have
been appointed and would continue in their respective positions in the AHD.
They would supervise the construction and equipping of regional centers and
the enlarged diagnostic laboratory and be responsible for organizing both
in-country and overseas training of veterinarians and veterinary assistants.
Diagnostic laboratory facilities in Sana'a would be enlarged and an animal
clinic, stores and garage built. Three regional veterinary centers would be
established in Sana'a, Taiz and Hodeidah. Also a quarantine station would be
built in Hodeidah.
Livestock and Range Improvement Component
4.10      As part of the UK bilateral aid to YAR, a comprehensive survey which
includes ground water studies is being made to identify priority avenues for



- 12-
agricultural development and recommend suitable programs for implementation.
In conjunction with the ODM Montane Plains program, an Agricultural Improve-
ment Station with a Range Improvement Center would be established near Damar.
Furthermore as part of the range improvement studies, 10 pilot Village Devel-
opment Associations (VDA) would be established. Work would be carried out on
methods of improving livestock production from rainfed land including range
improvement component which would examine forage crops, draft power husbandry
and means for improving communal grazing areas. Investment would include
buildings, vehicles, equipment, on-farm development and livestock. In addi-
tion to ODM expatriate staff who would cover a wider range of disciplines,
the project would include 2 specialists in the fields of livestock production
and range improvement.
Supervised Credit
4.11      Traditional Sector.  The component is aimed at the development of
livestock production through the provision of producer credits supported by
extension services. The supervised credit program would assist small sheep,
goat and dairy farmers to finance investments to increase production and also
would introduce small scale commercial poultry production in the traditional
sector. Since livestock production cannot be treated in isolation in a sub-
sistence agriculture, the credit program would finance an integrated on-farm
development package as outlined in Annex 7, para 4. The component would pro-
vide for subloans to landowners for on-farm infrastructure investments and to
their tenants for investments to increase livestock production, and it would
also encourage formation of small producers' groups or associations who would
otherwise not qualify for credit.
4.12      About 900 subloans would be made under this component.
(a)  24 loans to landowners for the provision of wells, irriga-
tion works and stock-holding facilities,
(b)  375 loans to tenants to increase forage production and build
up livestock production,
(c)  A total of about 500 loans to small holder poultry farmers
for 50 bird flocks.
Projections for the development of each type of production unit are based on
the models in Annex 7. Included in the component would be supervisory and
technical services to support the development program.
4.13      Under the component funds would be made available to finance inputs
and machinery for the production of forage crops, livestock, tube- and dug-
wells, small scale irrigation works, simple stockhandling facilities, poultry
housing, buildings, and the introduction of better herd and flock management
practices.



- 13 -
4.14      Meat Market Improvements.  To improve the standards of hygiene
within the retail markets proposed in the project, subloans under the super-
vised credit component would also be made available to about 84 butchers for
purchase of hand tools and equipment including small commercial refrigerators.
The renting of stalls in the new markets would be conditional upon the main-
tenance of improved meat handling techniques and hygiene standards by the
butcher-vendors.
Project Staffing and Training Program
4.15      YAR has only a limited availability of technical and specialized
management competence to implement and operate the proposed facilities. In
addition to establishing PU, LDC, AHD, LRID and MSMD, the project would pro-
vide for a major institution effort in expanding and strengthening ACF in co-
ordination with the Grain Storage and Processing Project. Included would be
financing for the following technical services, staffing services and train-
ing required for project implementation, monitoring and evaluation:
(a) Project Unit Manager;
(b) Financial Assistant;
(c) LDC staffed with an experienced Manager, a Dairy Division
Manager, a Poultry Feed Mill Division Manager, a Livestock
Finishing Division Manager, a Master Mechanic and an Irriga-
tion Crop Manager;
(d) AHD staffed with a Manager and 12 technical specialists by ODM.
One additional senior Veterinarian would be provided for from
other project sources;
(e) LRID staffed with an experienced Livestock Production special-
ist and a Range Improvement Expert;
(f) MSMD staffed with a Manager and Hides and Skins Specialist;
(g) ACF staffed with a General Manager effective Year 3 of the
project 1/, a Livestock Credit Officer, a Farm Management Ex-
tension Officer and an Accountant-Financial Controller to
assist in building the institution;
(h) overseas training, including 12 one to two year fellowships
in post-graduate studies for senior managerial and technical
staff, 45 three to six-month training courses for meat in-
spectors, veterinary assistance, animal husbandry specialists,
credit officers and LDC staff; undergraduate training for 10
veterinarians and animal husbandry officers; and,
1/   General Manager would be provided to year 3 under the TDP.



- 14 -
(h) the financing of two studies which include reviewing site
characteristics, tenure status of selected sites and the
possible effects of relocation of tenants.
(i)  Livestock Finishing Farm Site and the Wadi Surdud
Farm Study. The study would investigate the Tihama
region for selection of the most suitable site for
locating the Livestock Finishing Farm. A farm de-
velopment plan including technical assistance and a
farm management program would be prepared under the
Wadi Surdud Farm Study to continue to operate the farm
after the technical assistance being provided by the
USSR is completed.
(ii)  a site study to locate the most suitable sites for the
eight 50-cow dairy farms being financed by the project
would be the basis of subproject approval.
4.16      With the exception of ODM experts, the senior veterinarian and the
ACF General Manager the expatriate experts listed in (a)-(g) above are ex-
pected to be provided through a single contract with a specialized management
firm. During the negotiations, assurances were obtained that the project
features as described in paras 4.05 through 4.15 would be undertaken.
C. Implementation Schedule
4.17      Project implementation would begin in 1977 (see Annex 11, Table 1)
and would require about six years. Appointment of key project staff, site
acquisition, retaining the services of a management firm to furnish expatriate
staff and the establishment of LDB are key steps which would be essential for
expeditious project implementation and early action has been proposed for each.
D. Cost Estimates
4.18      The total project cost is estimated at US$32.1 million (YRls 144.6
million) of which the foreign exchange component is 53% or US$17.0 million
(YRls 76.5 million). The foreign exchange cost includes the cost of imported
equipment and materials, expatriate personnel required or the construction and
installations of project facilities, expatriate consultants, training abroad,
and the foreign exchange component of materials used in civil works constuction.
Detailed cost estimates are presented in Anenxes 4 through 9.
4.19      Cost estimates are based on end 1975 prices.  A 10% physical contin-
gency is allowed on the feed mill and 5% is included for the other project com-
ponents. An average price contingency of 69% of total project cost including
physical contingencies has been allowed for expected price inflation and are
summarized as follows:

The proposed IDA credit of US$5.0 million would be made to the Government on
standard IDA terms and would finance the foreign exchange portion of the
project expatriate staff and 27% of the supervised credit component subloans
made by ACF. The Kuwait Fund loan would be extended to the Government on
terms similar to the IDA credit; it would finance (a) the total investment
cost of the LDC dairy farms component and PU, (b) the investment costs of
the milk colonies component, (c) the investment costs of the livestock
finishing farm component, (d) the total costs of the site selection studies,
(e) the foreign exchange costs of the Animal Health component, (f) the for-
eign exchange part of the ACF organizational investment costs, (g) the local
costs of the expatriate expert staff, (h) the medium-term loan to ACF for
onlending to subborrowers for interest costs during construction and seasonal
credit, and (i) 14% of the supervised credit component subloans made by ACF.
The Netherlands bilateral aid would be extended to the Govermnent on terms
similar to IDA credit; it would finance, (a) the investment costs of the LDC
Poultry-Feed Mill, the LRID, VDA, and MSMD components, and (b) 59% of the
supervised credit component subloans made by ACF. The ODM bi:Lateral aid
would finance a portion of the animal health component and would be a grant
to Government. Government would finance the working capital requirements for
the milk colonies and livestock finishing components and the :Local costs of
the Animal Health component and the ACF organizational investment costs.
It would also finance any cost overruns which may occur. Agreement was ob-
tained during negotiations on these arrangements. The signing of agreements
between the Government and the following would be conditions of effectiveness
of the IDA credit: (a) Kuwait Fund for a total of US$11.6 million, (b)
Netherlands Bilateral Aid for a total of US$7.5 million, and (c) ODM for a
grant equivalent to US$2.4 million.
F. Procurement
4.21      MSMD, LDC, LRID and AHD Components.  Vehicles, equ4pment, facil-
ities and services financed by the Kuwait Fund, the Netherlands and ODM bi-
lateral aid would be procured in accordance with their respect:ive guidelines.
4.22      Consultants and Supervised Credit.  To provide the t:echnical ex-
perts required for implementing the project, who would be financed by IDA
and the Kuwait Fund, Government invited proposals from several, management con-
sultant firms. After evaluating the proposals and with the concurrence of IDA
and the Kuwait Fund, Government is negotiating a contract with the selected
firm. Purchases of goods and materials under the supervised credit component
by the traditional sector subborrowers would be numerous, small off-the-shelf
type items (which could not be bulked for bidding purposes) procured over the
six-year implementation period of the project. These items would be purchased
by the investors under the ACF's supervision directly from local commercial
channels. During negotiations, assurances were obtained that the procurement
procedures contained in this paragraph would be followed.



- 18 -
G. Disbursements
4.23      The IDA credit would be disbursed over six years.  Disbursements
would be made for 100% of the foreign exchange cost of expatriate staff serv-
ice and 27% of the ACF subloans for supervised credit. Disbursements for ACF
subloans for supervised credit would be made against certificates of expendi-
tures. Supporting documents and contracts for these subloans would be re-
tained by ACF and would be available for review by IDA during supervision.
Assurances-to this effect were obtained during negotiations.
H. Environmental Impact
4.24      The project would have a favorable environmental impact by improv-
ing sanitation and hygiene in slaughtering activities and in retail markets.
The project is not expected to have significant adverse environmental impact
on the country.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Project Organization
5.01      There is no present institution in YAR with the capability to im-
plement the project. A PU would be established to organize and manage the
project during implementation. The PU would have an internationally re-
cruited, experienced staff and headed by a Project Manager and a senior
Yemeni co-manager, who would be a agricultural graduate. The PU would be
responsible to LDB for the execution of the project. LDB would be estab-
lished under the Ministry of Agriculture by decree of the Council of Min-
isters. Overall responsibility for project coordination and policies would
be vested in the LDB. It would consist of the Minister of Agriculture, as
Chairman, a representatives of the Central Planning Organization, the Minis-
try of Finance, the Ministry of Municipalites and the General Manager of ACF,
the Project Manager and Project Co-Manager. The Board would establish devel-
opment objectives and guidelines and serve as the coordinating body for mat-
ters involving cooperation between various Government departments, institu-
tions and bilateral aid organizations.
5.02      The LDC would be established under the PU and would be a semi-
autonomous Government-owned enterprise established to own and to operate on
a sound commercial basis about eight 50-cow dairy farms, five milk collec-
tion colonies and a poultry-feed mill complex. Government drafted the LDC



- 19 -
charter prior to negotiations and Government's approval of LDC's charter satis-
factory to IDA would be a condition of making the proposed credit effective.
5.03      MSMD would be established under the PU for constructing and operat-
ing the slaughterhouses and retail markets in Sana'a, Taiz and Hodeidah dur-
ing project implementation. After project implementation, Year 6 of the proj-
ect, the responsibility for MSMD would be transferred from the PU to the Min-
istry of Municipalities. MSMD would continue to be responsible for the opera-
tion of these slaughterhouses and retail markets, and the regulation of all
other similar facilities within the country. During negotiations assurances
were obtained from Government that:
(a) regulations acceptable to IDA would be established to cover
the slaughterhouse and market regulatory criteria outlined
below. These regulations would take effect when the facil-
ities constructed under the project become operational.
(i) no slaughter would be authorized within Sana'a, Taiz
and Hodeidah except at the Government slaughterhouses;
(ii) the Ministry of Municipalities would take steps to
enforce meat inspection at the slaughterhouses;
(iii)  the retail markets being constructed under the project
would be licensed and maintained in a sanitary manner;
(b) slaughterhouse operating procedures outlined below would be
carried out.
(i) slaughter, delivery and retail market fees would be
charged to make the facilities financially self-
supporting;
(ii) animals would be slaughtered by MSMD employees and car-
casses and offal would be delivered by the slaughter-
house to retail markets; and
(iii)  MSMD would charge sufficient fees for their services
to operate on a sound financial basis the slaughter-
houses and retail meat markets.
5.04      The AHD would consolidate and expand the administrative and field
experience of the UK technical assistance (ODM) and the Ministry of Agricul-
ture's Directorate of Animal Health in the subproject. ODM would provide the



- 20 -
expatriate manager and the majority of the expatriate technical staff for im-
plementing the animal health program. The LRID would in conjunction with ODM
Montane Plains and Wadi Rima Program establish an Agricultural Improvement
Station with a Range Improvement component near Damar in the central Montane
Plains.
5.05      ACF would be expanded by adding regional branch offices and support-
ing staff to provide credit on a country-wide basis for implementing all com-
ponents of the project. ACF would be responsible, in cooperation with the PU
for implementing the supervised credit component of the project to the tradi-
tional sector subborrowers. Assurances were obtained during negotiations
that the project organization outlined in paras 5.01 to 5.05 would be estab-
lished.
B. Project Management
5.06      The Project Unit Manager would be responsible for organizing the
PU, the operating divisions and would assist these divisions in the prepara-
tion of subprojects, in the procurement of goods and services and in subproj-
ect implementation; advise on the selection of staff and terms and conditions
of employment; ensure that expenditures comply with the terms and conditions
of the credit documentation; introduce accounting and auditing controls with-
in the units; assist with the selection of staff for overseas training and to
coordinate internal training programs provided under the project. The Pro-
ject Manager would initiate and supervise studies for the dairy farm sites,
livestock finishing farm site, and Wadi Surdud farm. The Project Manager
would approve all loan applications for LDC, LRID, and VDA investments prior
to submission to ACF assuring that each subproject meet the criteria of the
project. The Project Manager in cooperation with the Division Managers would
formulate operating and administrative procedures for approval by the LDB.
The PU head office and LDC would be located in Sana'a while LRID would be
housed in Damar. Assurances to this effect were obtained during negotiations.
5.07      Technical Assistance and Training.  A major risk for the success-
ful implementation of the project is the severe shortage of experienced and
competent staff in YAR. To overcome this constraint, comprehensive technical
assistance and training programs have been incorporated into the project. The
project divisions would be staffed with 29 expatriate management and technical
specialists, for periods ranging from two to five year (see Annex 10, Table
1). These experts would be responsible for both project implementation and
for training of their local counterparts, who will take over management and
technical responsibilities when the expatriates complete their assignments.
The recruitment experience under the Southern Uplands projects indicates that
such experts and local counterparts are available. Government has agreed to
initiate early recruitment of expatriate experts and would through interna-
tional advertising engage an experienced agricultural management firm which



- 21 -
would supply all the experts as a team. The consulting firm would assume res-
ponsibility for selection, replacement and compensation in concurrence with
the Government, IDA and the Kuwait Fund. The terms of reference for each ex-
patriate position are provided in Annex 10. Local counterparit staff who are
expected to take over operational and managerial responsibilities after the
project's implementation period would undertake comprehensive training before
they assume their positions. The training would include specialized foreign
training for key personnel, undergraduate and graduate education for vete-
rinarians, and in-house training. Assurances were obtained during negotia-
tions that the selection of a management firm for implementat:ion of the proj-
ect acceptable to IDA and the Kuwait Fund and on terms and conditions satis-
factory to IDA and the Kuwait Fund would be a condition of credit effective-
ness. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that local staff as
described above would be appointed within six months of the credit effective-
ness. Assurances were obtained that the various training programs satisfac-
tory to IDA would be implemented.
C. Credit Arrangements
5.08      ACF Organization.  ACF, which was established under the TDP, would
be the lending channel for all subloans extended under the project. In addi-
tion, ACF would be the administrative channel for Government contribution to
LDC equity; for the Government grant to the MSMD component; anLd finally for
Government contribution to PU, AHD, LRID and ACF. ACF would appoint a senior
livestock credit officer, a farm management extension officer and an account-
ant financial controller in its head office and open two new offices, one
branch office in Sana'a 1/ and one field office in Hodeidah and expand its
Taiz/Ibb offices. Local counterparts would be assigned to expatriate ex-
perts; the new and expanded offices would be appropriately staffed with local
personnel. The branch manager in Sana'a would be responsible for organizing
the Sana'a branch office while the Hodeidah field office would fall under the
responsibility of the Wadi Zabid field manager. Since the services of the
expatriate ACF manager is being financed through the project year 2 under the
TDP, this project would finance his services during the last four years of
project implementation.
5.09      Credit Arrangements.  Of the LDC investment costs, 40% would be fi-
nanced by direct Government equity contribution and 60% by a loan. Govern-
ment would contribute 40% of the VDA's investment costs and ACF would make
loans for the remaining 60%. Of the supervised credit investment costs for
the traditional sector, subborrower contribution would be 20% and the remain-
ing 80% would be by a loan of ACF. For the credit portion of t'he above invest-
ments, the Government would on-lend part of the proceeds of thie IDA, Kuwait
1/   Would be physically located in ACF's central office to handle the Proj-
ect as well as the Grain Storage and Processing Project Credit No. 636-
YAR.



- 22 -
Fund, and Netherlands loans to ACF (US$9.0 million) at an interest rate of
4.0% per annum. ACF would maintain a spread of 5.0% which would be suffi-
cient to cover its administrative costs (average 3.2% of loans outstanding),
to make provision for losses (1.2%) of credit repayments on average during
the lifetime of the project) and to allow ACF to accumulate a reasonable
amount of equity (0.6%). In addition, ACF would also be the administrative
channel for the Government contribution to LDC equity (US$2.8 million) and
for the Government grant contribution to the PU (US$16.7 million). ACF would
not charge any commission or fees on the equity or grant portions that would
be administratively channelled through it by the Government.
5.10      Lending Procedures.  For the LDC subprojects managers assisted by
the LDC manager and staff and supervised by the PU would prepare subloan ap-
plications including detailed investment costs, projected income and cash
flow statements and economic analysis and submit them to ACF. For the
slaughterhouse and retail markets components, the MSMD supervised by the PU
would prepare grant applications supported with a similar documentation as
indicated for LDC subloans and submit them to ACF. For the village range
improvement component, the LRID supervised by the PU would prepare and submit
loan and grant applications to ACF. For the above components, ACF would re-
view subloan applications and evaluate the financial viability of the invest-
ment proposal. For the livestock finishing farm, the first two dairy farms
and milk colonies, the poultry-feed mill unit and the first slaughterhouse
ACF would, after its review, forward the subloan applications to the Kuwait
Fund and the Netherlands for approval, with appropriate recommendations. In
view of their staffing constraints IDA has explored with the Kuwait Fund and
the Netherlands the possibility of concurrently reviewing and commenting on
these documents but in any case IDA would receive an information copy.
5.11      For the traditional sector credit component, ACF extension agents
from branch offices would advise farmers in livestock and poultry development
and would encourage them to use the credit facilities of the project. These
agents would assist potential subborrowers in preparing loan applications for
submission to branch offices which would evaluate the creditworthiness of the
applicants' financial liability of the investment proposal and farm of se-
curity and the availability of the investor's own contribution which would be
at least 20% of the investment cost.
5.12      Investment Categories.  ACF would make loans to LDC for eight dairy
farms, a livestock finishing farm, five milk colonies and a poultry-feed mill
unit totalling US$4.1 million (YRls 18.6 million) (see Annex 5), and to VDAs
for ten village range improvement centers totalling US$0.2 million (YRls 0.8
million) (see Annex 9). ACF loans for supervised credit to farmers, land-
pwners and butchers for on-farm investments in livestock and poultry produc-
tion and for butcher tools and equipment would total US$4.3 million (YRls
19.2 million) (see Annex 7) (Annex 6).

5.14      Interest Rates.  Commercial interest rates in YAR range from 11%
for medium and long-term investment credits to 13%-14% for short-term credits.
A preferential rate of 9.5% is charged by YBRD to the General Cotton Company
which on-lends to farmers at no interest. Interest rates charged under the
TDA and SURDEP are 8% for medium and long-term loans and 9% for short-term
loans. Interest rates charged under the Grain Storage and Processing Project
for silo, warehouses and commercial and private bakeries are 9% p.a. Given
an estimated inflation rate of more than 25% in 1974/75, the real interest
rate would be negative with prevailing nominal rates. ACF's on-lending rate
to all subborrowers under the project would be 9% per annum. IDA would under-
take discussions with Government during the Project implementation to seek
adjustment in this rate according to changes in the interest rates for medium
and long-term credits in YAR. The Government's interest rate to ACF would be
4.0% per annum (para 5.09), ACF would earn a spread of 5.0% per annum to cover
its costs, to make provisions for losses and to allow for an average annual
increase of about US$116,000 to its equity capital. If ACF's on-lending rate
were to change, the Government's rate charged to ACF would be adjusted accord-
ingly to maintain a 5.0% spread for ACF. The Government would assume the
foreign exchange risk for all on-lending from external financial sources.
5.15      Loan Security.  According to ACF's present lending procedures,
short-term loans up to YRLs 5,000 are provided against guarantee of two per-
sons. Short-term loans in excess of the above amount and all medium and



- 24 -
long-term loans would require land security. The loans to LDC as a Govern-
ment enterprise and VDA would be secured by a Government guarantee. Loans to
landowners would require land and/or other property security. Land would be
acceptable as security up to 80% and other property up to 60% of the current
market value. Loans to butchers for tools and equipment would be made only to
those properly licensed to operate in a municipality retail market and would
be secured by a joint guarantee of two creditworthy persons. Loans to tenant
farmers would be secured by one of the following alternatives: (a) for short
term loans of up to YRls 5,000 a joint guarantee of two creditworthy persons
or a guarantee by the landowner, (b) for longer term loans, the landowners
guarantee or land security or a joint and several guarantee by a group of ten-
ants or livestock security which would involve some form of permanent animal
identification such as branding, earmarking or ear tagging with the number or
mark registered with ACF. Since this is not a common practice, extension
agents would need to fully explain the purpose of such identification and the
legal implications of having their livestock used as security for loans.
5.16      Supervision and Recovery of Loans.  Loans would be supervised by
ACF staff who would periodically visit LDC and VDA installations. Financial
statements would be obtained annually from the above subborrowers. Loans for
traditional sector would be supervised by ACF Branch office extension officers
equipped with motorcycles and depending on farm size and location would ser-
vice about 40 loans. Emphasis would be on field work with each beneficiary
visited at least once every 30 days during the first year of development. Ex-
tension officers would be directly responsible for on-farm loans supervision
and collection of debt service. They would adopt a cooperative and planning
approach rather than a coercive approach to loan recovery. As part of the
whole enterprise approach to farm extension the agents would have a knowledge
of farm finances and be in a position to assist subborrowers to plan and im-
prove financial management.
5.17      The resources required for financing ACF's loans proposed under the
Project would amount to YRls 18.6 million for the LDC investments, YRls 0.8
million for village pasture development and YRls 19.2 million for supervised
credit to traditional sector. The Government would make these funds avail-
able as two loans to ACF, one for 6 years and one for 20 years, at an interest
rate of 4.0% per annum. In addition, credit would be extended by ACF to LDC
for financing interest during implementation and seasonal credit totalling
YRls 2.0 million. The projected cash flow for ACF operations under the Proj-
ect including the inflow and outflow of project funds for which ACF has only
administrative responsibility is provided in Annex 12 Table 1.
5.18      ACF's Accounts and Audit.  ACF accounting has been handled by CB's
accounting department, with accounts kept separately from CB's own accounts.
As ACF's operations would increase substantially under this and the Grain
Storage and Processing Project, it would be necessary for ACF to build up



- 25 -
and handle its own accounts. An accounting and financial control expert
would be appointed not later than mid-1977 for a period of four years to as-
sist ACF in establishing and operating a modern accounting and financial con-
trol system. Until ACF's accounting system becomes operational CB's account-
ing department would continue to handle ACF's accounts. Under the project,
ACF accounts would also be audited by independent auditors acceptable to IDA.
Audit reports and financial statements would be submitted to IDA not later
than four months after the close of each fiscal year.
5.19      The items discussed in paras 5.08 through 5.18 would be included
in the Subsidiary Loan Agreement between the Government and ACF and would be
satisfactory to IDA. The operation and delivery of the Subsicliary Loan Agree-
ment would be a condition for making the Credit effective.
D. Project Accounts and Audit
5.20      LDC, VDA and MSMD would keep records consistent with sound account-
ing practices and adequate to reflect their operations and financial condi-
tions. PU and the remaining divisions would establish and maintain accounts
for project expenditures. These accounts would be audited anrnually by the
Government's Central Organization for Audit and Control, which is responsible
for auditing public and para public entities. Audit reports and financial
statements would be submitted to IDA not later than four months after close of
each fiscal year. Assurances to this effect were obtained during negotiations.
E. Project Monitoring and Evaluation
5.21      PU would prepare quarterly progress reports, to be sent to IDA with-
in three months of the close of each quarter. Assurances to this effect would
be obtained during negotiations. The PU would be responsible for evaluating
the benefits realized under the project. Information would be collected on
each component which would be consolidated into an annual report reflecting
the increased livestock production, improved income of participating rural
subborrowers, training courses completed, number of livestock treated, and
other benefits arising to the farmers response to the project. The program
for monitoring and evaluation are detailed in Annex 15. Assurances were
obtained to this effect during negotiations.
F. Retroactive Financing
5.22      To start the project's implementation expeditiously and to avoid
delays, Government has initiated action to recruit the management consult-
ant firm prior to the date of the proposed credit. Therefore, retroactive fi-
nancing for such expenditures is recommended up to an amount of US$150,000.



- 26 -
G. Risk
5.23      Since this is the first integrated livestock development project in
YAR and because of the small and underdeveloped base, on which it is initiated
it involves a higher than normal degree of risk and uncertainty. Project
risks stem from the country's low level of development, particularly institu-
tional weakness's, shortage of trained manpower, lack of experience in project
implementation and operation, widespread disease problems, and the almost com-
plete absence of people familiar with modern animal production techniques.
These risks have been fully considered in the design of the project. As des-
cribed in paras 4.15 and 5.07 a comprehensive technical assistance and train-
ing program combined with a six year implementation period and careful inter-
related phasing (see Annex 10, table 1, and Annex II, table 1), would adequa-
tely provide for institution building and strengthening. Furthermore, before
a fully Yemeni managed operation comes into effect, initial expatriate manage-
ment and training of local counterpart staff would be followed by a period of
local management assisted by expert advisers. Whilst the Project includes an
unusually high number of components the integration of these would serve to
minimize production risks. Disease control, animal production, extension,
credit, marketing, research, and agroindustry development, present an inte-
grated package of mutually beneficial components. Apart from organization
and management complexities, production risks arise from the uncertain avail-
ability of suitable and adequate irrigation water for the LDC farm sites, and
disease control. The proposed consultant studies would minimize the farm
site problems whilst the comprehensive Animal Health Control component (see
Annex 8) would reduce disease risks to an acceptable level. In addition, the
integration of the efforts of several concurrent bilateral livestock develop-
ment programs will increase the prospects for overall progress.
5.24      Because of prevalent animal diseases, the need for balanced feed and
husbandry and management factors, poultry, and to a lesser extent dairying,
can be regarded as high risk areas. Project design incorporates a scale of
operation which combined with components such as feed supply, disease control,
and supervised credit, not only reduces risk to an acceptable level, but
spreads it over a relatively large number of beneficiaries. On-farm benefi-
ciary risks are minimized by provision for an overall integrated development
package involving irrigation, improved seeds, fertilizer, pest control, super-
vised credit, disease control and product marketing (see Annex 5, para 15).
Furthermore, the traditional sector dairy development will build on the high-
ly developed skill which Yemeni farmers display in the care of their draft
animals. In addition climatic conditions in the highland areas are well
suited to the introduction of improved breeds of both poultry and cattle.
The present demand for livestock products in YAR is very strong and is ex-
pected to increase substantially. The needs of and the skills of the rural
poor are substantial. In considering the importance of agriculture and
livestock in the economy, the risks involved in the project are worth taking.



- 27 -
VI. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
A.  Incremental Production and Markets
6.01      As YAR's first integrated livestock development program, the Proj-
ect is expected to result in substantial and diverse benefits to the Yemen
economy. The initiation of programs in dairying, poultry production, and
livestock finishing from a small or non-existent base are bound to have a
significant effect on the future of the livestock sector. Similarly, the
initiation of hygenic livestock slaughtering and meat marketing would mark a
profound change from existing practices. Expansion of the embryonic animal
health service is fundamental to the future of the livestock sector, most
notably to protect draft animals which provide the motive power for Yemen
agriculture. It is expected that the forestry-range component--a new program
for the Yemen--would have beneficial long-term effects and be capable of great
expansion in the future.
6.02      Incremental annual production at full development resulting from
the Project is projected as follows:
Milk           4,725 tons, plus 21,300 tons per year of increased
production attributable to the animal health com-
ponent of the Project in Year 9.
Meat           650 tons, plus 6,200 tons per year of increased
production attributable to the animal health com-
ponent of the Project in Year 9.
Grain          1,300 tons of grain.
Poultry        3,000 tons of poultry feed
Feed
Egg            180 tons of eggs per year plus about 820 tons per
year of increased production attributable to the
animal health component in Project Year 9.

cremental output would substitute for food imports. Based on projections in

C. Economic Benefits
6.05      The Project has a calculated overall economic rate of return of 27%.
Additional, non-quantifiable, benefits would accrue to the YAR from this na-
tional livestock development program including:
(a) Improvement to public health through upgraded conditions of
slaughtering and vending meat.
(b) The training of a substantial number of Yemenis in diverse
skills, including management and administrative, animal hus-
bandry, and veterinary technology.
(c) The introduction of new enterprises and methods on a pilot
basis which can be expected to provide a framework for future
expansion. These include dairy farming, livestock finishing,
smallholder commercial poultry, and timber management.
(d) Building institutions which will contribute to the country's
progress beyond the present Project.



- 29 -
6.06      Institutional Building.  The Project will substantially strengthen
the Agricultural Credit Fund, will help build the Animal Health Division, and
will create the Livestock Development Corporation, as well as the Livestock
and Range Improvement Division. This institution building, will have favor-
able, but non-quantifiable effects for the future of the Yemen economy in in-
creased capacity to implement expanded activities in livestock development
and related activities.
6.07      Employment and training benefits from the project include 458 jobs
for Yemeni nationals together with extensive training in diverse skills.
Considerable import substitution effects would be realized. By Project year
5, milk production attributable to the project, would be equivalent to US$7
million per year. Similarly, the value of meat produced attributable to the
Project by year 5 would be equivalent to US$8 million per year. In addition,
export earnings from improved hides and skins are projected to increase by
US$0.22 million by Project year 5.
6.08      Economic Rates of Return by Component.  The overall rate of return
to the Project is 27%.  The animal health program has a very high return, as
might be expected from the initiation of effective measures where little has
been done in the past. The public health effects of the new abattoirs and
food markets have not been possible to quantify and setting their entire
costs against the quantifiable benefits arising from improved hides and skins
income results in a very low economic return. However, the slaughterhouse
and retail markets component is financially viable, as shown in Annex 6. The
Animal health program and the public health aspects of the new abattoirs and
meat markets are directly related, and in the same way, the animal health pro-
gram underpins all aspects of the Project. Sensitivity analysis was performed
for the whole project, and by component, as shown in Table 1, Annex 14. The
rate of return does not fall below 22% for the project.

ANNEX 1
