APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
The Agricultural Sector
The Resource Base and Nature of Farming
1.        Agriculture represents the major sector within the Yemen Arab
Republic economy, accounting for approximately 66 percent to the country's
GDP and employing 80-90 percent of the resident Yemeni population (See
Table 1). Although the agricultural sector is predominantly subsistence-
based, it provides over 90 percent of national export earnings from such com-
modities as cotton, coffee, cotton seeds, hides and skins.
2.        The land area in Yemen totals approximately 20 million ha, of which
only about 2 million ha are classified cultivable. At the present time 1.5
million ha are actually cultivated with 2.0 million ha kept fallow to be
brought into production given sufficient rainfall. Most of the cropped area
is rainfed with only 230,000 ha being under some form of supplementary irriga-
tion system. In addition, there are 14 million ha of rocky, desertic, and
rangeland areas, 1.6 million ha of bush cover, and about 2.6 million ha of
wasteland (See Tables 2 and 3).
3.        Climatic conditions vary widely, and micro-climatic differences are
locally important because of variable mountainous topography. Patterns of
rainfall and temperature are to a large extent determined by the relief.
Rainfall is erratic, and annual variations profoundly influence crop produc-
tion by affecting not only per hectare yields but also utilization of rainfed
as well as marginal cropland. Normally the major rains occur in July, August,
and September with minor rains in March, April and May. Reportedly 1/ a forty-
year climatic cycle is in existence, containing 20 years of high rainfall fol-
lowed by 20 years of relatively sparse precipitation. The 1974-75 season has
been pegged as the start of the former. Surface water resources are not
abundant, and the extent of underground supplies remains an unknown factor.
Preliminary results from test borings have pointed to the possibility of
significant underground water resources in the Tihama plain as well as in the
Wadi areas of the Highland Plains. A decline in the water table has been
observed within the last several years with replenishment of aquifers possible
only with considerable levels of precipitation. Winds likewise are an impor-
tant constraint on agricultural production, particularly in the Tihama and
the Intermontane Plain Regions. Soil characteristics vary widely, from ex-
cellent to poor, with salinity and alkalinity occurring in certain areas.
1/   Citations are made of data collected by an Italian aeronautics con-
sultant team which noted annual rainfall in Sana'a of 300 nmm in 1973
versus 600 mm in 1953.



ANNEX I
Page 2
4.        The principal cereal crops are sorghum, millet, barley, and wheat
with approximately 80-85 percent of crop acreage allocated to these produc-
tion activities. Wide variations in climate, topography, and soils howe-er
permit the cultivation of a considerable range of crops including coffee, to-
bacco, cotton, pulses, grapes, deciduous fruits, citrus, bananas, oilseeds,
potatoes, maize, alfalfa and qat (Cata edulis) (See Table 4). Livestock
assumes an important role in Yemeni agriculture by being the main source of
farm work power, contributing to foreign exchange earnings through exports of
hides and skins, and providing meat and milk for domestic consumption. For-
estry in the accepted sense of the word does not exist; however, moderately
large areas are covered by woody vegetation (shrubs) providing fuel and minor
construction materials, and accomplishing some soil and water conservation.
Deforestation over the last several decades has beco}e a serious problem
arising because of large consumptive demand for home cooking requirements,
especially of family bread, with no compensatory program to replace dyminish-
ing stands.
5.        Yemeni farmers are considered industrious, and the elaborate ter-
racing in the Highland and Midland regions remain as substantiation of this
observation. Participation by women and children in agricultural activities
is considerable especially in weeding, harvesting, threshing, and herding
activities and, they even were observed in plowing cooperations. In many
cases women are left to tend to agricultural matters during the lengthy
periods of absence of spouses engaged in non-agricultural employment in YAR
urban centers or in other nearby Middle Eastern States. Family labor remains
the main source of manpower in farm operations to be supplemented by hired
workers usually only in peak harvesting periods.
6.        Farming systems vary regionally according to climate, soil charac-
teristics, and water availability. The level of technology adopted remains
essentially traditional in nature, with utilization of modern production in-
puts such as chemical fertilizers,improved varieties, and pesticides at
present negligible, utilization of these modern inputs in the main circum-
scribed to those areas having guaranteed water supplies either through high
annual rainfall or supplementary irrigation systems. Soil nutrient reple-
nishment and supplementation remains in most cases limited to manure applica-
tion which in part accounts for the maintenance of relatively high fertility
levels in terraced areas over many centuries of continuous cultivation.
Research and extension services are presently minimal except for the limited
efforts financed with foreign assistance.
Agro-Ecological Zones
7.        The country can be divided into five major agricultural areas ac-
cording to their resource bases, agricultural outputs, and developmment po-
tentialities. These are:
(a) the Tihama,
(b) the Western slopes,
(c) the Midlands,
(d) the Highlands, and
(e) the Eastern slopes.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
8.        The Tihama (total land area--20,000 km2) consists of two arid and
semi-arid belts, the coastal plain and the foothills, and exterLds from the
southern border to the Saudi Arabian border in the north and approximately
30-60 km inland from the Red Sea. The main towns are Hodeidah (the princi-
pal harbor), Zabid, Beit-el-Fakeeh, Mokha, Al-Marawi'ah and Salif. The
climate is hot and humid with annual rainfall varying between 50 mm and 300
mm. Rainfed and irrigated farming are practiced in this zone. Sorghum,
millet, sesame, maize, and dates are produced almost entirely for subsis-
tence, and cotton, tobacco, vegetables and fruits as cash crops. The total
cultivated area, both rainfed and irrigated, varies from year t:o year, de-
pending on rainfall and surface water availability. Rough estimates have been
made that currently up to 600,000 ha could be planted under marginally rainfed
conditions, 70,000 ha under spate irrigation, and 25,000 ha under supplemental
irrigation from ground and perennial water sources. Livestock production is
important especially for the large portion of the Tihama population engaged
in such activities. Eight major wadies (intermittent streams) cross the Tihama.
In these areas there is a significant potentiality for surface and groundwater
development where soils are favorable. The Tihama thus remains the major area
for further land development in Yemen, with 200,000 ha currently estimated as
potentially irrigable with further development of underground wiater resources.
Livestock production in Tihama is based mainly upon crop residues which are cut,
removed from the field and hand-fed to animals rather than grazed. Although
prolonged overgrazing and shifting agriculture have largely destroyed the
natural grassland resources, the zone carries between 50 and 60 percent of the
cattle and about 35 percent of the nation's sheep and goat hercl, and it offers
the best livestock development potential in the country.
9.        The Western Slopes (total land area -- 35,000 km2) includes the area
between the Tihama and the Midlands and Highlands. Although population esti-
mates remain less than reliable, the upper reaches are known to be densely
populated. Climatic conditions, primarily rainfall and temperature, vary with
the altitude. All the cultivable land on the slopes is terraced, and irriga-
tion occurs in gorges along the wadi banks. The main crops are sorghum, mil-
let, coffee, alfalfa, bananas and papayas. The livestock popu].ation is not
known, but there is considerable evidence of overgrazing. There is virtually
no scope for expansion of agricultural land in this region; however production
might be boosted by utilization of pesticides, improved crop varieties, as
well as increased labor-management input in land preparation, seeding, weeding,
and optional crop rotation planning.
10.       The Midlands (total land area -- 20,000 km2) extends practically
from the southern border to the town of Qadah and covers the Taiz Governorate
and adjacent areas. It is the second most populous area of the country (about
900,000 people), and also the second largest in agricultural output. Alti-
tudes vary from 800 m to 2,000 m above sea level, with annual rainfall ranging
between 450 mm and 500 mm. Climate, soils, and water resources are suitable
for produciton of cereals, pulses, potatoes, fruits, vegetables, fodder and
coffee but as in the Western Slopes zone, there exists minimal potentiality
in the Midlands to stimulate higher agricultural production through further cul-
tivable land extensity. There does exist, however, some scope for reforesta-
tion, a project presently being investigated by UNDP.



ANNEX 1
Page 4
11.       The Highlands (total land area -- 35,000 km2) can be divided ecolo-
gically into two sub-areas, the Central Highlands and the Intermontane Plains.
The differentiation between these two areas is primarily with reference to
climate and topography, and therefore agricultural production. Animal produc-
tion is based on crop residues and the poor quality and largely depleted
natural pastures of the elevated land adjoining cropping areas. Despite the
harsh conditions, the farmers maintain considerable numbers of livestock, and
the zone is estimated to carry 350,000 cattle and 6,400,000 sheep (39% and 53%
of the national herd and flock respectively). Development potential lies in
intensive small stock and dairy production, and local forestry/grazing schemes
could have a marked effect on range carrying capacities.
(a) The Central Highlands comprise most of the Ibb Governorate,
with a population of approximately 800,000. Altitudes vary
from 1,500 m to 2,000 m. Annual rainfall is within the 600-
800 mm range, and soils are considered relatively fertile.
Supplementary water resources are at present mostly restricted
to limited groundwater and springs; there are, however, a few
wadis where surface irrigation is practiced on a small-scale.
Most of the cultivable area is terraced. Main crops in the
region are sorghum, wheat and barley, and of lesser coverage,
millet, alfalfa, maize, potatoes, pulses, and some fruits and
vegetables.
(b) The Intermontane Plains extend from Yerim to the northern
border and encompass the governorates of Radaa, Sana'a and
Saada. The urban population is concentrated in Yerim, Damar,
Sana' a and Saada; the rural areas are relatively sparsely
populated. This region is characterized by a series of
flat plains surrounded by mountains, or closed watershed
basins. Altitudes vary from 2,000 to 3,000 m, and rainfall,
averaging 200 mm to 500 mm, is relatively erratic. Tempe-
ratures and humidity are usually relatively low. Ground-
water resources are present but costly to tap thereby
restricting the scope for extensive small-scale irrigation
from wells and springs. Most of the soils are considered
marginal or poor, and the area is devoid of much natural vege-
tation. The major crops grown are sorghum, wheat, barley,
millet, qat, grapes, fruits and vegetables. Livestock acti-
vities, mainly sheep and goats, are an important source of
income.
12.       The Eastern Slopes (total land area -- 80,000 km2) is the largest
zone in size but poorest in agricultural resources. The slopes cover the
areas between the Intermontane Plains and the Saudi Arabian border to the
east. Climatic conditions are not favorable for agricultural production
since annual temperatures are high and rainfall is only about 100 mm to 200
mm. Soils generally are poor, however, there are a few valleys where irrigated
agriculture is practiced predominantly on a subsistence basis. Despite
existing remnants of thousand-year-old irrigation schemes, the possibilities
for significant agricultural development in this region seem extremely limited.
Because of the limited vegetation, crop production, and related rainfall in
the area, animal populations remain relatively limited.



ANNEX 1
Page 5
Land Tenure System
13.       The land tenure system in YAR remains very tradition--based and com-
plex. The great proportion of total cultivated land area remains under the
control of large individual owners who generally lease part of their land
out under sharecropping arrangements. The State and religious institutions
directly own 2-3 and 15% of the cultivable land respectively which also
are usually farmed by tenants. Procedures for cost and income division be-
tween landowners and tenants differ widely and appear a function of the par-
ticular crop cultivated as well as the availability and method of supple-
mentary irrigation. In general, the tenant receives between 1/4 - 1/3 of
production from irrigated land, and 1/3 - 1/2 of that from rainfed hectarage.
Although holdings range between 0.25 and 10.0 hectares, size seems to vary
with region and degree of non-rainfed culture; in areas where cultivation
remains rainfed-based and extensive in nature such as in the Tihama, Highlands,
and on the Eastern Slopes, holdings generally fall in the upper portion of
the range. If cultivation of owned hectarage remains beyond the managerial
and operational scope of available family labor, the excessive land is
usually leased under sharecropping arrangements. Even though most annual
tenure contracts are verbally established, officials report few cases of
termination of these pacts.
Taxation of Agricultural Production
14.       Agricultural production is subject to a 10% tax (zakat) based on
the gross value of output. Livestock activities are generally assessed ac-
cording to number of animals owned, and crop production on well-irrigated
land is taxed at a reduced rate of 5%. Although the zakat might be col-
lected in either kind or cash, an increasing quantity has been obtained
in the latter form over the last five years, reflecting a preference of
local development boards to obtain financial revenue in cash form. Farmers'
attitudes toward cash vs. kind payment appear mixed. Those wit:h limited
personally owned storage facilities and/or who are unsure aboul: prices
for any marketable surpluses seem to prefer kind payment. Those having dif-
ficult access to Ministry of Finance zakat depository points and/or who might
need personal family stock replenishment, opt for cash payment. The effective-
ness of zakat collection is a function of the dependability and administrative
efficiency of local and regional Ministry of Finance representatives charged
with its collection and management. At the village level the :Local amin, a
government representative and resident of the community fully familiar with
each farmer's productive performance, each year evaluates the respective farm-
er's livestock and/or crop, assesses the amount of tax, and oversees movement
of kind portion to designated depositories and collection of a:Lternative tax
in cash. Concerning the latter, a committee established by the local governor
determines the unit price at which the commodity is valued for tax purposes.
15.       Livestock activities are taxed proportionately to the number of
animals owned by each respective farmer. There is no special statutory provi-
sions for taxation of poultry, milk, or wool, and therefore these items are
assessed according to the general 10% rate. However, animals are taxed under
livestock-specific zakat assessment in the following manner:



ANNEX 1
Page 6
(a) Camels. One sheep (ewe) for every five camels up to 25.
For larger herds, the tax is increased proportionately,
and for a herd of 120, two three-year-old animals and
one ewe sheep are payable.
(b) Sheep and goats. For flocks of less than 40 no tax is
payable. Thereafter the rate is one sheep or goat for
flocks of up to 120 and two for flocks of 120 to 200,
with an increase of one ewe for each additional hundred
head.
(c) Cattle. No tax is payable on herds of less than 30. For
30 to 40 animals the tax is one yearling; thereafter it is
one two-year-old for every 40 animals in the herd.
Agricultural Credit
16.       A general agricultural institution has started only recently in
Yemen. Early attempts to establish such an institutional network failed
principally from lack of capital and trained manpower. Agricultural credit
from institutional sources has in previous years been limited to short-term
funding for cotton production by the General Cotton Corporation (GCC). The
GCC, financed by the Yemen Bank for Reconstruction and Development, provides
interest-free loans to cotton producers for seed, fertilizer, and tool acquisi-
tion against the security of their cotton crops.
17.       The Government authorized the creation of a small credit unit, The
Agricultural Credit Fund, under a subsidiary arrangement with the Central
Bank. The ACF, presently maintaining offices in Sana'a, Wadi Mawr and Wadi
Zabid, acts as the sole dispensing agent for the credit component of the
Tihama Development Project scheduled to cover investments in machinery and
tubewell units, short-term production loans as well as credit to agribusi-
ness firms for the installation of processing and marketing facilities.
Similarly, ACF has been chosen the credit channel for the Southern Uplands
Rural Development Project providing short-term loans for material inputs
(mostly fertilizers), and medium- and long-term loans for investment in
on-farm development, and on a limited scale, for creation of nurseries and
orchards, coffee culture expansion, and construciton of packaging and handling
facilities. ACF, presently in a formative phase, has dispersed a few short-
term loans mainly for the purchase of oil for irrigation pumps and some
medium-term loans for on-farm well sinking and tractors acquisition, all
totalling YRls 410,380 as of October, 1975.
18.       The Parliament has recently passed a law establishing a wholly
stateowned Yemen Agricultural Credit Bank (ACB). Authorized capital of ACB is
YRls 100 million, of which YRls 20 million were subscribed early in 1976.
ACB is expected to start its lending operations from the second half of 1976.
The establishment of the Bank would not, however, restrict the operation of



ANNEX 1
Page 7
ACF which, according to a cabinet decision, would continue to be the lending
agency for IDA projects. If the ACB is able to start up effective lending
operations, consideration to merge ACF with ACB would be made in consultation
with IDA toward the long-term goal of maintaining an effective, nationwide
agricultural credit institution.
19.       The extent to which the inflow of remittances from family members
employed in urban centers or abroad acts as a dampening agent on the demand
for agricultural credit remains an unknown and unexamined factor. However,
at present those farmers requiring credit must generally rely on private
sources for funding. Since most farmers are relatively poor, it is quite
probable that many depend on credit from relatives, neighbors, merchants,
or landowners. Although the terms of loans dispensed by private parties
remain essentially unknown, interest rates are though to vary considerably
according to the source of the credit and to be mitigated to some extent
by religio-cultural stigma attached to usury.
Development Planning
20.       To date no long-run development strategy has been formulated for
Yemen. In 1973/74 a first attempt at development planning was made with
the release of a three-year program that stressed (1) increasing national
income at an annual rate of at least 6%, (2) a substantial rise in the living
standard of the population, and (3) expansion of social services to all areas
of the country. The emphasis given to agricultural developmen,t is reflected
by the allocation to the agricultural sector of YRls 138.1 million, 15% of
the total YRls 935.6 million budgeted for the three-year program. As original-
ly proposed, 19% was to be financed from Government sources, 6% from local
private savings, and 75% from foreign aid.
21.       The following were cited as basic aims in the YAR agricultural
development strategy:
(a) A net increase in real agricultural income of not less than
6% in order to achieve a resource surplus subsequently to
finance development activities in other sectors;
(b) Achievement of self-sufficiency in the basic foodstuffs with
particular emphasis given to cereal crops, oils, vegetables
and livestock production;
(c) Rectification of the trade balance through the increase and
improvement of products for export especially cotton., coffee,
hides and skins, fresh and canned fish, vegetables and fresh
fruits;
(d) Improvement of the dietary standard of the population;
(e) Provision of credit and supporting extension, marketing, and
input distributional infrastructure for agricultural producers,
especially the smaller farmers.



ANNEX 1
Page 8
22.       While the Government recognizes the importance of the agricultural
sector in the economy, it has not been able to generate a clearly established
implementational scheme to translate aims into well-defined action programs and
detailed investment projects. Much of these difficulties seem to stem from:
(a) the lack of systematic long-term programming and the Govern-
ment's just recent introduction to short- and medium-term goal
determination;
(b) the lack of infrastructural instruments through which effective
proposals could be transformed into action;
(c) weak governmental institutions to a large extent dependent upon
foreign technical assistance and capital; and perhaps most
importantly,
(d) a resource base placing serious constraints upon growth pos-
sibilities.
23.       During this formative period the Government appears to be em-
phasizing:
(a) development of the Tihama as a long-term grain and cash crop
production center;
(b) the Midlands and Highlands development as a short-term cereal
expansion program;
(c) establishment of national agricultural research, extension and
credit institutions to stimulate crop production for domestic
as well as export markets; and
(d) increased focus on livestock production activities such as
poultry, dairy, sheep, and beef feedlot enterprises.
Prospects for Future Grain Production
24.       Because of the limited availabilities of grazing resources and
seasonality of precipitation in YAR, farmers engaged in livestock activities
are forced to rely heavily on cereal crop byproducts such as leaves and
stalks for fodder purposes. Often farmers strip the leaves from sorghum
plants prior to the harvest thereby resulting in an unmeasured, but noticable
decline in grain yields. Because of the heavy utilization of such materials
in livestock production, future expansion of livestock activities is there-
fore dependent upon the prospects for cereal production in the years to come.
25.       Utilization of marginal land, and therefore extent of rainfed
cereal cultures, are strickly a function of rainfaill. Pre-planting precipi-
tion levels determined the amount of area sown in a given year, and extent
and distribution of rain during the growing season affect the eventual yields
as well as quality of byproducts allocated to livestock. The great bulk of
cultivable land in YAR remains rainfed in nature, and therefore the associated



ANNEX 1
Page 9
risks of grain production on such area are not without their spillover effects
into livestock production. The Tihama region, presently supporting 50-60% of
the country's cattle population and approximately 35% of the sheep and goat
herd, is viewed as a major region for potential livestock development. How-
ever, at the same time because of relatively sparse rainfall in this zone,
area devoted to cereal cultures, predominantly drought - resistant sorghum
and pearl millet, is extremely sensitive to variability in precipitation.
Reflecting the sensitivity in the Tihama, sorghum-millet areas was reduced
over the&1970/71-1973/74 period by 28.5% to be increased the following season
from .88 to 1.95 million ha. Even in the relatively productive, high rainfall
areas of Taiz and Ibb, few opportunities of hectarage expansion exist for
cultivation of cereals and other crops as witnessed by the intricate system
of terracing covering all usable space.
26.       Grain crops are considered by the Yemeni farmer necessary for sub-
sistence -- to satisfy his family's consumptive needs throughout the year, to
insure adequate fodder for livestock, and through on-farm storage to provide
some insurance during poor rainfall seasons. It is therefore plausible that
if innovations in cereal technology do permit considerable boosts in per ha.
yields, the farmer could derive his subsistence from a smaller area than prev-
iously and thereupon substitute cash crops for these low-income cereal activi-
ties. High demand for livestock products will no doubt make production of
fodder crops a logical candidate for released land. However (1) the relative
profitability of livestock production alternatives versus cash crops such as
vegetables, fruits oilseeds and qat (in highland areas) and (2) the effect of
movement out of cereal production on national livestock production remain
unclear and unstudied.
27.       Farmers in several highland wadi areas north of Sana'a (Dhar, Al
Gara'a, and Dala'a) have already made the transition to specializaiton in qat
production and rely on market purchases for domestic cereal needs. In the
Ibb-Taiz region, terraced land having access to supplementary irrigation was
deemed suitable for cash crop alternatives such as qat, fruit, grapes, onions,
potatoes, and other vegetables; non-irrigated areas could be planted with
coffee or qat in lieu of cereals. Qat, in addition to its prof'itability, is
adaptable to a wide range of highland climatic environments--coldness, dry-
ness, or wetness--and can be harvested year-round starting one year after
planting. However, in many cases where farmers had access to well irriga-
tion facilities, alfalfa is planted not only to provide fodder for owned
animals but also to act as an important year round cash generator. Farmers
questioned expressed interest in allocating land to crop alternatives such as
vegetables and qat for family requirements and market sales if at some future
date family cereal needs could be met from reduced planting wit:h better varie-
ties and improved management.
28.       Because of high costs of well irrigation, farmers will probably be
forced to make crop substitutions. An economic analysis of the various crop
activity alternatives for the respective spate, well, and mixed irrigation
areas in Wadi Zabid confirm this assertion. Although total hectarage under the
irrigation scheme would increase by 14.34 percent from 22,800 t:o 26,070 ha
through the rehabilitation of the diversion structures, further expansion of



ANNEX 1
Page 10
spate irrigation area, and installation of additional well facilities, total
area devoted to cereals would decline by 11.12 percent from 17,400 ha under
present allocation to 15,465 ha even though maize, deemed profitable on well
and mixed (spate and well) irrigated land, would increase from 1,500 to 3,650
ha. Because of high construction and operating costs of well facilities as well
as increased utilization of certified seed, fertilizers, and insecticides,
production of millets and sorghum, displaying low potential profitability,
would be relegated to spate irrigated and rainfed land, while maize, vegetables,
and oilseeds (sesame, sunflower, and groundnut) would take precedence on irri-
gated land. The following is a summary of the budgeted averages for each crop
alternative:
Production Costs and
Taxes/hectare (YRls)       Cotton  Sorghum  Millet  Maize  Oilseed  Vegetables
Present                   831.7   543.1    504.8   725.7   465.9    2,499.4

29.       Given its importance in foreign exchange generation through export
earnings, the Government might in the future deem it advantageous to support
the cotton price to stimulate production thereby increasing competition with
cereals for available land especially in spate-irrigated areas potentially
altering the relative profitability of livestock-related production activi-
ties, e.g. alfalfa cultivation. Additionally, adoption of better performing
varieties of cereals, an understandable goal in a grain-based economy with
limited scope of cereal growth through extensive measures, might prove com-
petitive, rather than complementary with livestock production. Newer varie-
ties of sorghum, millet, maize and other cereals being introduced from
external sources and developed internally through crosses of exotic and
indigenous strain often display a reduced stalk-grain and leaf-grain ratios
than observed in more traditional varieties thereby reducing the potential
fodder offtake per plant. Although land might be released for livestock--
supportive crop cultures (as well as for other cash crops), with the adoption
of high yielding varieties now proposed, the production of cereal--based
fodder would decline even more rapidly. However at the same time, limited
fodder from new varieties and the importance of livestock in the small farm
enterprise could limit demand for the shorter strains. The micro as well as
macro implications require additional study.

Livestock Production, Consumption and Trade
Domestic Livestock Production
1.        The estimated livestock population of twelve million sheep and
goats, 900,000 cattle, 600,000 donkeys 100,000 camels is concentrated on the
small farms of the cultivated areas where yet forms an integral part of the
farming system. There is also a fluctuating national poultry population
of upwards of three million village chickens. The importance of the national
herd lies not only in its value as a food supply but also in the fact that
crop production depends almost exclusively on animal traction for cultivation.
In the absence of a net of agricultural feeder roads, camels and donkeys pro-
vided an effective form of transport in the rural areas. The majority of
cattle are located in the Tihama, whereas sheep and goats are more evenly
distributed. Regional stock numbers 1/ are estimated as follows:
Cattle         Sheep/Goats
Tihama                              500,000          4,000,000
Southern Uplands                    200,000          2,400,000
Central Highlands and
Intermontane Plains                150,000         4,000,000
Eastern Slopes                       50,000          1,600,000
Total                               900,000         12,0002000
2.        Because of its key role in agriculture, livestock contributes signi-
ficantly to the resources of the rural population, which in 1973 produced 66%
of domestic income. In the fiscal year, 1972/73, livestock-related products,
principally hides and skins, accounted for 19% of total exports. With simple
improvements in flaying techniques, handling and grading, the value of hides
and skins could be considerably increased.
1/   Since no livestock census has been taken in YAR, National livestock herd
and flock numbers estimate is based on five potential sources, town
slaughter figures, sales of hides, vaccination figures, survey informa-
tion and tax collection records, which give widely divergent estimates.
Source Project Preparation Report, C. M. Chisholm & Associates.



ANNEX 2
Page 2
3.        Cattle.  The indigenous cattle of the country are meclium-sized,
thoracic humped zebu with docile temperament and excellent working ability.
They are well adapted to cope with the high temperatures and humidities found
in the Tihama and mountain zones and the common practice of selecting a
superior bull annually for use with all cows in one or more villages maintains
this adaptability. Almost all cattle belong to settled farmers (nomadism is
restricted to a small number of bedouins in the east), although seasonal
movement to market or in search of additional grazing do occur. For the
small terraced holdings, cattle provide the only feasible traction of culti-
vation; the farmer is thus careful with his stock, and practicularly with
his work bulls and oxen.
4.        Cows are kept to provide milk, which is consumed on the farm or may
be made into cheese and sold at local markets. Age at first calving is as
old as three years; calving intervals are long and average millc production
estimated at less than two litres a day. Because of limitations in the feed
supply, mortality in young animals is high and those that surv:Lve are fre-
quently sold immature and in unfinished condition. These, together with
aged work bulls or oxen, comprise the majority of the herd offtake, which
is estimated at 9% of total numbers.
5.        The main source of feed is sorghum or millet crop residues low in
both protein and energy; they are hand-fed with lucerne of other green crops.
This is supplemented, where possible, with grazing of natural pastures,
browse species or standing crop residues. During droughts, trees such as
Ziziphus spina-christi (carrob) and Acacia spp. are pollared and the branches
fed to stock.
6.        Inadequate control of animal diseases is a severe conistraint on the
development of the livestock subsector. As recently as 1972, rinderpest
caused large losses of cattle, particularly in the Tihama. Foot and mouth
disease, straptothricosis and other skin diseases, malnutrition and repro-
ductive disease, ticks and tick-borne diseases and internal parasites are
widespread and cause large economic losses from lowered productivity as well
as from death. In 1972, under the terms of a bilateral aid agreement with UK
technical assistance (ODM), a small veterinary field team was established
to investigate animal disease in YAR, to operate a diagnostic service in
Sana'a, to conduct vaccination and curative treatment campaignis in most of
the important livestock production areas of the country.
7.        Sheep.  There is a wide regional variety of sheep breeds with
sharply differing physical and color characteristics. In the Tihama, a
major producing area, most sheep are of one type: small to medium in size,
hornless, with rudimentary ears and little wool. In the northeast, they
show similarities to the Saudi Arabian Naji and Awasssi and, in fact, con-
siderable movement of sheep takes place between the two countries. With
such a widely-based genetic pool, sheep constitute a highly va:Luable re-
source with the potential to show increases in productivity in response to
selective breeding programs.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
8.        As in the case with cattle, most sheep are owned by settled farmers.
Often grazed in mixed flocks with goats, they utilize barren or rocky hill-
sides not accessible to cattle, or stubble fields of poor quality. Farmers
prefer to graze their animals on a communal or village basis, and few own
large flocks. Zakat records indicate that fewer than 15% of sheep and goats
are owned in flocks of more than 40.
9.        Mating is uncontrolled and takes place throughout the year.  Given
good seasonal conditions, ewes may lamb twice a year. Twining is common.
With nutritional and veterinary assistance, therefore, the potential repro-
ductive rate of the flock is high. Ewes' milk is manufactured into a fer-
mented and sweetened product for use with the chewing of qat; lamb growth
suffers accordingly. Average daily yield is less than 1/4 litre.
10.       Annual offtake is estimated at 35% of flock numbers less than 5% of
which is channelled through the abattoirs in the four main towns of Taiz,
Hodeida, Ibb and Sana'a. The balance is accounted for by village slaughter-
ings or slaughterings in homes during the religious festivals. Farmers do
not retain sheep to an old age; most slaughterings are of lambs or hoggets.
11.       Of the diseases of economic importance which affect sheep, pox is
the most widespread and severe. Foot and mouth disease, sheep scab (particu-
larly in the east), sheep mange and various other skin conditions caused by
mites, lice and keds abound. Liverfluke causes serious productivity losses
in poorly drained regions of the highlands; blood and intestinal parasites
are also common.
12.       Goats.  Usually grazed with sheep, but efficiently utilizing refuse
and rough forage on which sheep and cattle will not thrive, goats are the
most productive of all livestock. They, too, exhibit variety in color, con-
formation and hair characteristics. Production parameters are similar to
sheep although kidding rates are slightly higher. Diseases affect goats as
for sheep, with the addition of contagious pleuropneumonia, which is wide-
spread and causes serious losses.
13.       Camels and Donkeys.  Even though the road system of YAR has been
developed greatly in recent years, these species continue to be used for
transporting goods. Many parts of the country are inaccessible to motor
transport but can be reached by camel or donkey, which are also cheap to
purchase and maintain. In addition, they are used for drawing water from
wells, and in parts of the Tihama, they replace cattle for pulling culti-
vating implements. Cattle and sheep receive priority in feeding; camels
and particularly donkeys receive barely sufficient feed to maintain them-
selves and carry out their work. Camels are occasionally slaughtered for
consumption, but there is no recorded market for donkey meat. Little is
known of the incidence of diseases affecting camels and donkeys, but
Trypanosoma evansi and camel pox have been identified. Infections and ulcers
caused by ill-fitting saddles or harneses are common.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
14.       Poultry.  The local poultry are small, rarely exceeding 1 kilogram
liveweight, and of low productivity and potential. They are distributed
thr. aghout the country in flocks averaging less than 10 birds, although large
areas are periodically depopulated of poultry by Newcastle disease. Grain
feeding is rarely practiced; birds scavenge for food around the villages.
Poultry meat fetches a price comparable with other meats and farners normally
produce birds and eggs for cash rather than for home consumption. Traders
collect eggs in the rural areas for sale in the large towns, but delays in
transport lead to a large percentage of spoilages. Chickens are sold at local
markets, exhibiting a degree of price uniformity throughout the country.
15.       Livestock Marketing.  Farmers in the Yemen are mainly subsistence
grain farmers. Of the livestock they own, first in importance are plow oxen,
second are milk cows. Typically, a family will own two oxen and one milk cow.
In addition a farmer may own sheep or goats, which are often put with neigh-
bor's stock to graze commmon lands. He may also own a small flock of chickens.
Livestock represent work power, a source of family food, a reservoir of cash
capital, and the means to obtain needed items through barter or cash transac-
tions. The output from these animals consists of culled cattle, sheep and
goats as needed, sour milk and butterfat, a few eggs, and some chickens. The
farmer will market this livestock, dairy, and poultry output in of three ways:
(a) by barter for needed items which are locally produced;
(b) by lending to neighbors, who will reciprocate at a later
date; and
(c) by selling for cash.
In general, he will retain for his family's own consumption only sour milk,
and an occasional goat, sheep, or chicken-meat is often reserved for feast
days. Butterfat, eggs, and meat have to be sold to get needed cash. Such
transactions take place either in the village, or at a nearby local market.
16.       Livestock marketing in YAR is poorly developed.  Animals are sold
in primary markets and outlying villages often make up a small herd for sale
at the nearest market, sometimes two or three days' trek away. Cattle traders
are the main buyers although trade is done with butchers on their own account.
Road transport is used extensively to move animals to the larger urban markets.
The markets are administered by Government Qfficials and a tax is levied on
transactions at 2-1/2% of the sale price. No sales records are kept, nor are
receipts used. There are no weighing facilities, bargains are struck on the
basis of estimated weights and no premium is paid for quality. It is esti-
mated that in addition to the sales tax, other direct charges in droving and
herding costs amount to no more than an additional 2-1/2% of the sales price.
Livestock prices fluctuate with seasonal supply. Sales of livestock tend to
peak at certain times of the year, and are influenced by availability of cash
(especially after harvest receipts), state of the pastures--a function of
rainfall--timing of tax payments, market prices for livestock and crops and
important religious feasts.



ANNEX 2
Page 5
17.       Constraints to Livestock Production.  The major constraints to in-
creasing levels of livestock productivity are summarized below:
(a) Lack of adequate animal disease control and prevention
services. Endemic diseases such as foot and mouth disease,
camel pox, sheep pox, and caprine pleuropneumonia are
widespread throughout YAR and are major causes of mortality,
reduced milk yields, and sub-optimal work performance. Simi-
larly, internal parasites such as the liver flukes, the sheep
nostril fly, and the sarcoptic sheep scale are major problems.
Severe outbreaks of Newcastle disease likewise have in the past
wiped out the poultry population in many areas of the country.
(b) Inadequate natural grazing for most of the year, and severe
overstocking of small communal grazing areas. UNDP officials
report significant regeneration and reappearance of indigenous
grasses in Southern Upland areas selected for range management
experimentation, competition for land for livestock as well as
crop activities makes sound management procedures difficult to
implement.
(c) Inadequate supplies of good quality fodder crops due to competi-
tion from grain and food crops and inadequate supplies of irri-
gation water.
(d) Lack of trained livestock extension personnel and services.
(e) Underdeveloped marketing facilities which do not provide the
farmer with sufficient price incentives to supply finished
animals.
(f) Lack of organized credit facilities and the problems faced
by the tenant farmer in providing adequate and acceptable
guarantees for a loan without the security of a long-term
lease on his land.
(g) Absence of improved and adapted breeds for specialized
dairy or wool production.
(h) Inadequate, irregular, and expensive supplies of fertilizers
and other farm inputs.
Prospects of Growth in Domestic Livestock Production
18.       Records and estimates compiled by the Ministry of Agriculture indi-
cate a decline in cattle, sheep and goat number during the five year drought
period which ended in 1975. Figures from the Ministry of Municipalities in-
dicate a decline in recorded cattle slaughtering and a fluctuating but mod-
erate increase in sheep and goat slaughtering over the same period. Data re-
trieval systems which would establish the major determinants of supply, i.e.
present herd and flock sizes, coefficients of production, price and income
elasticities of supply have only recently been introduced in YAR and no in-
formation on these factors is yet available. However, long term trends, under



ANNEX 2
Page 6
more normal rainfall conditions, traditional herds would increase in the
production of beef, mutton, goat and poultry meat, eggs and daLry products at
about 1% per annum (Table 4).
Consumption of Livestock Products
19.       The staple food group in the Yemeni diet is cereals with approxi-
mately 75 percent of average caloric and 72 percent of protein intake derived
from the consumption of cereal-based food items according to FAQ Food Balance
Sheets for Yemen, 1964-66 (see Table 1). The same source, based on production
and population data, reported per capita cereal consumption averaged 414.1 g
per day during the same period. In an independent 1972 village-level study,
Bornstein 1/ discovered daily direct per capita cereal consumption averaging
400 g.  The principal form in which cereals are consumed is wholegrain native
flat breads, with cereal-based porridges, gruels, and puddings also eaten.
Bread however is the principal basis of all meals and may represent the sole
food consmed at a meal.
20.       Although data concerning consumption patterns of livestock commodi-
ties remain limited, available information suggests that (1) consumption of
animal products per capita except for milk, is minimal, (2) such products are
expensive, and (3) livestock commodities are considered "normaL" goods with
relatively high income elasticities, (4) consumptive patterns vary interregion-
ally and change with urbanization. According to the rather dated FAQ Food
Balance Survey, (1964-66) consumption of animal products (inclusive of fish)
accounted for only 7 percent of average total per capita caloric intake and
17 percent of protein ingestation. These figures appear relatively low in
comparison with those for cereals given above.
21.       Regional Consumption Patterns.  Bornstein gives more recent depth
and insights into Yemeni consumption patterns, especially from interregional,
and urban vs. rural bases:
(a)  Mountainous areas.  Milk represents a small but reguLar seg-
ment of the diet in these regions, but is rarely consumed in
whole form. Milk is usually allowed to sour in a preparation
goard after which time the buttermilk is consumed directly or
utilized in traditional dishes, and the butterfat is commonly
marketed in response to high prices.  Although demand for
sheep's milk remains high, unlike in other areas of 'ZAR, that
for camel's milk remains high. The only common cheese in this
region is a white solid cheese prepared from goat's milk. Meat
is considered a luxury item eaten on the average once per
month, and often consumed only during religious celebrations.
All parts of the animal are utilized inclusive of alL internal
1/   A. Bornstein, Food and Society in Yemen Arab Republic, ES14:MISC/74/14,
FAQ, Rome, 1974.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
organs (except the lungs). Chickens are produced as an income-
generator and for family consumption on special occasions. Eggs
are enjoyed in food preparation but are usually sold for cash
or reserved for hatching purposes.
(b) The Tihama Zone. In the coastal areas of the Tihama, almost all
foods, except for fish, must be acquired from outside their com-
munities. Some villagers maintain small herds of sheep and
goats from which they acquire some internally generated milk
and meat supplies, however in the main, transactions are made
with semi-nomadic sheep and camel herdess for milk products.
Residents of the Tihama are averse to consuming beef, and be-
cause of high costs, reserve meat consumption for religious
holidays. In the more productive wadi areas of the Tihama,
production and subsequent consumption is relatively higher
than along the coast. The relatively lower per capita con-
sumption of livestock products in the Tihama is offset by
a major compensatory dietary factor--a higher intake of
fish from nearby sources.
(c) Urban Areas. Although some urban residents maintain link-
ages with rural sectors from which foodstuffs are funnelled,
or raise a kitchen garden or family cow partially to satisfy
family needs, the majority are wage-earners dependent pre-
dominantly on urban markets for consumption requirements.
Traditional food habits in such areas are undergoing modifi-
cation with (1) the relatively greater, non-seasonal avail-
ability of meats and other food items made possible through
more accessible supplies from internal sources with improved
transportation facilities and from supplementation of domes-
tic production with imported commodities; and (2) the intro-
duction of new foods such as wheat, rice, powdered milk, and
tinned foods from abroad. Data for urban-rural comparison of
consumptive patterns are particularly limited. Bornstein, in
a survey of lower-middle class immigrant families in Sana'a
and of residents of the village located in the highland
plateau near Yarim, attempted to examine possible differ-
ences (See Table 2). Although no quantitative information
was supplied, and reliance was made strictly on frequency re-
ports, the greatest point of diet variation was in the fre-
quency of meat consumption. Almost half of the Sana'a families
reported preparing meat every day while only 5% of respondents
in Arb did the same. Egg consumption appeared low in both the
urban and rural survey locations. Fruit consumption was con-
sidered abnormally high because of the seasonal availability
of grape supplies.



ANNEX 2
Page 8
22.       Population Growth.  The most important factor affecting future de-
mand for livestock products in Yemen will be population growth. According to
the 1975 nationwide census, the resident population totalled 5.2 million of
which only 0.35 million, or 6.7%, were located in governorate capitals. The
most densely populated areas are in the rainfed highlands, particularly the
Ibb/Taiz region where densities are probably twice the national average of
about 30 persons per square km. In desert and semi-arid areas of the Eastern
Zone and in the Tihama, the population remains relatively sparse. At the
time of population enumeration, an estimated 1.2 million persons were living
outside the country most of whom were males of working age holding employment
in nearby Gulf States, particularly Saudi Arabia.
23.       The population growth rate in Yemen presently estimated at 2.4% per
annum would be expected to begin to rise given the establishment of mortality-
depressing social amenities. For consumption project purposes, it has been
assumed that the population will continue to grow at 2.4%, 1976-1978; there-
after the rate is estimated to increase to 3.0% by 1990. It is likewise as-
sumed that the population in the major urban centers of Sana'a, Taiz, and
Hodeidah totalling 293,544 according to the preliminary results of the popu-
lation census, will continue to grow at 7% through 1978 and thereafter will
increase at a higher rate to either percent by 1990. The increase reflects
projected initial concentration and efficient application of social amenities
in urban areas as well as a continued rural-urban migration. On the other
hand, smaller towns and rural areas are expected to grow at 2% until 1978
after which the rate would rise to 2.45% by 1990. The slower growth in the
latter rate suggests the existence of a migratory dampening factor as well
as a slower establishment of social infrastructure potentially resulting in
continuation of high mortality rates. Population in the three urban centers
composed approximately 6% of the total resident population in 1975 and with
the higher rate of growth assumed to continue and even to rise, would repre-
sent 12% of the projected total of 7.75 million by 1990.
24.       Income Effect.  No study of the income elasticity of demand for any
commodity has been published in YAR. Available figures which might allow an
estimate of elasticity are either too incomplete or cover too short a time
period to be useful. For instance, although real prices of meat, fish and
eggs fell in Sana'a during 1973 and 1974 1/, consumption of meat also appears
to have fallen. FAO 2/ estimates in similar countries indicate income quan-
tity elasticities of demand for meat and fish ranging approximately between
0.4 and 1.0, and for eggs and milk ranging between 0.3 and 1.6. In most cases,
food elasticities are lower in the urban sector for basic local food crops and
higher for improved or processed foods. An uniform figure of 1.0 has been em-
ployed below for consumption projection purposes.
1/   CPO unpublished statistics.
2/   FAO, Income  Elasticities of Demand for Agricultural Products, Rome,
1972.



ANNEX 2
Page 9
Imports and Export of Fish and Livestock Products
25.       The value of animal products 1/ in current terms has ,over the last
several years represented only a minor portion of total imports into Yemen.
However, while total imports almost tripled in nominal terms over the 1969-
1973 period from 149.035 to 563.891 million YRls, imports of animal products
displayed even higher growth from 0.516 million YRls in 1969 to 14.678 million
displayed even higher growth from 0.516 million YRls in 1969 to 14.678 million
YRls in 1973. Correspondingly, animals, livestock products and fish as a per-
centage of the value of imports total grew from 0.35% in 1969 to 2.60% in 1973
(see Tables 3 and 4). In tonnage terms, imported fish and livestock products
have made up a small part of current supplies. Beef, fish, poultry meat, eggs,
and dairy product imports, as percentages of these current supplies represented
only 1.27, 2.29, 0.47, 0.02, and 2.02% respectively. No mutton and goat meat
is currently being imported into YAR indicating present sufficiency of domestic
supplies in terms of market demand, though not of nutritional needs. In abso-
lute quantities, processed dairy products dominated the livestock commodity
import category with 2,828 tons in 1973, while edible fish--an import as well
as an export items--was second in volume with 137 tons.
26.       Hides and skins are a major source of export income and rank only be-
hind cotton and coffee in income generation; in 1970 earnings in current prices
from hides and skins export totalled 1.2 million YRls, a sum increasing to 5.5
million YRls by 1973 but declining to 3.9 million YRls in 1974 (current prices).
In percentage terms, the commodities represented 7.58 and 15.2%, and 6.4% of
total merchandise export value in 1970, 1973, and 1974 respectively. Although
tonnage of hides and skins exported has stabilized recently at approximately
1,800 tons per year, it is felt that volume and value could be boosted in the
future by the introduction of improved flaying and handling techniques, as part
of a simple hides and skins improvement scheme.

import data (1973), estimated production for the various commodities, as well as
consumption estimates in part derived from CPO urban survey information, the
following table was constructed for 1973 (in tons):

28.       In Table 5 projections of domestic production, demancl and resultant
surpluses (deficits) to 1990 have been made in conformity with assumptions
stated therein. The magnitude of the projected deficits clearly displays the
degree of inability of domestic production under traditional patterns to satisfy
future demand for meat and other livestock products of a population considered
on the verge of now rapid growth and increasing urbanization, as well as display-
ing strong preference for such goods. Potential excess demand quantities are
projected to represent an increasing percentage of quantities clemanded (see
Table 3). In 1990, for example, amounts demanded over domestic production will
represent 74% for beef, 60% for mutton and goat meat, 16% for edible fish, 58%
for poultry meat, 64% for eggs, and 60% for dairy products. Provided that for-
eign exchange is available, some of these deficits could be erased by increased
imports of food products. However, foreign exchange for such product acquisi-
tion from foreign suppliers could quite likely prove in short supply given
projected huge excess demand for cereals, the staple food, in years to come
(see IBRD, An Appraisal of a Grain Storage and Processing Project, Yemen Arab
Republic).



ANNEX 2
Page 11
29.       Considerable pressure could likely be placed on livestock product
prices by the existence of such inadequacy in local production possibly re-
sulting in some substitution of fish, vegetables, and fruit for potentially
increasingly costly livestock items. However, further research into the
magnitude of price elasticities and cross-price elasticities of supply and
demand for various food commodities would be required for further assessment.
It does appear clear, however, that such deficits expected in the future would
not only suggest potentially profitable enterprises for progressive farmers em-
ploying improved livestock technology in diversified operations, but also pro-
vide technical and social justification for specialized projects capturing econ-
omies of scale aimed at increasing livestock production.
30.       A small number of cattle and camels are recorded as exports to Saudi
Arabia annually. The only significant export intended for human consumption,
however, is fish. Fish exports totalled 2,687 tons in 1972 and fell to 444
tons in 1973. The fall in exports is partly accounted for by the scaling down
of operations of the United Fisheries Company of Kuwait which ceased operation
in Yemen altogether in July 1974.

Agricultural and Agroindustrial Credit
Banking System
Background
1.        The practice of modern banking in YAR dates back to 1962 when the
Yemen Bank for Reconstruction and Development (YBRD) was established by Gov-
ernment with a majority state shareholding. Besides performing commercial
banking functions, it implemented Government monetary and development poli-
cies until the formation of the Central Bank of Yemen (CB) in 1971. Five com-
mercial banks, four foreign and YBRD, now operate in Yemen. The foreign banks
are the Habib Bank, the British Bank of the Middle East, the United Bank and
the Arab Bank. There are no specialized institutions for financing of agri-
cultural and industrial sectors.
2.        The Banking Law of 1972, administered by CB, regulates all banking
operations in YAR. It provides for licensing and supervision of banks, stipu-
lates capital, reserve and liquidity requirements, and prescribes accounting
and auditing procedures.
The Central Bank of Yemen
3.        The CB was established in September 1971 with an authorized capital
of YR 10 million. IMF experts assisted the Government in its establishment
and helped in organizing its various departments. CB's main functions include
regulation of banking and credit in the country and acting as banker to the
Government. Credit supply by banks is regulated through two major instruments,
namely statutory reserve requirements and rediscounting of credit papers. The
reserve requirement is seasonally adjusted in line with demand for credit and
is generally reduced during the cotton financing season to allow larger flow
of credit. The CB also rediscounts commercial papers with a maturity not ex-
ceeding six months from the date of discount and is also authorized to redis-
count agricultural and industrial credit instruments with a maturity of not
more than 9 months. In addition to the above facilities, CB provides loans
to the Cotton General Company (public sector organization), under a consortium
arrangement with commercial banks, for production and marketing of cotton.
Table 1 gives its balance sheets for the years 1971 to 1975.
4.        CB's management is vested in a Board of Directors consisting of a
Governor who is the Board's Chairman, a Deputy-Governor, a representative each
from the Ministry of Finance and the Ministry of Economy, and three other di-
rectors nominated by the Minister of Finance. Both the Governor and the
Deputy-Governor are appointed by the President of the Republic for an initial



ANNEX 3
Page 2
period of four years and are eligible for reappointment. The Governor is
the Chief Executive Officer and is responsible for implementation of the
policies determined by the Board. The CB has six departments including a Bank-
ing Control Department recently established to regulate and supervise the op-
eration of banks. Its Head Office is in Sana'a and it has two branch offices,
at Hodeidah and Taiz.
5.        CB periodically fixes interest rates on deposits and loans for guid-
ance of banks. The current minimum rates fixed are 5% on savings and 6%-9% on
fixed deposits for 3 to 12 montrhs. The minimum lending rates are 12% on com-
mercial loans and 14% on personal loans.
Yemen Bank for Reconstruction and Development
6.        YBRD is the largest commercial bank in YAR.  Its share capital is
YRls 10 million of which 51% is held by the Government and the rest by the
public. Nearly half of its resources are derived from deposits which amounted
to YRls 156 million at the end of 1974. YBRD extends short-term loans and ad-
vances mainly for internal and foreign trade and for industrial activities to
private and public sector enterprises. It grants substantial credit facilities
to the Cotton General Company every year for procurement and export of cotton
and also provides assistance for import of farm machinery, fertilizers and other
agricultural inputs. A few loans are also granted to farmers for purchase of
farming equipmnt and for development of groundwater. Its loans and advances in-
creased from YRls 52 million in 1969 to YRls 212 million in 1974. In fulfillment
of its developmental role, YBRD has contributed YRls 16 million to the share
capital of eleven public sector companies and is also involved in their
management.
7.        The Bank is managed by a Board of eleven directors, six of whom in-
cluding the Chairman, are Government representatives. The remnaining directors
are elected by the private shareholders. The Bank's Head Office and principal
business office are in Sana'a and it has eleven branches and agencies through-
out the country.
8.        YBRD was selected in 1973 as the credit channel for the Industrial
Estate Project (Credit 465-YAR).
Foreign Banks
9.        The four foreign banks handle about 30% of YAR's banking business.
They finance the the commercial sector, mainly foreign trade, and are per-
mitted to accept deposits from the public. They have six branches in three
major towns - Sana'a, Hodeidah and Taiz.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
Agricultural Credit
Background
10.       Institutional credit to agriculture in YAR is practically limited to
the cotton crop at present. Farmers principally depend on their own resources,
remittances from relatives from abroad (mainly Saudi Arabia) and other non-
institutional sources such as friends and traders, for investment in agriculture.
11.       Prior to the establishment of Agricultural Credit Fund (ACF), two
attempts were made to establish a national institutional credit source for the
agricultural sector. A proposal was mooted in 1969 to set up an agricultural
credit department within YBRD for providing production loans to farmers, but
it did not materialize due to lack of resources and trained manpower. In 1971
the National Parliament enacted a law creating an Agricultural Bank. This bank
could not be established due to more or less similar reasons. Parliament in
1975 passed a new law establishing a wholly state-owned Agricultural Credit
Bank (ACB) under the Ministry of Agriculture which began operations on July 1,
1976 with a paid in capitalization of YRls 20 million, and of an authorized
capital of YRls 100 million to be subscribed by 1980. Government is con-
sidering merging the Agricultural Credit Fund (ACF) with ACB and has requested
IDA to assist in this effort.
The Agricultural Credit Fund (ACF)
12.       It was decided under the Tihama Development Project (TDP) to estab-
lish an Agricultural Credit Fund within the CB to finance the project benefi-
ciaries. The ACF has since been selected also as the financing channel for
the Southern Uplands Rural Development Project (SURDEP) 1/. Under both the
projects, short, medium and long-term, loans would be provided to farmers for
purchase of agricultural inputs and for capital investments.
13.       According to organizational arrangements worked out for ACF under
TDP, it will be headed by a Manager (expatriate) who will recommend to the CB's
Board of Directors, policies to be followed by the ACF and, after their approval,
would be responsible for their implementation. He would also formulate detailed
procedures for ACF's operations and would undertake training and supervision of
local staff, both at headquarters and at the field offices. The Manager was
appointed February 1975. The TDP area would be served by two offices, one at
Wadi Zabid and the other at Wadi Mawr. For the SURDEP area, there would be
two more offices at Taiz and Ibb. The offices in each project area would be
under the charge of an expatriate Field Manager who would be responsible to
the Manager.
1/   Credit 545-YAR.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
14.       The first field office at Wadi Zabid was opened in April 1975 and
lending operations have since started. Table 2 of this Annex shows ACF's finan-
cial position as of October 31, 1975. Size and scope of 1975 credit activities
(through October) are still modest, but the growth trend is encouraging (see
Table 3). Short-term loans mainly for purchase of oil for irrigation pumps and
fertilizers, and medium-term loans for on-farm development and farm machinery
aggregating YRls 410,000 have been disbursed so far. Good progress has been
made in establishing the operational procedures and systems. T'he expatriate
Credit Officer assigned to the Wadi Zabid Field Office has trained two Yemeni
loan officers in loan appraisal and other operational duties. However, ACF
still faces problems of recruiting Yemeni counterparts and proper support of
the CB. The expatriate Manager in Sana'a is still without a qualified counter-
part and the CB's lack of interest is reflected in the assignment of inade-
quately qualified and poorly motivated counterparts to head office positions.
ACF also needs additional transport facilities (e.g. motorcycles) for loan in-
quiries as well as for periodic supervision and collection of loans. Under
the project, ACF's resources would increase substantially, which would de-
crease ACF's dependence on the CB. It would also be staffed and receive addi-
tional technical assistance for carrying out properly its responsibilities
under the project (see para 18 below).
Security
15.       According to ACF's present lending procedures, short-term loans up
to YRls 5,000 are provided against guarantee of two persons. Short-term loans
in excess of the above amount and all medium and long-term loans would require
land security. The loans to LDC as a Government enterprise would be secured
by a Government guarantee. Loans to landowners would require land and/or
other property security. Land would be acceptable as security up to 80% and
other property up to 60% of the current market value. Loans to butchers for
tools and equipment would be made only to those properly licensed to operate
in a municipality retail market and would be secured by a joint guarantee of
two creditworthy persons. Loans to tenant farmers would be secured by one
of the following alternatives (a) for short-term loans of up to YRls 5,000
a joint guarantee of two creditworthy persons or a guarantee by the land-
owner, (b) for longer term loans, the landowners guarantee or land security
or a joint and several guarantee by a group of tenants or livestock security
which would involve some form of permanent animal identification such as
branding, earmarking or ear tagging with the number or mark registered with
ACF. Since this is not a common practice, extension agents would need to
fully explain the purpose of such identification and the legal implications
of having their livestock used as security for loans.
Supervision and Recovery of Loans
16.       Loans would be supervised by ACF staff who would periodically visit
LDC and VDA installations. Financial statements would be obtained annually
from the above subborrowers. Loans for traditional sector would be super-
vised by ACF Branch office extension officers equipped with motorcycles and
depending on farm size and location would service about 40 loans. Emphasis
would be on field work with each beneficiary visited at least once every 30
days during the first year of development. Extension officers would be



ANNEX 3
Page 5
assist in on-farm loans supervision and collection of debt service. He would
adopt a cooperative and planning approach rather than a coercive approach to
loan recovery. As part of the whole enterprise approach to farm extension he
would have a knowledge of finance and be in a position to assist subborrowers
to plan financial management.
ACF Organization and Staffing
17.       The Organizational expansion and additional staffing of ACF dis-
cussed here is based on the requirements of this and the proposed Grain Storage
Project. The two projects would operate largely in the Sana'a, Hodeidah and
Taiz areas. ACF's field office at Taiz proposed under the Southern Uplands
Project would become operational by the time the project would mature for im-
plementation. An expansion of the Taiz field office and two additional field
offices, one at Sana'a and the other at Hodeidah, would be necessary to service
the two projects. ACF's organization chart showing the required additions is
provided in Annex 3, Chart 1.
ACF Staffing
18.       ACF is not, at present, able to undertake financing of agroindustrial
and livestock activities. A major institution building effort is required to
prepare ACF's Head Office, as well as the field offices, for the increased
volume and variety of lending operations. Table 1 of this Annex shows in detail
the staffing and additional offices and equipment that would be required under
these two projects. In summary the project would provide for:
(a) Head Office, Sana'a. The extension of ACF's General Manager
for three additional years after the termination of his term
under the Tihama Development Project. One industrial credit
officer and one livestock credit officer would establish
lending procedures for, and supervise, the agroindustrial and
livestock loans. One accountant/financial controller would
set up ACF's accounting and financial control system. A farm
management extension officer would supervise the farm credit
extension operations. All four additional staff would be
expatriates and work under the overall supervision and
guidance of ACF's Manager. Apart from their operational
responsibilities, the expatriates' main function would be
the selection and training of local counterparts. Each ex-
patriate would have a Yemeni counterpart. In addition, the
ACF head office would be staffed with a legal adviser.  The
Yemeni staff would undertake operational and management re-
sponsibilities during the project's implementation.
(b)  Branch Office, Sana'a.  To handle the increased lending opera-
tions in the Sana'a area, a local branch office would be es-
tablished in Sana'a. It would be staffed by a branch manager,



ANNEX 3
Page 6
an agricultural credit officer and an industrial credit officer
as well as five extension officers.  All staff would be locally
recruited: it would work under the guidance and supervision
of ACF's head office manager and staff.
(c) Field Office, Hodeidah. A field office would be established
in Hodeidah. It would be staffed with a senior credit officer
and an extension officer, both locally recruited, and it would
operate under the supervision of the (already appointed) Wadi
Zabid Field Manager.
(d) Expansion Taiz/Ibb Office. ACF's field office at Taiz and
Ibb would receive one additional credit officer and three
extension officers, all Yemeni staff.
ACF's additional staff requirements would thus be four expatriate experts,
and eight Yemeni credit officers, one Yemeni legal advisor and. ten livestock
extension officers. The head office, branch and field offices would be
provided with the necessary general supporting staff, equipment, vehicles
and other facilities. (For details see Table 4 of this Annex).
Training
19.       Apart from on-the-job training to be conducted largely by the ex-
patriate experts, the projects would provide for six two-year graduate courses
on banking and financing, with emphasis on agricultural and agroindustrial
financing. They would also provide for five six-month courses with practical
training at agricultural credit institutions, preferably in Arabic speaking
countries. The Government has been asked to initiate the recruitment process
for training candidates and ACF staffing. It would prepare a list of suit-
able candidates for the actual training to be started as soon as possible.
Resources of ACF
20.       The originally decided funding of ACF for medium- and long-term
credits under the Tihama Development Project was to consist of: (a) US$0.665
million lent to ACF by Government; and (b) US$2 million from the proceeds of
the IDA credit. The funds for short-term production credit were to be ob-
tained by ACF from Government by special agreement, from loans and rediscount
facilities, and other sources. The Government loan under (a) was to be made
at an interest rate of 4% per annum for 15 years, including five years of grace;
the same conditions were to be applied for the on-lent proceeds of the IDA
credit. However, it was agreed that if ACF should become an independent
agricultural credit institution, the Government loan would be transformed
into equity.



ANNEX 3
Page 7
21.       The initial resources as described in para 28 above were comple-
mented under the SURDEP by (a) US$1,500,000 (YRls 6,750,000) from the proceeds
of the IDA/other sources credit for financing of short-term agricultural in-
put loans and by (b) US$1,100,000 (YRls 4,950,000) from the proceeds of the
same credit for financing of medium- and long-term loans. The former amount
will be a governmental grant in case ACF becomes an independent institution.
The latter amount will be passed on by Government to ACF as a loan at an
interest of 1% per annum for a term of 15 years including five years of grace.
A 3% interest rate differential will be credited by ACF to a special reserve
fund account to cover losses of ACF arising from drought conditions which may
reasonably be expected to depress borrowers' repayment capacities over the
life of the project.
22.       The resources required for financing ACF's loans proposed under the
Livestock Credit and Processing Project would amount to YRls 18.6 million for
the LDC investments, YRls 0.8 million for village pasture development and
YRls 19.2 million for supervised credit to traditional sector. The Government
would make these funds available as a loan to ACF at an interest rate of 4.0%
per annum. In addition, medium-term credit for financing interest during im-
plementation and seasonal credit would amount to YRls 2.0 million. ACF would
repay the medium-term loan to Government not later than project year six. The
projected cash flow for ACF operations under the Livestock Credit and Process-
ing Project including the inflow and outflow of project funds for which ACF
has only administrative responsibility is provided in Annex 12 Table 1.
Accounts and Audit
23.       ACF accounting has been handled by CB's accounting department, with
accounts kept separately from CB's own accounts. As ACF's operations would
increase substantially under this and the proposed Grain Storage and Process-
ing Project, it would be necessary for ACF to build up and handle its own
accounts. An accounting and financial control expert would be appointed not
later than mid-1977 for a period of four years to assist ACF in establishing
and operating a modern accounting and financial control system. Until ACF's
accounting system becomes operational CB's accounting department would con-
tinue to handle ACF's accounts. Under the project, ACF accounts would also be
audited by independent auditors acceptable to IDA. Audit reports and finan-
cial statements would be submitted to IDA not later than four months after the
close of each fiscal year. LDC and MSMD would keep records consistent with
sound accounting practices and adequate to reflect its operations and finan-
cial conditions. PU, AHD, LRID would also establish and maintain accounts for
project expenditures.
