APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
Project Organization and Operating Procedures
Project Organization
1.        There is no present institution in YAR with the capability to imple-
ment the project. A Livestock Development Board (LDB) would be established,
by the signing of the proposed credit, under the Ministry of Agriculture for
this purpose. Estimated costs are shown in Table 1. The LDB would consist of
the Ministry of Agriculture as Chairman, a representative of the Central Plan-
ning Office, the Mlinistry of Finance, the Ministry of Municipalities and, the
General Manager of ACF, the Project Manager and Project Co-Manager. For the
administration and management of the project during implementation, a Project
Unit (PU) responsible to LDB would be established. Government has, at IDA's
suggestion, contracted with a management firm, Hendrikson Associates, to sup-
plv Project management personnel.
2.        The PU's responsibilities wqould be:
(a)  to establish the LDC, the Municipality Slaughterhouse and
M1arkets Division (MSMD), the Animal Health Division (AHD)
andl the Livestock and Range Improvement Division (LRID);
(b) to implement in cooperation with the above entities, dairv
farm, livestock finishing farm, milk collection colony,
poultry and feed mill, slaughterhouse, retail mrket, animal
health and livestock and range improvement components;
(c)  to implement in cooperation with ACF the supervised credit
component for the traditional sector credit for on-farm
livestock development and for retail butclRer credit;
(d) to initiate and supervise the dairy farm site, livestock
finishing farm site and Wadi Surdud farn  studies;
(e) to coordinate the internal and external training programs
provided under the project for Yemeni staff; and
(f) to coordinate the employment of expatriate technical staff
for the various components.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
3.        The LDC would be a semi-autonomous Government-owned enterprise es-
tablished to own and to operate on a sound commercial basis about eight
50-cow dairy farms, a 250 ha livestock finishing and dairy farm, five milk
collection colonies and a poultry-feedmill complex. Government has been asked
prior to negotiations to draft the LDC charter and Government's approval of
LDC's charter satisfactory to IDA would be a condition of making the proposed
credit effective. Government has initiated recruitment action to employ the
expatriate team to implement and manage the LDC project component.
4.        MSMD would be established for implementing and operating slaughter-
houses and retail markets in Sana'a, Taiz and Hodeidah. After project year 6,
MSMD would be transferred from the PU to the direct control of the Ministry of
Municipalities and would be responsible for the operation of all slaughter-
house and retail markets within the country.  Government ass&rances were
obtained that the following requirements would be enforced:
(i) no slaughter would be authorized within Sana'a, Taiz, and
Hodeidah except at Government slaughterhouses;
(ii) the Ministry of Municipalities would take steps to enforce
meat inspection at the slaughterhouses;
(iii) the retail markets being constructed under the project
would be licensed and maintained in a sanitary manner;
(iv) slaughter, delivery and retail market feeds would be
charged to make the facilities financially self-supporting;
(v) animals would be slaughtered by MSMD employees and carcasses
and offal would be delivered by the slaughterhouses to
retail markets; and
(vi) MSMD would charge sufficient fees for their services to
operate on a sound financial basis the slaughterhouse and
retail markets and all its revenue shall be transferred
back to Government.
5.        The AHD would consolidate and expand the administrative and field
experience of the U.K. technical assistance (ODM) and the Ministry of
Agriculture's Directorate of Animal Health in the subproject. ODM would
provide the expatriate manager and the majority of the expatriate technical
staff for implementing the program.
6.        ACF would be expanded by adding regional branch offices and sup-
porting staff to provide credit on a country-wide basis for implementing
all components of the project. ACF would be responsible, in coordination with
the PU for implementing the supervised credit component of the project to the
traditional sector subborrowers.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
Project Management
7.        The LDB would decide on all policy issues, major investments and
operational matters, and approve key personnel decisions. Detailed Board pro-
cedures would be drafted and be approved within 45 days of the signing of the
proposed credit. These would include the following:
(a) the Project Manager would act as the convenor of the Board
and he would initiate the meetings in consultation with the
approval of the Chairman;
(b) a decision of the Board could be obtained by circulating
working papers. Decisions taken in a Board meeting or by
circulation could be acted upon before the minutes are
confirmed formally in the next meeting;
(c)  the Board would delegate adequate financial and admiristrative
authority to the Project Manager and the divisional heads
so that meetings of the Board would be necessary only for
major policy and essential operational issues;
(d) the Board would approve all major appointments such ELs consul-
tants, all expatriate personnel, and all senior Yemeni per-
sonnel. Authority for appointments to junior posts would be
delegated to the Project Manager and the divisional heads
as appropriate;
(e)  all major tenders for construction, supplies and services
would be subject to the approval of the Board. However,
relatively minor supplies and services would be the re-
sponsibility of the Project Manager and the division heads.
8.        The Project Manager would have the overall operational responsibility
for the proposed project implementation and management. Four operational
divisions, MSMD, LDC, AHD and LRID would be in charge of the individual proj-
ect components. The financial assistant with the PU would assist in devel-
oping accounting and financial controls within the divisions. A project im-
plementation organization chart is provided (Chart 1). The Project Manager
in cooperation with the Division Managers would formulate operating, accounting
and auditing procedures for approval by the LDB. The PU head office and the
divisional offices would be located in Sana'a except the LRID which would be
housed in Damar.
9.        In view of the severe shortage of experienced technical staff in YAR,
the project implementation units would be staffed with 29 expatriate management
and technical experts, for periods ranging from two to five years (see Annex 10,
Table 1). These experts would be responsible for both project implementation and
for training of their local counterparts, who would take over inanagement and
technical responsibilities when the expatriates complete their assignments.
Further details on expatriate duties are provided in Annex 10.

ANNEX 5

APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
Livestock Development Corporation
1.        Organization.  Under the proposed project, a Livestock Development
Corporation would be established by decree as a legal semi-autonomous Govern-
ment enterprise to operate a commercially Driented business with the objective
of increasing domestic production of meat, poultry and dairy produce in YAR.
The LDC would comprise three divisions:
(a) Dairy Production Division. This division would provide milk
for urban markets through eight 50-cow commercial dairy herds,
and five associated milk colonies.
(b)  Poultry and Feed Division.  This division would have a broiler
production unit to serve Taiz, a hatchery to produce its own
chicks as well as to supply stock to smallholders in the
region, and a feed mill with the potential to supply the
country's poultry industry as well as other livestock.
(c) Livestock Finishing Division. This division would fatten
livestock on an irrigated fodder and feedlot farm in the
Tihama.
These divisions would not only increase production of dairy and meat products,
but would serve as pilot models for future expansion.
2.        The LDC Manager, who would be an expatriate for the first five
years, would report to the Project Manager. A Yemeni Co-Manager would be
trained to take over LDC management after five years. Each of the three
divisions would have an expatriate manager, and a Yemeni co-manager. The
consolidated cash flow for the three divisions is shown in Table 1, Appen-
dix 4.
Dairy Production Division
3.        Background.  Except for two small commercial operations, one each
in Taiz and Hodeidah, and a Government-owned dairy barn close to Sana'a, there
is no dairy production serving the major urban markets in Yemen. Milk is pro-
duced in varying quantities from sheep, goats, camels and the local zebu cows.
Milk surplus to family requirements is either made into cheese or ghee, or
exchanged with neighbors on a reciprocal basis. Very little fresh milk is
marketed. Increasing urbanization, and changing social customs such as
feeding babies from bottles, together with increased spending power of urban
dwellers, is combined to create a steadily increasing off-farm demand for
imported milk powders and milk products.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
4.        Objectives.  Within LDC, the Dairy Production Division would estab-
lish eight 50-cow commercial type dairy units, five near Sana"a, three at Taiz
,d one at Ibb.  A similar sized unit would be incorporated into the Livestock
Finishing Division farm to be established near Hodeidah. In addition dairy
production would be encouraged amongst subsistence farmers in areas close to
the proposed dairy farms. It is planned to establish five mi:Lk colonies,
each with about 200 imported, improved cows, with in each case, one of the
commercial farms acting as the focal point for milk collection, extension
and demonstration, bull services, and at a later date supplying small num-
bers of improved cows and bulls. The proposed farms would begin to improve
the supply of milk to urban markets thereby contributing, albeit in a small
way, to improving nutrition standards and reducing demand for imported milk
products. Furthermore, they would serve as an important training ground
for Yemen farmers and technicians and lay the foundation for expanded pro-
duction in a second phase project. Because there is no experience with com-
mercial dairy farming in Yemen, and because of constraints caused by the
availability of suitable land and the supply of adequate water for irrigation
in the upland regions, the size of each farm would be confined to about 30 ha
supporting 50 improved cows and all replacements. Should larger areas of
suitable land become available then herd sizes could be increased up to about
100 head or even more.
5.        Site Selection.  Before final selection of each site a consultant
report (provided for in project costs) would be required on t]ne suitability
of the land for irrigation and the crop production proposed, and on the avail-
ability of suitable water. Furthermore, Government assurances would be sought
on the actual availability of the land and in the event of some or all of it
being occupied, that tenants would be paid fair compensation. The consultant
report would also cover such aspects as milk marketing and distribution and
any environmental factors likely to be affected.
6.        Project Phasing and Development.  One farm would be established in
Project Year 1 and two in Year 2. Three further farms would be commenced in
Year 3 and two more in Year 4. The Hodeidah dairy component would start in
Year 1. Development of the milk colonies would be commenced in each of Years
2, 3 and 4. Irrigation requirements are based on about 20-25 ha being irrigated
from one tube well and the balance of 5-10 ha from spate or flood irrigation.
The tube-well channel distribution system, land levelling and preparation, and
about 10 ha lucerne sowing would be completed during Year 1. During Year 2,
the total area would be sown to crops or lucerne. Total investment in each
farm, including 5% physical contingency, is shown in Table 3. Investments
include land levelling, roads and irrigation, building and stockhandling
facilities, machinery plant and vehicles and livestock. Because there is
considered to be no economic advantage, either short or long term in importing
very high priced pedigree cows, cost estimates provide for disease free,
guaranteed in-calf, commercial grade Friesian cows, but registered pedigree
bulls.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
7.        Forage Production and Farm Management.  Each farm would be fully
mechanized. Operating costs may be reduced by direct grazing of lucerne and
forage sorghum but as the required management skills are completely absent
in YAR, and are more difficult to efficiently implement, initially feed would
all be on a feed-lot basis. Animals would be housed in simple, cheaply con-
structed barns with partly open walls providing maximum air and sunlight pene-
tration. Adequate shade would be provided along with necessary feeding throughs
and watering facilities. Cows would be tied for milking which would be done by
portable 2-cow vacuum plants either electrically or motor operated. Sophis-
ticated and equally expensive milking machinery and plant is not considered
to be justified in this first phase dairy development project. Similarly,
artificial insemination would not be introduced at this stage.
8.        Forage production would be based on a six year rotation growing
improved varieties of maize and forage sorghum, with a winter catch crop of
green barley between, followed by four years of lucerne. About 5 ha of
lucerne, maize, sorghum and barley would be sown every year. Some 5 ha of
maize would be conserved as silage, stored in cheaply constructed open trench
silos covered with plastic. In Year 4 total utilizable forage dry matter
(DM) production would be about 392 tons or about 13 tons DM per ha. An addi-
tional 11 tons DM of feed grain (9.5) and calf concentrate would be purchased
in each. In Year 1 a surplus of about 40 tons would be carried forward as a
permanent revolving forage reserve.
9.        Total animal units including 50 milk cows are estimated to reach
90 in Year 5 and 97 in Year 8. During the first four years whilst replacement
numbers are building up, surplus forage except for the permanent reserve, would
be used to finish up to 50 feeder cattle on the same basis as with the livestock
finishing farm. In addition to a 25% technical contingency allowed in the an-
nual forage requirement (4,725 kg DM) for each milk cow and 10% for all other
cattle, the feeder cattle allow a safety value during the establishment years.
Should, for various unforeseen reasons, expected forage production not be
achieved, they would not be purchased but the milking herd projections would
remain unaffected. Animal forage requirements for all livestock are based
on balanced rations with adequate energy and protein concentration so as to
allow the projected milk and growth rate projections to be safely achieved.
Milking cow rations would include an average of 50-60 gms of grains per cow
daily. The emphasis would be on keeping grain production to a minimum.
Calving would be on all year round basis and heifers would calve for the
first time at three years. During the post-development phase, growth rates
are expected to allow calving at 2-1/2 or even two years, thereby increasing
the percentage of productive animals, and total farm milk production.
10.       Production.  Annual milk production per cow would average about
3,600 litres from Year 5 onward, rising from an initial level (adjusted for
first year calving) of 2,000 litres. After allowing for calf consumption of
about 10,500 litres, total annual sales from each farm.would average about
170,000 litres from Year 5. Except for a fmew culls aIl calves would be reared



ANNEX 5
Page 4
for replacement or sale. First sales of one-year old surplus bulls would be
available in Year 3, and surplus in-calf three year heifers in Year 5. From
Year 9 total annual sales would amount to about 36 head of wlhich some 24 would
be available for farmers. Annual meat production of all saleable cattle would
average about five tons. When the eight farms are in full production in Year
9, total annual milk sales would amount to about 1,360,000, plus an additional
150,000 litres from the dairy component of the livestock finishing farm. A
further 1,615, 000 litres per annum would be marketed from the five associated
milk colonies. Total annual project marketed milk from Year 9 onward would
be about 3,125,000 litres.
11.       Milk Marketing.  Because the volume of milk from each farm does not
justify special treatment facilities, nor at this stage would any economic
advantage be derived from such expense, milk would be sold untreated direct
to the public. This is now the practice at the Government Farm in Sana'a.
Milk would be available twice daily at selected points and be sold direct
from the truck or farm. In the event of the farms being sited some dis-
tance from an urban center, an allowance has been made for cooling and
transportation facilities and costs. Milk would be marketed in towns at
YRls 2 per litre, the current average price in the larger urban centers of
Sana'a, Taiz and Hodeidah.
12.       Management and Labor.  As modern dairy management and production
skills are virtually unknown in YAR an expatriate Dairy Division Manager
located in the Project Headquarters Unit, would be responsib]le for the
development and initial management of the eight farms. An important func-
tion of the Divisional Manager would be to ensure the practical training of
Yemen farm managers and herdsmen so that adequate staff woulcl be available
for the farms to be developed in subsequent years. For this reason in order
to facilitate closer supervision and training, the first farms would be
developed near Sana'a. Each farm would have a resident manager and senior
herdsman, and 11 supporting staff including a mechanic, records clerk, and
two tractor drivers. The Divisional Manager would coordinate with the Live-
stock Finishing Division Manager and ACF extension staff in the establishment
of the proposed milk colonies, and would be responsible for the overall super-
vision of the milk collection and marketing aspects of the colonies.
13.       Animal Health Control.  Apart from the imported cattle and the
feeder animals purchased during the first four years, the farms would be self-
contained. All livestock would be vaccinated against rinderpest, anthrax,
foot and mouth disease, clostridial diseases, and females against brucel-
loisis. Full allowance has been made for regular control of both internal
and external parasites and all animals would have daily access to a compre-
hensive mineral mixture. Facilities for cool storage of drugs and medicines
would be provided. Animal health control would be exercised by the Senior
Herdsman under the supervision of the Regional Veterinarian.
14.       Investment and Financing.  Of the total investment, 40% would be
contributed as equity and the balance would be financed by a long term loan
from ACF. During the first three years additional medium term loans would



ANNEX 5
Page 5
be required to cover interest during construction. These would be repaid
before the long term loan repayment which would be spread over seven years,
commencing in Year 5 as shown in Appendix 1, Table 2. The financial rate of
return is estimated to be about 12%.
15.       Milk Production Colonies.  The future development of Yemen agri-
culture and livestock depends largely on the private or traditional sector.
The milk colonies would represent the first attempt to implement integrated
development with an important milk production component. These are described
in para. 4, and in greater detail in Annex 7. They would therefore be of
considerable importance in setting the pattern for farmer acceptance of new
technology and in achieving a considerable production increase. LDC and ACF
would attach high priority to their successful establishment of milk collec-
tion centers and marketing of milk produced by the proposed milk colonies. It
would also coordinate with ACF extension person nel in selecting suitable
areas and farmers, arranging for the supply and distribution of imported cows
and providing technical back-up to farmers.
16.       The selection of each colony area would depend on its proximity
to one of the divisional farms and suitable urban markets. Furthermore the
farms and farmers would have to meet the requirements necessary for imple-
mentation of the proposed development. Development of each colony would
be phased over 3 years with 20, 60 and 20 farmers starting in each consecu-
tive year.  On-farm development would take about 4 years to achieve full
production in each case. The marketing operation of the colonies would be
under the direction of the divisional manager. Each colony would be managed
by a supervisor and supporting staff, equipped with a small conveniently lo-
cated collection center consisting of an office and milk receiving room in-
cluding where necessary milk cooling facilities. In some cases the LDC farm
may be the center.
17.       The Milk Collection Center (MCC) for each colony would operate every-
day. If necessary, out-station collection points would be established. The
milk truck would act as receiving station in these cases. Farmers would bring
milk to the centers twice daily. It would be measured, tested for water con-
tent, and the farmer paid in cash. Total production records from each farm
would be kept by the milk center. The price paid to farmers would be below
that obtained by the LDC in the urban market thus allowing the cost of trans-
portation and marketing to be recovered. In addition the MCC would act as an
ordering center for farmers' input supplies, veterinary and bull services.
Input supply requirements would be passed on to ACF extension personnel,
veterinary needs to the region veterinary center, and bull service needs to
the farm manager. If cows requiring service could not be walked to the
divisional farm, bulls would be transported to an agreed point in a small
trailer.
18.       Projected income, cash flow and investments are shown in Annex 5,
Appendix 1, Tables 6, 7 and 8. Details of on-farm investments costs, develop-
ment program, income, and projected cash flow are shown in Annex 7, Appendix 1,
Tables 1 to 7.



ANNEX 5
Page 6
Livestock Finishing Division
19.       Objective.  Although livestock are an integral part of the YAR farm-
ing industry, the sub-sector is characterized by the absence of any finishing
operations.  As a consequence a high percentage of the total annual offtake
are slaughtered when in comparatively poor condition and in many cases are
well below potential liveweight. The Livestock Finishing Division of LDC
would begin to tackle this problem by the establishment and operation of a
250 ha irrigated fodder farm in the coastal Tihama, where the availability of
suitable land and water is reasonably assured. A small 50-cow dairy unit
would be included in the farm operation.
20.       Operations.  Irregular rainfall and recurrent droughts make it
necessary to produce assured supplies of suitable forage through irrigated
crop production. The irrigated fodder production proposed calls for an in-
tensive mechanized system since it is unlikely that sufficient labor would
be found to operate the farm on a labor intensive basis. The feedlot opera-
tion would establish a regular market for unfinished animals, sheep, goats,
and cattle of varying ages, produce a regular supply of meat of standard
quality irrespective of the season and assist in providing rural employment
opportunities. Furthermore, the proposed project would bring virtually waste
land into production and by feeding unfinished animals for a short intensive
period immediately prior to slaughter contribute to increasing national meat
supplies. A small amount of milk would be marketed and a start made towards
examining the possibilities for larger scale dairy production in the Tihama
region.
21.       Irrigation.  Apart from a coastal sand belt, soils of the Tihama
are for the most part free draining alluvial and colluvial sandy silts. They
are in general very alkaline with pH readings varying between 7.0 and 8.4.
A large (unmeasured) underground aquifer is said to exist. Existing wells,
of which there are an increasing number, vary between 22 - 90m in depth.
Water is of varying quality decreasing in salinity as distance from the coast
increases. The alternative sites proposed for the farm would have access to
some spate of seasonal flood water but basic irrigation would be provided from
10 tube wells using a concrete or stone lined channel distribution system.
Each well is estimated to yield 18 litre/second which after taking account of
local weather conditions, and crop water requirements together with a 75% ef-
ficiency allowance, would irrigate about 20 hectares. Complete cost of each
well (70m depth) including drilling, casing, pump, motor, house, control dis-
charge valve and installation is shown in Table 3. Final farm site selections
would be based on the findings and recommendations of a consultant study, pro-
posed as part of the Project.
22.       Project Development.  On completion of the irrigation System in year
one, the productive area of 250 ha would be progressively sown to forage crops
until full utilization in the second half of year three. Investrnent costs in-
clude roads and land preparation, tubewells and channelling, buiLdings and
stock handling facilities, machinery and vehicles, and livestock. Full facili-
ties for machinery repair and spare parts has been made. Because of the inten-
sity of the proposed operations, further substitution of capital intensive
machinery and labor would not be practicable.



ANNEX 5
Page 7
23.       Forage Production.  Forage production projections are based on a
proposed rotation growing three consecutive crops of improved varieties of
maize and forage sorghum followed by the winter legume berseem (trifolium
alexandrinum). Two hundred percent cropping intensity would be achieved from
year 4. Trials wuld be conducted to find the most suitable tropical pasture
legume both for direct forage and interplanting with maize and sorghum and
would include dolichus lablab and lucerne. Whilst there appear to be no tech-
nical or climatic constraints apart from adequate water, which would prevent
achievement of the proposed crop production, care has been taken to ensure
adequate provision in the cost estimates for full chemical weed and pest con-
trol, and comparatively heavy levels of fertilizer application. Furthermore,
the levels of utilized dry matter production are considered conservative and
should be exceeded with little difficulty.
24.       Utilized annual forage production between 75 and 85% of gross pro-
duction) at full development in years 4 and, including maize and sorghum
silage, and green cure sorghum and berseem, would amount to about 4,400 tons
dry matter (DM) or 17.6 tons per productive hectare - 250 ha. An additional
65 tons DM of feed grain and cottonseed cake would be purchased and fed to
milking cows, young calves, and sheep. About half - 2,200 tons of the total
dry matter production would be conserved as silage stored in open trench silos.
In the first year of operation about 400 tons of the silage DM, or 10% of an-
nual requirements would be withheld from feeding. This would provide a per-
manent revolving feed reserve as an insurance against crop failure, water
shortage, mechanical breakdown, etc. Operating costs could be reduced by
direct grazing of berseem and sorghum by both sheep and cattle. Because of
possible management complications (cattle movement, ditch and soil damage,
grazing management skills), the projections have not allowed for this but
grazing would be included on a trial basis.
25.       Animal Production.  An essential prerequisite for a successful feed-
lot operation is an assured supply of feeder animals. Following a period of
prolonged drought there are signs (very high market prices, reported increased
imports of live animals from Somalia and Ethiopia) that annual offtake from
the national flock and herd may have significantly decreased. Accordingly it
is proposed to establish, as part of the forage farm, a breeding flock of
about 3,000 locally bred ewes. At full production these are expected to yield
about 4,500 weaned lambs each year. It is estimated that about 3,000 would be
sold prime at about 12 weeks of age while the balance after weaning would be
fed for about 100 days. Ewes would be purchased from the traditional markets
and would produce about four crops of lambs (2 each year) before being sold
for slaughter.
26.       Because of the local preference for sheep meat and because more
efficient conversion of forage to meat can be acieved, emphasis would be on
sheep meat production. In addition to sales of 3,000 prime lambs and 1,500
aged ewes, a total of about 13,700 feeder sheep would be sold each year.
Furthermore about 2,000 feeder cattle and about 30 cattle from the small
dairy operation would be sold. Flock and Herd projections are detailed in
Appendix 2, Table 4, and dairy cattle projections in Appendix 1, Table 4.



ANNEX 5
Page 8
Cattle would be purchased at about 200 kg liveweight and after feeding for
120 days sold at about 288 kg a total liveweight gain of 88 kg or an average
of 0.73 kg daily. 1/ Dressing out percentage is expected to improve from 44
to 52 to provide an estimated carcass weight gain of 62 per beast. Feeder
sheep would be purchased at about 22 kg liveweight and after 100 days feeding
sold at 32 kg to show an average daily liveweight gain of 100 gms. Prime
lambs would be sold off mothers at weaning at about 15 kg liveweight, whilst
cull and aged ewes would average about 40 kg having been purchased at about 35
kg. At full production (year 5) total annual sales would amount to 21,200
sheep and 2,030 cattle, to provide an estimated 283 tons incremental meat
production.
27.       Because the consumer market in Yemen does not pay any premium for
quality meat and because of the necessity to use national grain production as
efficiently as possible, no feed grains would be fed to sheep or feeder cattle;
a minimal amount would be fed to dairy cows. The proposed rations would have
sufficient energy density and protein to ensure that the assumed growth rates
would be achieved. The emphasis would be on producting meat and not fat (there-
by maximizing meat production per unit of forage produced) so that feeder
sheep and cattle would be sold in 'forward' and not finished condition. Pro-
vision has been made for some cottonseed cake to be available to breeding
ewes and feeder sheep.
28.       Climatic conditions in the Tihama do not favor milk production from
temperate type dairy cows. Because of the need to increase milk supply in the
area and because of the potential in available land and water resources, a
small 50-cow dairy unit would be included in the proposed forage farm. Cross-
bred (exotic/zebu, friesian-jersey x sahiwal) cows would be introduced. Faci-
lities would not be elaborate and feeding would be on a feed-lot basis. The
milking ration would include about 4% (on ADM basis) of high energy locally
grown feed grains. Some proprietary concentrate ration (about 2.5 tons/annum)
would be fed to dairy replacement calves. At full production (year 5) annual
milk production per cow would average about 3,200 litres and after deductions
for calf consumption annual sales would total about 150,000 litres.
29.       Management and Labor.  An operation of the complexity proposed would
require organizational, managerial and technical skills not now available in
Yemen and foreign experts would be required to fill key posts duaring the
development phase and to give on the job training to local personnel. The
Divisional Manager would be responsible for the overall farm and feed-lot opera-
tion for the first two and half years. In year four he would hand over to an
on-farm trained Yemeni, but would remain as an expert adviser for a further
year. He would be supported by an internationally recruited master mechanic
and an irrigation and crop production specialist for 2 1/2 and ithree years
respectively. Each of these would be responsible for in-service training of
Yemenis. Yemeni technicians would assume full operational management in year
1/   Cattle are expected to show no gains during the first 10 days and to
average 0.8 kg LWC thereafter.



ANNEX 5
Page 9
five. Each would then have had at least one year as acting manager under the
appropriate specialist. Fully developed, the farm would employ a total staff
of about 74.
30.       Marketing and Livestock Procurement.  Under the general direction of
the Divisional Manager, a Yemeni marketing manager together with a senior as-
sistant, would be responsible for coordinating the regular supply of livestock.
Provision has been made for trucks and service vehicles for transportation to
the farm-but this could be supplemented with local carriers if required. An
average of about 340 sheeps and 40 cattles would be needed each week. Live-
stock would be purchased at local markets both in the Tihama and Upland areas.
If necessary, particularly in the initial stages of development some feeder
sheep could be imported from Ethiopia and Somalia at landed prices somewhat
below those currently prevailing in Yemen. Livestock would be sold on the
farm direct to butchers who would be likely to come from throughout the coun-
try. Disribution and slaughter would be coordinated with municipal authorities
operating licensed slaughterhouse facilities. Taking account of the current
apparent shortage of reasonably finished animals in the country, selling is not
likely to be a problem. Weekly sales would average about 400 sheeps and 40
cattles.
31.       Animal Health Control.  Animal Health control would be exercised
through an animal health assistant operating under the guidance and supervi-
sion (through the farm manager) of a fully qualified and experienced regional
veterinarian stationed in Hodeidah. All animals entering the farm would be
quarantined for 10 days and vaccinated against the most important known poten-
tial diseases (rinderpest, foot and mouth disease, anthrax, blackquarter, sheep
pox, caprine pleuro-pneumonia, contagious bovine pleuro-pneumonia) and treated
for both internal and external parasites. Regular spraying against ticks and
lice would be undertaken. Full provision for animal health control including
free access to comprehensive mineral mixtures has been included in the cost
estimates.
32.       Credit and Cash Flow.  Of the total investment, 40% would be contri-
buted as equity and the balance would be financed by a long-term loan through
ACF. During the first four years medium-term loans would be required to fi-
nance operating deficits. This amount would be repaid before long-term loan
repayment commenced in Year 6 and loan repayment would be completed in Year 10.
Financial projections are shown in Appendix 2, Tables 1-5. The loan repayment
schedule has been designed to ensure that the division has adequate working
capital resources at all times. Financial rate of return would be about 12%.
Commercial Poultry and Feed Production
33.       Present Poultry Production.  There is at present no commercial poul-
try production in the country. Chickens are raised by villagers; they scavenge
for food, and produce about 30 eggs per year. Both eggs and meat are sold in
the markets for cash, and only small amounts are consumed by the producers
themselves. The market prices are about YRls 15 for live chickens (weighing



ANNEX 5
Page 10
between 1/2 kg and 1 kg), and YRls 6 for one dozen eggs. Urban demand is sup-
plemented by imported eggs priced at YRls 8 per dozen, and imported frozen
Lickens (up to 1-1/2 kg weight) priced at YRls 24.
34.       Potential.  The potential for commercial poultry production in the
YAR rests upon (a) supplementing imported poultry products with domestic pro-
duction, and (b) expanding the market by eventually lowering the price of
poultry products. It is desirable to increase the protein intake of the
population, and poultry production is an effective means of doing this be-
cause of the relatively favorable feed/meat conversion ratios of poultry
products, and the fact that eggs, sold singly, are affordable protein units
to persons who might not otherwise be able to afford meat products.
35.       Poultry Production to be Financed under the Project.  Bilateral aid
by the Dutch and the U.S. has been committed to developing the commercial poul-
try industry, specifically in the region of Sana'a. The Project would there-
fore plan to develop a poultry unit at Taiz, both to supply that city, and to
be the source of supply of young birds principally to the traditional sector'
in the Taiz area. The planned output of this unit at Taiz would be determined
by the estimated size of the market in the city, and - with respect to supply-
ing stock to the traditinal sector - by the reasonable time span estimated
for establishing flocks in that sector. These estimates are 125,000 broilers
per year to be sold live in Taiz, and the establishment of 500 flocks of 50 birds
each over a five year period in the traditional sector. The projected invest-
ment in such a unit is shown in Appendix 3, Table 4. On-farm investments are
shown in Annex 7, Appendix 2, Tables 1-4. This sum includes the cost of an
expatriate manager who would oversee construction and start up. As a result
of this project, annual production of 125,000 broilers, 4.3 million eggs, and
25,000 culled hens would be generated.
36.       Operations.  Initially, fertile eggs would be purchased to build the
flock, and thereafter a parent fLock would be built up. The birds would be of
a suitable dual purpose breed in\order to satisfy the requirements of both
urban markets and traditional secior producers. While Taiz has a relatively
temperate climate, heating would be provided for brooding, especially as the
January minimum temperature may be as low -100C. The operations would be
labor intensive rather than automated. In addition to the expatriate manager -
who would be employed for the first two years of operation, as well as one
year of construction and start up supervision (he would perform the same
functions for the feed mill); there would be a Yemeni Co-manager, two techni-
cians, and twenty workers. One of the most important operational functions
is disease control, and accepted practice will be adhered to in this respect.
37.       Feed Mill.  An integral part of this poultry unit at Taiz would be a
small feed mill. This mill - the first in the country - would supply the entire
industry, and it is desirable to locate the mill in this Southern part of the
country, near the center of grain sorghum (durra) production. The feed mill
would be a very simple operation, grinding feed grain and mixing with imported
pre-mix. The latter would -include soybean meal, alfalfa meal, minerals and
vitamins. This would avoid the necessity for complex on-site formulations,
which is uneconomic at the low annual volumes forecast for the first years of



ANNEX 5
Page 11
the Yemeni poultry industry. The projected ouput of this feed mill is corre-
lated to the expected needs of the Yemeni poultry industry, which are fore-
cast to rise from 1,400 to 3,000 tons per year over a six year period. The feed
mill capacity would be sufficient to produce the country requirements for dairy
cattle feed supplements.  The investment cost of this feed mill is shownA in
Appendix 3, Table 5. A 12 year loan would be required plus an equity contri-
bution of 40%, of total investment as shown in Appendix 3, Table 3.
38.       Financial Viability of Poultry & Feed Faciliy.  The viability of
the combined poultry and feed-mill unit is dependent upon the maintenance of
an appropriate ratio between feed costs and selling price of poultry products.
It would be important that local grain be purchased, at harvesting time, to
take advantage of lowest prevailing prices. This would be stored, as bagged
product, on the mill premises, or under certain circumstances, in adjacent
public warehousing. The projected rate of return for the poultry unit and
feed mill at Taiz is 13%. This is very sensitive to changes in operating
revenues and costs as is true of poultry operations elsewhere in the world.

ANNEX 6
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
Municipal Slaughterhouse and Retail lMarkets
1.        Present Facilities and Slaughtering Practice.  Facilities for hygenic
slaughter in the Yemen Arab Republic are non-existent. Of the three major
cities, Sana'a, Taiz, and Hodeidah, only latter abattoir has an adequate water
supply. The small abattoirs at Taiz and Hodeidah are little more than concrete
slabs with adjacent walled courtyards. Slaughter at Sana'a takes place on open
ground near to an uncompleted abattoir. Refuse is scavenged by numerous packs
of dogs, and there is no protection from flies, at the existing sites. Further-
more, the meat is transported from the slaughter site to the retail markets
under most unhygenic conditions, generally wrapped in its hide or skin, and
thrown into a dirty truck or on a motorcycle.
2.        Present Practices in Meat and Fish Retailing.  Sana'a has four meat
markets (two municipal, 1 Wafq, and 1 private) with a total of over 100 meat
stalls plus an unknown number of meat vendors who squat in courtyards. Fish
is said to be offered intermittently, and to be sold immediately. The two
municipal markets are in reasonable structural condition; the Wafq market is
old and congested; the condition of the private market is unknown. In
Hodeidah, fish is more important than in Sana'a. There appears to be only
14 meat stall vendors (it should be noted that home slaughter is common in
lUodeidah).  The present central location is rather distant (3 km) from the
town outskirts. In Taiz, the number of meat vendors is more than in Hodeidah
and considerably less than in Sana'a. The five locations could be reduced to
two.
3.        Most facilities are too small and too crowded, they generally lack:
(a) screening from flies
(b)  piped, potable water, and wash basin in each stall
(c)  washable surfaces for cutting and selling
(d) prohibition of courtyard vendors, and general licensing system
to prevent other non-inspected sales points
(e) effective health inspection
(f) acceptable refuse disposal.



ANNEX 6
Page 2
4.        Organization.  Abattoirs and retail meat and fish markets are under
the jurisdiction of the Ministry of Municipalities, the PU would establish a
MIunicipality Slaughterhouse and Mtarkets Division (MSMD) to construct the
slaughterhouses and retail markets in Sana'a, Taiz and Hodeidah included in
the proposed project. MSID would operate these facilities on a commercially
sound basis. It would provide slaughtering facilities, delivery service and
market stalls to facilitate health control as well as orderly marketing.
Services charges would be levied to cover operating costs, depreciation and
financial expenses (Tables 1, 2 and 3).
5.        With respect to public health, an important aim of the project com-
ponent is to control sanitary conditions from slaughter to retail sale. Ac-
cordingly, similar standards of inspection would be introduced and enforced
in the new structures whose available facilities would make sanitary practices
possible and the municipalities would control conveyance of meat from abattoir
to the markets. The Ministry of Municipalities would introduce and enforce
regulations preventing slaughter of livestock for trade outside the approved
slaughterhouses in Sana'a, Taiz and Hodeidah. A uniform system of vendor
licensing would be adapted for controlling retail meat and fish marketing.
6.        The new municipal abbatoirs would be organized in the manner found
effective in many other countries:
(a)  The abattoirs would employ their own butchers directly, but
would not buy and sell animals for their own account.
(b) Slaughter fees would be set by the Ministry of Municipalities
which would relate both to the cost of operating the facilities,
as well as the current level of such fees.
(c) Hides and skins would be purchased from the owners, the cost of
which would, in effect, be a credit against slaughter fees.
(d) No meat grading system would be initiated in the immediate
future, as it is judged too early for such a scheme in the
Yemen.
(e) Livestock would be deliveed to the abattoir by the owners accord-
ing to their assessment of the following day's market demands.
The livestock would be inspected and rested overnight prior to
slaughter the following morning.
(f) Slaughter would commence very early in the morning so that meat
could be delivered to retail markets for sale during the morning
hours.
(g)  The Hodeidah and Taiz abattoirs would not have chill rooms,
but the Sana'a abattoir does have a chill room and thus would
be enabled to carry over some carcasses. The butcher shop
improvement loans (Annex 7), would provide butchers with small
freezer units, so that they could carry unsold meat.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
7.        MSMD with the assistance of the PU would prepare grant applica-
tions for the subprojects that would include detailed investment costs,
projected income and cash flow statements and economic analysis and submit
them to ACF. Upon approval, ACF would provide proceeds from a Government
grant to implement the subprojects. After year 6, the PU would pass the
control of MSMD to the Ministry of Municipalities.
8.        Abattoirs to be built under the Project.  The project provides for
new abattoirs at Taiz and Hodeidah at a cost of YRls 2.2 million each (Table
5) and the completion of the Sana'a abattoir at a cost of YRls 1.2 million.
The proposed abattoirs in Taiz and Hodeidah would be simple enclosed plants,
with gravity on-the-rail dressing, and provision for hot, potable water under
pressure, a hide shed, and sewage treatment. Capacity would be 15 cattle and
50 smallstock per hour (Sana'a), and 10 cattle and 25 smallstock per hour
(Taiz and Hodeidah). The equipment would be simple to operate and maintain.
There would be no cooler, since fresh carcasses would be moved to the retail
market the morning of slaughter. The processing of by-products is not envi-
saged because in YAR virtually all parts of the animal are consumed. Com-
pletion of the abattoir at Sana'a is feasible and desirable as an alternative
to building a newly designed unit. This installation was financed jointly by
the UNDP and the YAR, but delays have been caused by problems with contrac-
tors, and with shortage of funds on the part of the Ministry of 'Municipalities.
With improved inspection, and the use of elementary sanitary practices, these
plants would deliver cleaner meat to the urban retail markets with consequent
benefits to the public health. In addition, the treatment of hides would be
improved, with a net benefit to the economy.
9.        Retail Markets to be built under the Project.  Recognizing the com-
plexity of retail marketing, the project proposes to initiate improved prac-
tices by financing the construction of 154 retail stalls appropriately distri-
buted in the three urban centers. The model (Table 6) for these retail mar-
kets is based on 28 fronted stalls of 9m2 each, with a covered extension of
an additional 4-1/2m2, and a central, drained courtyard. Investment cost per
stall is estimated shown in Table 6. Such a facility is relative easy to con-
struct, but attention would be paid to the following factors:
(a) location with respect to existing and future markets for
optimum consumer convenience
(b) overall future needs by city, with respect to space and
number of vendors
(c) revised stall rental rates to cover operating costs of
improved facilities.
10.       Total Investment Costs and Rate of Return.  A Government grant
(Table 4) would be provided to finance the construction of two new abattoirs,



ANNEX 6
Page 4
the completion of a third, and 154 new retail meat and fish stalls as well as
the divisional headquarters cost. The financial rate of return for the divi-
si~n is projected at 13%.
11.       Technical Assistance.  Construction and start-up supervision, as
well as management and training for the first years of operation, would be
undertaken by an expatriate, whose cost is carried in the MSMD Budget. Con-
struction time for each abattoir would be one year, and the three abattoirs
would be. completed over a three year phased period. An expatriate Hides and
Skins Specialist would also be retained for two years. The Hides and Skins
Specialist would in addition to training abattoir management and employees in
techniques for improved flaying followed by proper working and diry-salting
of hides and skins. In addition, this expert would conduct demonstrations
in rural communities on sound flaying methods, carcass dressing and dry-
salting processing of hides and skins. Provisions are included in this com-
ponent for training slaughtermen in livestock slaughtering techniques. Also
included is supplementary foreign training for YAR managers in abattoir ope-
rations in Middle East/East Africa country. Training provisions are in-
cluded for training a minimum of eight YAR meat inspectors in a Middle East/
East African country with similar livestock and conditions (Appendix 4,
Table 1).

APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
Supervised Credit
Landowner and Tenant Farmer Loans
1.        The Traditional Sector.  The nature and structure of the agriculture
sector have remained basically unchanged for centuries. A major constraint
to agricultural development is a deeply conservative rural population with
complex socio-economic and political institutions associated with the power
structure inherent in the centuries-old tribal society and resulting land
tenure arrangements. Agriculture remains almost entirely subsistence and
over 90% of the cropped area is devoted to sorghum, maize, and millet, yield-
ing an average 800 kg/ha. Less than 10% of the total cropped area is irri-
gated, mostly from spate flooding.
2.        Most agricultural land is privately owned.  Accordingly to the
area, between 5 and 15% is Waqf or state land. The percentage of the 3
types of farmers--owners, owner/tenants, tenants--varies considerably depend-
ing on locality. There are, however, a high percentage of tenant farmers,
most of whom farm small parcels of land varying from 0.3 to 6 ha. In some
cases individual holdings may be larger. Tenancies are on a year-to-year
basis but according to custom, security of tenure is more real than it ap-
pears. Government levies a 10% production tax (Zakat) on agricultural crops.
(Each party pays his own Zakat according to the proportion of crop production
received). The most frequent crop sharing pattern is 50% to the landowner
with the latter's share increasing up to 75% in the case of investment in ir-
rigation and tree crops. Usually, the tenant bears all costs of agricultural
inputs such as seeds, fertilizers, labor and draft power, although the owner
may share seasonal labor costs, and the share of other costs may be adjusted
to the proportion of the crop each party receives. Use of modern production
inputs is negligible. Farmers, however, are industrious and given the con-
straints of their resources, skilled. Farmers are prepared to accept new
methods provided their value has been clearly demonstrated. Except for the
excellent system of soil conservation and preserving rainfall for productive
use the level of agricultural technology is low.
3.        Livestock are largely geared to meet the needs of agricultural pro-
duction which relies almost entirely on animal power, and to rural transport
requirements which are still best supplied by camels and donkeys. Production
is low; about 250 kg milk/lactation every 2 years; 0.5-1 kg poor quality
wool (from the wool sheep); low calving rates; slow growth rates, and high
mortality from disease and parasites. Sheep and goat reproduction rates from
2 breeding seasons, are said to be as high as 200% but severe mortality



ANNEX 7
Page 2
amongst lambs due to disease, parasites, and unreliable feed supplies, great-
ly reduces this high potential production. Poultry exist entirely as
scavengers and are seldom fed; egg production would not exceed 30-40/year
and disease is rampant. Livestock are grazed on usually overgrazed village
communal rangelands. Sorghum and millet stover are preserved for use as dry
season feed. Lucerne is grown over a wide area but only where water and sub-
sistence is reliable. An important feature of animal husbandry is the care
and attention devoted to draft animals which receive priority treatment and
are usually in good condition. The main constraints to increasing livestock
production can be summarized as follows:
(a) widespread disease and parasite problems
(b) low agricultural crop production which prevents farmers from
actively pursuing livestock production safe from the risk of
being caught with inadequate food for family requirements
(c) inadequate and unreliable feed supplies
(d) lack of adequate water
(e) absence of seasonal and long-term credit for programs designed
to increase livestock production
(f) lack of trained extension services
4.        The Project.  The Project Unit in cooperation with ACF would com-
mence a supervised credit program to assist small sheep, goat, and dairy
farmers, and introduce small-scale commercial poultry production in the tra-
ditional sector. Recognizing that livestock production cannot be treated in
isolation in a subsistence agriculture the proposed credit program would be
an integrated package involving:
(a) provision of adequate water for irrigation by way of tube or
dug wells;
(b) increased agricultural crop production by introduction of
improved seed varieties, proper chemical fertilizer and
pest control, diversification into high yielding crops such as
maize, and the introduction on a regular basis, of a legume
into the rotation;
(c) provision of reliable, quality animal forage by the introduc-
tion of lucerne into a permanent crop rotation;
(d) the supply of improved dairy cows, whilst at the same time
recognizing the need to maintain the local zebu breed for
draft purposes;



ANNEX 7
Page 3
(e)  the integration of milk production with an organized marketing
system;
(f)  the further strengthening of on-farm economy by the introduc-
tion of quick yielding investments in the form of small-scale
poultry production with improved breeds and a back-up supply of
prepared balanced rations; and
(g) the provision of back-up technical and extension services.
5.        On-Farm Development.  The project would assist participating land-
owners, either individually or as groups to install tube or dug wells and as-
sociated irrigation works to provide reliable irrigation for tenants. These
loans would be made in conjunction with tenant farmer loans for seasonal crop
inputs, animal health control expenses, and improved dairy cows. Thus a
joint development approach using the existing pattern of landholdings and
preserving the local social structure would result. An area of 20 ha held
by one landowner and operated by 8 tenant farmers each with 2.5 ha of pro-
ductive land has been selected as a model. Most farmers keep 1-3 cows, the
main purpose of which is to provide a continuing supply of draft oxen and
some milk and meat. In addition a small mixed flock of sheep and goats is
usually kept. Priority for the usually inadequate forage supplies is given
first to the oxen and secondly to the milking cows. Sorghum is the only
crop grown. Although the model development program is centered around dairy,
poultry, and sheep/goat production, flexibility would be exercised so that
cattle fattening loans could be made where it was shown to be technically
and economically viable. Each 2.5 ha units would maintain 1 ha of sorghum,
but introduce 1 ha of maize and 1/2 ha of lucerne. Improved seeds, fertil-
izer, pest control and provision of reliable irrigation water, would be part
of the technological package involving the integration of agricultural and
livestock production. Using the farmer's skills in feeding and handling
cattle, two improved dairy cows would be introduced. One local cow and
bull/oxen would be retained, as the objective of introducing new breeds would
be to improve meat and milk supply and not change the draft power cattle.
At the same time a full animal health control program and improved nutrition
would result in substantially increased production from the sheep and goat
flock.
6.        Project Production.  Pre-project grain production of about 2.25
tons would be maintained on the reduced area of 1 ha. In addition, 3.5 tons
of maize, 40 tons of green lucerne forage, and 6-7 tons of maize stover would
be produced. Production increases would be phased over a 3 year period (see
Tables 4-6, Appendix 1, Annex 7). Milk production would increase from a
pre-project level of about 250 litres to 4,500 litres from 2 improved and
1 local cow. Pre-project sheep/goat numbers are assumed to be about 18, in-
cluding 11 mature animals. Over a 5-year period, the flock on hand would in-
crease to 25, including 11 mature animals. Of these 8, or 73% would be breed-
ing ewes, compared to 45% pre-project. Annual sales would increase from 3 to
12 over a 5 year period. Incremental meat production would be about 400 kg/o
annum (see Table 7).



ANNEX 7
Page 4
7.        Investment and Credit Program.  Total landowner investment is shown
in Table ID.  Subloan repayment (80% of total investment) would commence at
the end of Year 3 and be completed over a 4-year period. Subloan agreements
and development plans would require the landowner to guarantee the tenant
loan and tenancy for at least the period of the loan. This would mean that
each group of tenants jointly and severally guaranteed each loan and the
annual repayments. Furthermore, LDC in conjunction with ACF would examine the
feasibility of establishing an insurance fund to which all borrower would
automatically contribute and which would specifically cover the improved cows
and poultry during the period of loan repayment. All loans would be channel-
led through ACF which would be assisted by the PU in the preparation of farm
development plans, and in providing back up technical support and loan super-
vision. Over a 6 year period about 24 landowner loans and some 375 tenants
loans for livestock would be made. A further 500 loans would be made for
poultry. Each tenant would invest over a two year period of which 20% would
be his contribution and 80% would be provided by long-term ACE loan at   %
interest. Included would be provisions for incremental working capital.
Subloan repayment would commence at the end of Year 4 and be completed over
a 4 year period for a term of 8 years.
8.        Net Income and Cash Flow.  Landowner pre-project net income after
payment of Zakat is shown in Table IA. Full development would be in Year 3,
and loan repayment completed in Year 6. The financial rate of return would
be about 30%. After payment of Zakat, increased rental, and non-family labor,
maximum post-development net income would be achieved in Year 6. Furthermore,
this increase in net income would be after allowance for about five times in-
crease in the value of family subsistence. During the first three years net
cash flow is zero although family subsistence would increase about five times.
Loan repayment would be completed in Year 8 after a 4 year grace period. Fi-
nancial rate of return is estimated to be 25%. See Table 4 and Table 5 of
Appendix 1 for details of operating income and projected cash flow.
Smallholder Egg Production
9.        The Project.  The productivity of village poultry is low in the
Yemen, the birds producing an estimated 30 eggs per year. The potential
exists for increasing yields five-fold by introducing improved breeds, provid-
ing access to feed, and the application of husbandry practices. As a begin-
ning, it is proposed to finance 500 flocks of 50 birds each over a four year
period in the region of Taiz. The ouitput from such a flock, after due allow-
ance for losses, would be a net cash income per year as shown in Appendix 2,
Table 1, in addition to the estimated value of family consumption shown.
This represents a significant addition to smallholder income and nutrition.
The farmer would purchase three week old chicks fully vaccinated, from Taiz,
and feed them for approximately 17 weeks, when they would commence laying.
Egg production is estimated at 180 eggs per bird; as well as an edible carcass
after 70 weeks. Feed would be purchased from the feed mill at Taiz, and would
be delivered periodically. Extension training would be given in poultry man-
agement, with emphasis on health aspects, and feeding rates.



ANNEX 7
Page 5
10.       Loan Requirements.  The investment per smallholder would comprise
poultry houses and equipment as well as an initial "feed bank" to cover the
period before pullets begin to lay (Table 3). A seven year loan would be
e.  nded, amounting to 80% of investment commencing in the second year.  Re-
turii on investment is estimated at 17%. It is estimated that the number of
loans taken up over four years would be 60, 100, 150 and 190.
Butcher Shop Improvement Loan
11.       Financing under the project of retail meat markets (Annex 6), makes
it desirable that provision be made for associated small loans to butchers
who would rent stalls in such markets. The investment per butcher is shown
in Table 3, of which a freezer unit would be a major item. A loan of 80% in-
vestment would be extended for three years. It is contemplated that 84 such
loans would be made. The purpose of the freezer unit is to enable the vendor
to carry over unsold meat until the following day in good condition. The
present system is for unsold meat to be resold to other vendors who sell the
spoiled portions at reduced prices. The balance of the loan is for tools
of the trade. Eventually it is expected that use of freezers arLd adequate
tools of the trade will be general in the retailing of meat (including poul-
try) and fish, and that these loans will encourage that trend.

ANNEX 8
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
Animal Health
1.        BACKGROUND.  Until recent years there were no trained veterinarians
and no animal health control service in YAR. Inadequate control of animal
disease is a severe constraint on the development of the livestock subsector.
Almost all the important major diseases and animal parasites are thought to
be present in greater or less degree. Epidemic diseases, such as rinderpest
and newcastle disease, periodically devastate the ruminant and poultry popula-
tions, respectively. A wide range of endemic diseases, including parasite
infestations, pox and skin diseases, and foot and mouth disease, result in
high livestock mortality and general economic loss. Disease and parasites
greatly compound the effects of inadequate nutrition and the ravages of
drought (and vice versa). Improved road communication has resulted in more
speedy movement of livestock throughout the country thereby increasing the
possibility for rapid spread of epidemic diseases, such as rinderpest. With-
out quarantine facilities and adequate staff, increasing imports of live
animals from neighboring African countries presents a continuing danger.
2.        In 1972, under a bilateral aid agreement with U.K. technical assist-
ance (ODM), a small veterinary team was established in the Ministry of Agri-
culture to investigate the animal disease position and to form the basis of
a national animal health service. A small diagnostic laboratory is operated
in Sana' and limited vaccination and curative campaigns have been undertaken.
Farmer response to the services has been encouraging. The current ODM pro-
fessional team of two veterinarians and one diagnostic officer together with
Yemeni counterparts and supporting staff is not ony unable to cDpe with cur-
rent demands but is inadequate in terms of providing the basis of a worth-
while animal health service. Under a new agreement the ODM Veterinary project
is to be substantially expanded. The Livestock Credit and Processing Project
proposes to assist with further essential expansion.
3.        The Project.  Over a six year development period, it is proposed to
finance the facilities and staffing required by the Directorate of Animal
Health to establish a small but comprehensive field service, backed by ade-
quate diagnostic facilities and veterinary investigation staff. A small head-
quarters exists and both the Director of Animal Health and the project manager
(ODM) have been appointed. These officials would be responsible for the
strengthening of the administrative framework of the Directorate, and for
advising on policy and planning including the drafting of appropriate animal
health control regulations. They would also supervise the construction and be
responsible for organizing both in-country and overseas training of veterinar-
ians and veterinary assistants. Diagnostic laboratory facilities in Sana'a



ANNEX 8
Page 2
would be enlarged and an animal clinic, stores and garages built. Three re-
gional veterinary centers with offices, stores and associated buildings would
be established in Taiz, Sana'a, and Hodeidah. These would be headed by three
senior veterinarians (two ODM, one Project financed) and assisted by five
associate experts, and Yemeni counterparts and assistants. A cadre of vet-
erinary scouts would be trained to undertake field assignments. The centers,
including the diagnostic laboratory would be fully equipped with veterinary
supplies, vaccines, veterinary equipment, and vehicles, including 4-wheel
drive (complete with field equipment) and motorcycles. In addition to the
primary duties of regional disease control, the centers would provide vete-
rinary services to project-related enterprises including the livestock
finishing farm, dairy farms and associated milk colonies, range improve-
ment centers, village development associations, and the poultry complex.
To reduce the danger of importing contagious disease, a quarantine station
would be built at Hodeidah, the principal port. It would be small and un-
sophisticated, but capable of expansion at the appropriate time.
4.        Management and Training.  The leader of the U.K. aid team would
act as project director for five years, the projected period of current ODM
commitment. He would, along with the Director of Animal Heath, coordinate
with the project manager on all project related matters. From year five the
Director of Animal Health would be a fully trained Yemen veterinarian who
would assume control of the animal health subproject. Funds for training
counterpart personnel would constitute a most important aspect. During early
years, the high ratio of expatriate support would facilitate in-service train-
ing. A full-time expatriate training officer would be provided for a period
of five years. Furthermore, the UNDP/FAO training school at Taiz, which is
already assisting SURDUP, has agreed to introduce an animal health and husban-
dry course suitable for veterinary assistants and scouts. The Project
Manager would assist with preparation of a suitable curriculum, and in addi-
tion to direct project financial assistance; the Regional Veterinary Officer,
and the SURDUP Veterinarian and Livestock Production Specialist, would assist
with course implementation. Furthermore, overseas training would be provided
as follows:
(a) subject to final approval of the Project Manager and Director
of Animal Health, three graduate veterinarians would complete
one-year graduate courses in veterinary medicine;
(b) a total of 36 man-years in a full six-year veterinary medicine
course would be provided. By project Year Six, four graduates
would be available followed by an additional four over the next
five years;
(c) following successful completion of two years in-service training,
twenty veterinary assistants would undertake six-month courses
in disease control measures and field practice in Syria, the
Sudan or Egypt.



ANNEX 8
Page 3
Flexibility would be maintained in regard to the number undertaking graduate
and post-graduate training. Because of the urgent need for trained Yemen
staff, greater emphasis may be placed on sub-professional overseas study
(2-3 years). More sophisticated study and training could be introduced at a
later stage when it is likely to be of more value to the country. At the
end of the six year disbursement period, Yemen personnel would have assumed
the positions previously held by expatriates, with the exception of one
veterinary officer and two laboratory technicians.
5.        Cost Estimates.  Full details set out in Tables 1 and 2 are sum-
marized as follows:

ANNEX 9
