APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
Livestock and Range Improvement
1.        Background.  The basic conflict between individual ownership of
livestock and community ownership and uncontrolled use of rangelands has
resulted in severe overgrazing and depletion of natural pastures. Live-
stock producers face severe problems in improving herd and flock productivity
on poor natural pastures and with crop residues low in both energy and pro-
tein. With controlled improvement and management program, the low rainfall
in important livestock areas, coupled with intense pressure for the use of
land from agricultural activities as well as livestock, makes productive
regeneration of natural pasture an impossible task. Furthermore, range im-
provement, like all aspects of rural development cannot be undertaken in
isolation, but must be part of a wider program designed to meet the overall
needs of the people. It should also, wherever possible be based on're-
search results and practical experience. Little is known of the tech-
niques and technology necessary to bring about signficant productive im-
provement of natural pastures under the widely varying climatic and
ecological conditions in YAR. Similarly, although indigenous sheep, goats
and cattle are very well adapted to the local environment their capacity
to respond to improved management and nutrition is not really known. The
exceptional quality of many work bulls/oxen, sheep and goats seen in the
country indicates that good potential responses could be obtained both to
nutrition and genetic upgrading.
2.        Forestry development can be complementary to rangeland improvement.
Firewood is an important source of energy over most of the country, partic-
ularly in rural areas. Almost all building timber, even of the most rudi-
mentary kind must be imported. Natural bush and forests, already severely
depleted face further devastation as rapidly rising population escalates de-
mand for more energy. Preliminary work being undertaken in UNDP/FAO Projects
in Taiz with quick yielding trees combined with range spelling is promising
and is worthy of wider application and detailed investigation.
3.        The Project.  In conjunction with the ODM Montane Plains and Wadi
Rima (MDWR) Program, an Agricultural Improvement Station with a Range Improve-
ment component would be established near Damar in the central Montane Plain
area. Furthermore, as part of the range improvement studies, 10 pilot Village
Development Associations (VDA) would be established. The knowledge and ex-
perience gained as a result of the work undertaken would provide a valuable
base for expanded second phase development.



ANNEX 9
Page 2
4.        Damar Agricultural Improvement Center.  A part of British bilateral
aid to YAR, a comprehensive survey is being made of the Central Montane Plains
and Wadi Rima. The objective of the survey, which includes a wide range of
ground water studies, is to identify priority avenues for agricultural develop-
ment and recommend suitable programs for implementation. An expanded program
is to include the establishment of an Agricultural Improvement Center (AIC)
which would involve both agricultural research and extension. It is proposed
that the AIC would inter alia experiment with new crops, new varieties of
existing crops, water use, crop husbandry techniques, and the economic res-
ponses to various agricultural inputs such as fertilizers, pesticides, etc.
Work would also be carried out on methods of improving production from rain-
fed land. Originally the program called for a small livestock and associated
range improvement component which would examine forage crops, draft power
husbandry and means of improving communal grazing areas, including the estab-
lishment of browse shrubs. An important function of the AIC would be to
identify, train and work through suitable Yemen extension agents. The total
ODM project including investment, expert and recurrent costs would amount to
some YRls 5.2 million over a five year period.
5.        Project Development.  In order to avoid duplication and to take
advantage of the background already built up by the ODM team, the Livestock
Credit Processing Project would provide for an expansion of the Livestock and
Range Improvement component of the proposed AIC. Total investment cost is
shown in Table 1 and would be phased over four years. Investmient would in-
clude buildings (office, store, housing), vehicles and equipment, on-farm
development and livestock. A 5% physical contingency would be provided.
In addition recurrent costs over a six year period would be incurred (see
Table 11). Under the project two or three representative areas (about 200
ha in total) of rangeland would be fenced, stocked with local sheep, and a
pre-arranged program of improvement undertaken. The objective would be to
determine as quickly as possible the pasture species and management tech-
niques required to improve rangeland pasture productivity responses and
changes under a grazing regime. Furthermore, a wide range of browse species
would be introduced with a view to determining those most suitable for estab-
lishing in a number of ecological zones. The emphasis would bea a simple ap-
plied research which would lead to the evolution of practice techniques and
management systems suitable for application under village conditions.
6.        In addition to the ODM expatriate staff who would cover a wider
range of disciplines, the project would provide two specialists in the fields
of livestock production and range improvement. The actual fields of special-
ization would be finally decided by the Project Manager after consultation
with the ODM Project Director and the Minister of Agriculture. These spe-
cialist would be integrated into the ODM team and be responsib:Le to the Proj-
ectg Manager through the ODM Project Director. A prime responsibility would
be the training of Yemeni technicians and extension agents and to assist with
the formation of VDA's and implementation of the proposed development programs.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
7.        Village Development Associations.  As a pilot project designed to
accumulate experience and background for wider scale second phase development,
and to serve as an integral extension of the range improvement research pro-
gram, 10 VDA's would be formed. Each VDA would involve a package program of
communal grazing control, integration of forestry, grazing and natural pasture
improvement, formation and operation of small village associations or coop-
eratives, provision of credit, livestock improvement and animal health control,
and extension. Each VDA would consist of about five subvillages. About 125 ha
(25 ha per subvillage--no more than 1/3 of total village grazing land) of com-
munal grazing land would be fenced and planted with about 20,000 trees, half
of which would be browse shrubs. In addition the natural pasture would be
oversown with suitable grass and legume seeds. Timber seedlings would be
obtained from either the UNDP/FAO nursery at Taiz or the Directorate of
Forestry, Ministry of Agriculture. Seeds for browse shrubs and pasture
would be imported. Livestock would be kept out of the enclosed areas for at
least 12 months and then reintroduced on a controlled basis. Simultaneously
a comprehensive animal health program would be introduced and villagers
encouraged to reduce the number of head being carried. Livestock would
remain privately owned.  They would, however, be grazed under community con-
trolled management. Owners would pay a fixed grazing fee based on headage.
Benefits of increased productivity would accrue to individuals.
8.        Where possible, VDA development would coincide with other project
inputs such as credit for livestock, improved seeds, irrigation, etc. Close
cooperation with SURDEP, TDA and other development programs would also be
practiced. In such cases totally integrated village development involving
improved crop production, provision of village water supplies, expanded
irrigation and improvement of feeder roads, etc. would be put into practice.
The complex problem of improvement to village community grazing lands in-
volving a fundamental change in established custom would then be 'Linked to
popular more readily accepted development.
9.        Project Cost, Credit and Cash Flow.  Investment costs, spread over
three years, covering fencing, seedling trees, planting, and pasture seeds
are shown in Table 4; 20% would be contributed by villagers mainly as labor
and kind. The balance would be financed by ACF on a long-term basis. In-
cremental working capital required during the first six years would be con-
tributed by villagers. Livestock owners would pay an annual grazing fee of
YRIs 8 per head of livestock. Sales of timber (poles) and firewood would
commence in year 7. Long-term subloan repayments would commence in year 7
and be completed in year 11. Details of projected income, cash flow, invest-
ment, and flock, are given in Annex 9, Table 3, 4, 5 and 6 respectively. Fi-
nancial rate of return would be about 10%. The first VDA would be established
in year 1 followed by three in year 3 and six in year 4.
10.       Project Benefits.  Carrying capacity of the enclosed area would
triple over a six year period. Assuming an initial village flock of 229 (23
AU) numbers would initially be decreased and then steadily increased to reach
a stable maximum of about 450 (42 AU). Flock productivity would improve with



ANNEX 9
Page 4
lower mortality, and annual sales improved as shown. Supply oif low grade
building poles (used extensively in house construction) would be substantially
improved as would the availability of firewood. More importanlly this experi-
ment in village cooperation could provide the catalyst needed for improvement
of the potentially valuable natural pastures of YAR.

YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
Staff Recruitment & Consultant Service - Duties and Responsibilities
Project Staff
1.        The provision of foreign specialists and associate expert staff
for all positions except the senior veterinary officer and those furnished
by ODM, will be furnished by an experienced agricultural management and
consulting company, internationally selected according to IDA. guidelines,
that would supply all personnel through a packaged team, with the consulting
company assuming full responsibility for selection, replacement and salaries
(for each post, Government would require a candidate selection and approval
system). This Company,Hendrikson Associates, has ben selected.
Expatriate staff should have a knowledge of Arabic and English languages.
Positions where Arabic is considered essential are specified in the following
detailed job descriptions.
Project Headquarters
Project Manager
2.        The Project Manager would be required for five years.  He would be
recruited internationally, be senior in nature and have a broad professional
experience with the following specific qualifications:
(a) university degree or its equivalent in Agricultural Science,
preferably with a livestock-farm management, agribusiness
management bias;
(b) experience in the start-up and management of national level
livestock credit and development projects in developing countries;
(c) at least 10 years field experience of which at least 5 years would
have been in livestock and agricultural crop production;
(d) a proven ability in organization and management and experience with
staff management;



AN'NEX 10
Page 2
(e) knowledge of Arabic is considered essential.
Duties and Responsibilities
(a) directly responsible to the chairman of the Livestock Development
Board (Ministry of Agriculture) for implementation of all compo-
nents of the project in accordance with policies laid down by
the Board (LDB).
(b) advise LDB and appropriate Government institutions on the terms,
conditions and qualification of employment of all staff (foreign
and local);
(c) recommend employment of such staff as he considers necessary
to carry out the project and in accordance with the final agree-
ment with Government.
(d) define the duties and responsibilities of the staff, train them
and supervise their work;
(e) assist the various project related Ministries and institutions
in selection of the project staff for overseas and local training
and arrange such training in consultation with the appropriate
training organizations;
(f) with the Livestock Development Corporation Manager, assist all
departments and institutions in the formation of their respective
project subcomponent;
(g) recommend to the LDB any adjustments to the project implementation
schedule which may occur for technical or economic reasons;
(h) assisted by the Financial Assistant, introduce an accounting and
auditing system which will be acceptable to Central Government
auditor, and coordinated with the accounts of participating in-
stitutions;
(i) training of Yemeni staff in project organization and management.
Livestock Development Corporation (LDC) - General Manager
3.        The LDC General Manager would be required for five years and have
the following qualifications and experience:
(a) a university degree in Agricultural Economics, Agricultural
Science, Agricultural Commerce or its equivalent;



ANNEX 10
Page 3
(b) a broad professional experience of at least 10 years in agri-
culture or related sectors in agriculture or related sectors,
part of which should have been spent in a developing country;
(c) a thorough knowledge of the principles and practices of economic
dairy, beef, sheep and if possible poultry production, and farm
management;
(d) a proven ability to organize and to handle staff;
(e) a knowledge of Arabic would be desirable but not essential.
Duties and Responsibilities
(a) be directly responsible to the Project Manager and under him
assume responsibility for the establishment and initial manage-
ment of the Dairy, Poultry and Feed, and Livestock Finishing
Divisions of LDC;
(b) advise the Project Manager on the selection and recruitment of
local technical staff required for Project implementation;
(c) prepare suitable loan applications supported by farm development
plans, cost estimates, cash flow and economic evaluation, for
submission to ACF so as to facilitate the financing of the devel-
opment farms and other components under his supervision;
(d) coordinate and supervise the duties and responsibilities of the
LDC Divisional Managers and their counterpart staff;
(e)  in conjunction with the Project Manager and the Financial Assistant
establish and supervise the maintenance of suitable records and
financial accounts for all development components under his
supervision;
(f) advise the Project Manager on, and assist with, the procurement
of all materials, equipment, machinery livestock and other re-
quisites necessary for Project implementation;
(g) train a Yemeni successor to assume his responsibilit:ies at the
termination of the LDC Manager's contract and ensure the same
process with all expatriate personnel within LDC.
4.   Financial Assistant
The Financial Assistant would be:
(a) a fully qualified accountant with experience in developing coun-
tries and in all aspects of accounting and auditing procedures
at both government and corporate levels;



ANNEX 10
Page 4
(b) preferably experienced in Arab countries with some language
competency in order to adapt financial procedures to particular
systems and at the same time to train a locally recruited counter-
part and subordinate staff in those procedures;
(c) directly responsible to the Project Manager for the financial
organization of the Project;
(d) design and incorporate a system of accounting and audit for
consolidated accounts, including the coordinated design and
implementation of subproject accounts and be responsible for
all disbursement and reimbursement procedures of Government,
IDA and Kuwait Fund loan funds.
(e) ensure the adequate training of all Yemeni staff under his
control and in particular that of his own Yemeni counterpart
who would ultimately replace him.
5.   Dairy Production Division Manager would be:
(a) a university graduate or diplomate in Agriculture, Animal
Husbandry, Farm Management, or Agronomy, with at least
8 years experience in practical commercial farm operation
part of which must have been dairy. Experience in a devel-
oping country and a working knowledge of Arabic would be
essential;
(b) assume responsibility for the operation of the Dairy Production
Division including the commercial dairy farms and milk colonies,
and staff training; assist with the preparation of farm develop-
ment plans and associated cost estimates and ACF loan applica-
tions; assist with milk colony supplier farm selection and co-
ordinate with other Divisional Managers, assume responsibility
for the health, production and development of the dairy herd;
by teaching and example, create a team of skilled Yemeni cowmen.
He would need to have particular ability to impart experience and
teach practical and visual skills.
Livestock Finishing Division
6.   Livestock Finishing Division Manager would be:
(a) be a university graduate or diplomate in agriculture with at least
5 years experience in practital irrigation, fodder production and
livestock fattening, part of which would have been acquired in
developing countries; a working knowledge of Arabic would be
desirable; and



ANNEX 10
Page 5
(b) assume responsibility for the physical and financial control
of the Livestock Finishing Division, including fodd[er produc-
tion and livestock management, procurement and sales, farm
development planning and ACF loan application preparation,
staff training; and coordinate with other Divisional Managers
and Project staff.
7.   Master Mechanic would:
(a)  posses a qualified "A" grade mechanics certificate or its equiva-
lent, have a minimum of 5 years experience in the repair and
maintenance of agricultural machinery, and wide experience in
the operation and management of agricultural machinery partic-
ularly that related to forage crop production and conservation,
a demonstrated ability to manage labor and trained technical
staff, an excellent knowledge of Arabic and at least a working
knowledge of English; and
(b) be responsible to the Divisional Manager for the overall mecha-
nical management, maintenance and repair of machinery, tractors,
and pumps; staff training and supervision; advise on the procure-
ment, type, operation and management of the farm machinery, and
establish and supervise the operation of the machinery repair
shop, including a spare parts section with a system for sustain-
ing the inventory.
8.   Irrigation - Crop Manager would:
(a) be a tertiary diploma holder in agriculture, agronomy, or irriga-
tion with at least 5 years practical experience in irrigated crop
production part of which must have been in a tropical envi-
ronment and a developing country; have demonstrated ability
to manage labor and trained technical staff; an excellent
knowledge of Arabic and a working knowledge of English; and
(b)  be responsible to the Divisional Manager for overall management
of crop production and conservation including the preparation of
a schedule of irrigation operations in accordance with crop and
soil requirement; organization and supervision of irrigation crews
and machinery operation; crop husbandry including weed and pest
control; coordination of forage supplies with animal requirements;
and training of Yemeni staff.
Poultry-Feed Mill Division
9.        Poultry-Feed Mill Division Manager would:
(a)   be a university graduate or diplomate in Agriculture, Poultry
Science, Agricultural Engineering or Agronomy with at least



ANNEX 10
Page 6
8 years experience in commercial poultry and/or feed mill op-
erations. Experience in a developing country and a working
knowledge of Arabic would be essential;
(b)   assume the responsibility for establishing and the operation
of the poultry-feed mill division including raw material ac-
quisition, processing, marketing, financial management, staff
training, loan application preparation, technical assistance
to the traditional sector poultry producers, and coordinate
with other Divisional Managers and Project staff.
Agricultural Credit Fund
10.       Livestock Credit Officer would:
(a) be a university graduate or diplomate in Agricultural Economics,
Farm Management, Animal Husbandry or Agricultural Science, includ-
ing a livestock bias with at least five years experience working
on livestock of agricultural systems with Government or private
banks; a working knowledge of Arabic would be desirable; and
(b) be responsible directly to the ACF General Manager for the prep-
aration and administration of a livestock credit system, including
formats and procedures for farm/ranch development plans on both
technical and creditworthiness grounds. His duties would include
the preparation and implementation of a training program for YAR
credit technicians. He would establish a supervised credit and
collection system for ACF sub-loans, supervise and coordinate the
activities of the Farm Management Extension Officer, and where
possible assist all Project related staff including those in
SURDUP and IDA.
11.       Farm Management Extension Officer would:
(a) be a university graduate in Agricultural Science or its equivalent
with a Farm Management bias and have at least 5 years experience
involving agricultural credit, extension and professional farm
management; on appreciation of and experience with the need to
approach farm development on a whole enterprise basis involving
both agriculture and livestock, technology and economics would be
essential; experience in a developing country and some knowledge
of Arabic would be desirable; and
(b) be responsible to the ACF General Manager through the Livestock
Credit Officer for field credit supervision in particular, the
preparation of farm development plans and the appraisal of loan
applications, supervision of loan disbursement and farm development
and the training of Branch Office Managers and Extension workers.



ANNEX 10
Page 7
12.       Accountant - Financial Controller would:
(a) be a fully qualified accountant with experience, preferably in a
developing country, in all corporate audit, accounting and finan-
cing procedures; a working knowledge of Arabic would be essential;
and
(b) be responsible directly to the General Manager of ACF and for all
duties common to this type of post. He would also be responsible
for coordinating his acccounts with a system to be developed by
the Financial Assistant of LDC and to train all ACF aclministration
and office staff including those in the various branch offices,
in complete procedures for the efficient operation of ACF as a
commercial company. He would train a local successor to take
over his responsibilities at the completion of his contract.
Animal Health
13.       Project Manager - This post is presently financed and filled by an on-
going bilateral assistance project for Veterinary Health (ODM). It is expected
that ODM will renew its program and that this post will continue to be supplied
under the new technical assistance bilateral agreement.
14.       Senior Veterinary Officer - One veterinary officer is presently sup-
plied under the ODM programme; his responsibilities are and wil:L continue as
officer-in-charge of the central diagnostic laboratory and as assistant to
the Project Manager. Provision has been made under the project to finance one
Senior Veterinary Officer who would be recruited by ODM to the same standard
as those staff already on-station in YAR. ODM would also furnish under the
technical assistance bilateral agreement, a second senior veterinary officer,
a veterinary investigation officer, 2 veterinary laboratory teclnicians, 5
livestock officers, an administrative officer and an animal hea:Lth training
officer.
Livestock and Range Improvement
15.       Range Management Specialist would:
(a)  possess a university degree, preferably at post-graduaIte level in
agronomy, agricultural science, or plant science, with some animal
husbandry/management bias, have at least 5 years field experience
in applied research, farm management extension, or range improve-
ment, part of which must have been in a low rainfall semi-arid
climate. He must also have experience in and understianding of
the pasture/sheep complex as it relates to animal maniagement;
experience in a developing country where community grazing of
livestock is customary would be an advantage; and



ANNEX 10
Page 8
(b)  through the ODM Project Manager assume responsibility, in
conjunction with the Livestock Production Specialist, for
establishment and operation of the Range Improvement component
of the Project, including a program of applied research designed
to devise economic management systems for the improvement of YAR
rangelands, and in particular systems which take into account
indigenous socio-economic customs; assist other project staff and
advise the Project Manager on the establishment and operation of
the proposed Village Development Associations; assist with training
of Yemeni extension workers and technicians, and advise the Project
Manager on the nature, place and extent of overseas training for
Yemeni staff.
16.       Livestock Production Specialist would:
(a) possess a university degree in agricultural science, animal husbandry,
farm management, or animal nutrition including some agronomy and
plant science; have at least 5 years field experience in applied
research and agricultural extension, part of which must have been
in a low rainfall, semi-arid climate; and
(b) in conjunction with the Range Management Specialist assume respons-
ibility for the livestock and extension aspects of the Range
Improvement and Village Development Association components as
described under (b) above.
Municipalities Slaughterhouse and Markets Department
17.       Slaughterhouse Specialist would:
(a)   be a professionally qualified and experienced slaughterhouse
manager with at least eight years of experience including a
minimum of five years in a developing country; he should have
experience in designing, supervising construction and start up
of new slaughterhouses; he should have experience in training
meat processing techniques to both management and butchers;
(b)   be responsible directly to the Project Manager for the estab-
lishment of the three slaughterhouses, municipal retail meat
markets, hides and skins improvement program, enforcing legis-
lation and operating procedures; he would coordinate closely
with the manager of ACF in identifying and selecting retail
butcher loans and he would be responsible for establishing
training programs for butchers, management and meat inspectors.
18.       Hides and Skins Specialist would:
(a)   be a professionally qualified and experienced hides and skins
specialist with at least ten years experience in hides and



ANNEX 10
Page 9
skins flaying techniques, post slaughter treatment and pre-
servation, storage and transport; he would have experience in
international marketing of hides and skins including grading
standards; and he should have experience in training and de-
monstration techniques for impoving hide and skins quality
for export markets;
(b)   be responsible to the MSMD Manager for the development of a
hides and skins improvement program in YAR that would include
training butchers in the urban slaughterhouses in proper flay-
ing and preservation techniques; he would assist in designing
the new slaughterhouses to assure facilities to properly handle,
preserve and store hides and skins, and he would set up demon-
strations throughout YAR for improving hides and skins removal,
preservation and handling methods.
Consultant Services
I.   Livestock Finishing Division Farm Site and Wadi Surdud Farm Study
A consultant team of agricultural production and marlketing specialists
and irrigation and groundwater experts would be recruited internationally for
about 24 man-months to make recommendations to Government and the Project Manager
on the most suitable site for the proposed Livestock Finishing Farm and the con-
tinued development and operation of Wadi Surdud State Farm.
The team would consist of:
(a)  a team leader - an agriculturalist experienced in irrigated crop
production in arid and semi-arid environments;
(b) an agricultural engineer with irrigation systems experience;
(c) a soils expert;
(d) a crop marketing specialist;
(e) a livestock farm management specialist with dairy and intensive
feed experience; and
(f) an agricultural economist/financial analyst.
(g) other short-term specialists as considered necessary to comply
with the terms of reference.



ANNEX 10
Page 10
1.   Farm Site Selection
(a) review all existing groundwater, soils, climatic and crop produc-
tion data at present available from various sources for the Tihama
Region; including in particular present and future groundwater
use trends in relation to probable total water supply;
(b) taking account of the objectives of the proposed Livestock
Finishing Division including the type of crop and animal
production proposed, and using the data derived from (a) above,
examine the alternative sites available, including Wadi Surdud
Farm, and recommend to Government the one considered most suitable;
(c) the consultants would inter alia, have particular regard to water
availability and quality, soil characteristics and their suitability
for irrigation and fodder and agricultural crop production, any
climatic constraints, road access, existing land use, availability
and capacity of markets, and the desire to bring new land under
irrigation.
2.   Design Work
Once the final site was selected the consultants would:
(a) Prepare detailed designs, working drawings, and tender, advertise-
ment and contract documents including specification for all elements
of the Project, including: (1) Land grading, (2) Tubewell construc-
tion including pumps, and equipment, (3) Canal system with struc-
tures, (4) Drainage system with structures, (5) Road system with
structures, and (6) Domestic and stock water system;
(b) Prepare a schedule for tendering so that the irrigation component
would be completed in a timely fashion;
(c) Advise LDC on issuing the tender, advertisement and contract docu-
ments on the pumping equipment (separate from the civil works);
(d) Advise LDC on the issuing of the tendering documents on civil
works; and
(e) analyze bids and make award recommendations for the pumping
equipment and the civil works.
3.   Wadi Surdud Farm Study
(a) a complete review of farm activities and performances in the past;



ANNEX 10
Page 11
(b) recommend revised production patterns and their interrelationships -
i.e. cotton, rice and other products with livestock byproduct util-
ization opportunities, feed grain production or livestock fattening
and dairying integrated with intensive forage production;
(c) market potentials and domestic and foreign price expectations
for surpluses;
(d) the integration of large-scale low-cost dairy production;
(e) recommendations for harvesting, transportation, storage, process-
ing and wholesaling of farm production;
(f) a corporate structure suitable for public and private sector;
(g) an administrative structure including organization and manage-
ment procedures;
(h) a technical support package and technical staff requirements;
(i) complete financial estimates, including incremental development
and working capital, rates of return estimates, cash flow pro-
jections after debt services and a recapitalization schedule;
(j) the identification and preparation of an expansion program to
optimize economy of scale benefits.
II. Dairy Production Division - Farm Site Study
An international consultant team of agricultural production, and
irrigation and groundwater experts would be recruited for up to 24 man-months
to make recommendations to Government and the Project Manager on the selection
of suitable sites for the proposed dairy farms to be established near Sana'a,
Taiz and Ibb.
The team would consist of:
(a) a team leader - an agriculturalist experienced in irrigated
crop production preferably with livestock integration;
(b) a livestock farm management specialist with dairy and intensive
feed experience;
(c) an agricultural/groundwater engineer with irrigation systems ex-
perience, and preferably a good knowledge of soils and their
requirements for irrigation; and
(d) an agricultural economist/financial analyst;



ANNEX 10
Page 12
(e) other short term specialists as considered necessary to comply
with the terms of reference.
The terms of reference would include:
1.   Farm Site Selection
(a) review all existing groundwater, soils, climatic and crop produc-
tion data for the Sana'a, Ibb, and Taiz areas where dairy farms
sites are proposed;
(b) examine in detail the precise area (survey, if necessary) of land
proposed for each site, and if necessary prepare an existing land
use and tenure plan, so that the area of private, wafd, or govern-
ment land is known;
(c) having due regard to the project proposals, including the establish-
ment of milk colonies, forage production, milk marketing, future
development expansion and environmental factors, to the data de-
rived from (a) and (b) above, and in particular the availability
of both adequate land and water, recommend to Government and to
the Project Manager, the most suitable farm sites.
2.   Design Work
For at least the first two sites, the consultants would:
(a) Prepare detailed designs, working drawings, and if necessary,
tender, advertisement and contract documents including specifica-
tion for all elements of the Project, including: (1) Land grading,
(2) Tubewell construction including pumps and equipment, (3) Canal
system with structures, (4) Drainage system with structures, (5)
Road system with structures, and (6) Domestic and stock water system.
(b) Prepare a schedule for tendering so that the irrigation component
would be completed in a timely fashion;
(c) Advise LDC on issuing the tender, and advertisement and contract
documents on the pumping equipment - (separate from the civil works);
(d) Advise LDC on the issuing of the tendering documents on civil works; and
(e) Analyze bids and make award recommendations for the pumping equipment
and the civil works.
3.   Farm Development Plan
In conjunction with the Project Manager the consultants would prepare
detailed farm development plans including, inter alia, initial layout, farm



ANNEX 10
Page 13
building design, crop rotation, fodder conservationn facilities, livestock feed-
ing and milking facilities, and farm machinery. In accordance with the require-
mnents of ACF, and the Project Manager, the plans would be supported by cost
estimates including investment, projected income and cash flow statements, and
economic evaluation.

The Poultry-feed component and the Milk Colony components are very sensitive to
reductions in the margin between revenues and raw material costs. This is to be
expected because in these cases raw material costs constitute a high percentage
of revenues. Conversely, the Landowner farm components and the VDA show small
sensitivities because they have no substantial raw material costs. Normally,
this margin is kept constant, such that the sales price is adjusted to changes



ANNEX 12
Page 2
in raw material costs; consequently, the risk shown is not unduly high, but
rather a function of the structure of these enterprises.
2.        All income statements and cash flow statements for models have been
projected at constant 1975 prices. No changes in relative prices are foreseen.

ANNEX 14

APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
Economic Rate of Return of the Total Project and Components
1.        Overall Benefits.  Overall, the Project has a calculated economic
rate of return of 27%. Additional, non-quantifiable, benefits which will
accrue to the YAR include:
(a) Improvement to public health through upgraded conditions
of slaughtering and vending meat,
(b) The training of a substantial number of Yemeni in diverse
skills, including management and administration, animal
husbandry, and veterinary technology,
(c) The introduction of new enterprises and methods on a pilot
basis which can be expected to provide a framework for
future expansion. These include dairy farming, live-
stock finishing, smallholder commercial poultry, and
timber management,
(d) Building institutions which will contribute to the coun-
try's progress beyond the present Project.
2.        Price Assumptions.  The economic rates of return are based on
specific price assumptions for input and output components. The signific-
ant price assumption are as follows:
(a) Output Prices
(i) Beef Cattle: YRls 10,000 per ton (US$2,200/T), equivalent
to the estimated landed price at Hodeidah of meat of simi-
lar quality.
(ii) Mutton: YRls 9,000 per ton (US$2,000/T), equivalent to the
estimated landed price at Hodeidah of meat of a similar
quality.
(iii) Poultry: YRls 8,000 per ton (US$1,800/T), equivalent to the
estimated landed price at Hodeidah of meat of a similar
quantity without price penalties attached to very small
orders.



ANNEX 14
Page 2
(iv)  Eggs:  YRls 9,000 per ton (US$2,000/T), based on observed
prices in the YAR of imported eggs.
(v) Milk: YRls 2,000 per ton (US$440/T), or YRls 2/liter, based
on reported import prices in the Yemen.
(vi) Hides and Skins: A 30% added value increase over before
project export income based upon improved processing tech-
nology.
(b) Input Prices
(i) Labor: Labor was not specifically shadow priced for the
calculation since unemployment is not a serious problem in
Yemen. Although available data are not wholly dependable,
information from the YAR preliminary census results and
from WHO provides a round indicator of the limited size
of the domestic labor pool. WHO has estimated that 53.1%
of the resident population comprises those withirn the 15-
65 age group, or the "working age" sector. Since men ac-
count for the major portion of the work force especially
in urban centers, a group supplemented by female partic-
ipation only in rural areas in agricultural activities,
extrapolation from census data reveals a domestic working
age male population of only about 500,000:
Total estimated population                 6,471,893
Male population (50%)                      3,235,946
Male population in "working age"
group (53.1%)                            1,718,187
Yemeni outside the country 1/               1,234,000
Resident "working age" males                 484,287
Although boys often do hold employment before entry into the
formal "working age" category, the figure above appears
strikingly low and indicative of lack of domestic manpower
surpluses. Availability of higher paying employment in
nearly countries, especially Saudi Arabia, has induced
a male manpower drainage from Yemen currently estimated at
1.2 million. Additionally, urban populations reflect
1/   Census officials reported that only a very small percentage of those out-
side the country were women and that most represented men employed in
nearly Middle Eastern states, especially Saudi Arabia.



ANNEX 14
Page 3
significant sex ratio imbalances as males have migrated
internally seeking better jobs off the farm.
(ii) Imported investment items have been valued at landed
price Hodeidah. Other inputs have been prices in conform-
ity with prices prevailing at the end of 1975. It has
been assumed that these input prices sufficiently reflect-
ed the marginal value product of the respective factor
of production making additional shadow pricing unnecessary.
Only the price of petroleum products was adjusted for
taxes.
(c) Exchange Rate.
The prevailing exchange rate of YRls 1.00 = US$0.22 has been em-
ployed in the economic analysis. The rate con tinues to display
market stability bolstered by overall surpluses in balance of
payments reflecting significant remittances from workers abroad
as well as foreign aid for development and budget-
ary obligations. Currencies are easily convertible within Yemen
in conformity with this rate.
3.        Benefits of Respective Components.  Measurable economic benefits
attributable to the project would flow from the following components:
(a) Livestock Development Corporation (LDC)
(b) Animal Health Division (AHD)
(c) Hides and Skins component of Municipality Slaughterhouses
and Market Department (MSMD)
(d) Village Development Associations (VDA)
(e) Supervised Credit to the Traditional Sector
4.        The benefits from the LDC would be as follows:
(a) Eight dairy farms and five milk colonies which would produce
3,125 tons of milk per year at full development (including
the dairy component of the Livestock Finishing Farm).
(b) A Livestock Finishing Farm which would produce 283 tons of
meat per year (carcass equivalent) at full development, of
which 197 tons would be sheepmeat and the balance beef.
(c) A poultry and feed mill unit which would produce 150 tons of
poultry meat per year and 3,000 tons per year of feed at



ANNEX 14
Page 4
full capacity. The net economic benefits of the feed mill
will be the value added at the mill, which is the cost of
mixing and bagging the ingredients.
5.        The economic benefit of decreased mortality of cattle (including
dairy), sheep, goats, and poultry populations is calculated as the incre-
mental annual production, at their economic prices, resulting from the animal
health program. This is calculated as 1-1/2% per annum growth incremental
to pre-project growth of 1% per annum. The base figures for estimated pop-
ulations and offtakes are given in Annex 2, Table 5. The net benefit takes
into account the costs of the animal health program (Annex 8, Tables 1 and
2), and also the costs of the Livestock and Range Improvement Center (Annex
9, Table 1), as this part of the project should be allocated against greater
meat and dairy production.
6.        The economic benefits of the MSMD program include both quantifiable
and non-quantifiable benefits. The latter are chiefly the considerable
public health benefits arising from the improved hygiene of new abattoirs and
meat markets. The condition of such facilities as presently exist is con-
sidered injurious to the public health, and this is confirmed by the wide-
spread occurrence in the YAR human population of internal parasites.
7.        The quantifiable benefit are the improved value of hides and skins
resulting from better techniques at the time of slaughter. It is estimated
that a 30% increase in export prices will be attained from a substantial
proportion of hides and skins. Taking as a base 197-exports of YRls 3,066
million, the incremental benefits of the program are projected as rising to
YRls one million in project year 5 and increasing at 5% per annum thereafter.
8.        The economic benefit from the VDA includes sales of timber and the
incremental output from improved pastures, which is projected to be two tons
of sheepmeat per year for each of the 10 VDAs.
9.        The economic benefits from the three components of supervised
credit to the traditional sector are as follows:
(a) Economic benefits projected for the on-farm loans to 24
landowners and 375 tenants include:
(i) incremental meat production of 150 tons
(ii) incremental milk production of 1,600 tons
(iii) incremental milk production of 1,300 tons
(b) Loans to 500 smallholders for poultry production will result in
production of 180 tons of eggs.



ANNEX 14
Page 5
(c) Loans for butcher shop improvement are not projected to result
in quantifiable economic benefits, but will have a beneficial
effect on public health through improved hygiene. The costs
of these butcher shop loans have been allocated against the
supervised credit component in calculating the economic rate of
return.
10.       Institution Building.  The Project will substantially strengthen the
Agricultural Credit Fund, will help build the Animal Health Division, and
will create the Livestock Development Corporation, as well as the Livestock
and Range Improvement Division. This institution building will have favorable
but non-quantifiable effects for the future of the Yemen economy in increasing
capacity to implement expanded activities in livestock development and related
activities.
11.       Foreign Exchange Earnings.  Foreign exchange earnings and savings
under the project are projected as follows:
(a) Milk production: 4,725 tons production is projected at full
development of new enterprises, plus 11,100 tons of dairy
products (in project year 5) attributable to the animal
health component. At a value of US$440 per ton the net fo-
reign exchange savings will be US$7 million per year.
(b) Hides and Skins: the incremental value is projected as
rising to US$0.22 million in project year 5.
(c) Meat output attributable to the project is projected at
4,000 tons in project year 5, the value of which is equi-
valent to US$8 million
(d) Grain output attributable to the project--from tenant farm
production--is projected at 1,300 tons per year. This out-
put may be projected at US$0.3 million per year; valued at
the import price of wheat in order to calculate import sub-
stitution savings.
12.       Training.  Considerable, but non-quantifiable, benefits will re-
sult from training of Yemeni nationals under the project. Major categories
of training will be:
(a) On-the-job training by expatriates
(b) Budget grants for formal training
(c) Training of farmers in specific skills by extension workers.
Over the project, the acquisition of skills by Yemeni is expected to be an
important contribution to the country, especially considering the present
small percentage of trained personnel in the Yemen.



ANNEX 14
Page 6
13.       Impact of the Project on Employment.  Number of jobs expected to
be created under th project for Yemeni nationals total 458:
(a)  LDC:                               237 jobs
(b)  AHD:                                78 jobs
(c)  ACF:                                24 jobs
(d)  Other:                             119 jobs
No additional employment will be created through the smallholder poultry
loans as they will use family labor. No additional employment: is assumed to
be created by the three municipal abattoirs as they will recruit butchers
and allied workers who currently follow the trade.
14.       Distribution of Benefits.  Project benefits will be widely distri-
buted:
(a)  The animal health program will benefit farmers throughout the
country. Of first importance will be reduced mortality to
work oxen, which provide the power for most of the country's
agriculture. In addition, owners of smallstock and poultry
throughout the country will benefit from the prograrn.
(b) On-farm loans to 500 smallholders (poultry loans), :375 tenant
farmers, and 24 landowners will benefit the rural sectors.
The VDAs will also contribute to rural income.
(c) LDC production of milk, poultry, and meat will mostly be
marketed in urban area, thereby contibuting to improved nutri-
tion.
(d) MSMD activities in abattoirs and markets will improve the
public health of urban dwellers.
15.       Impact of Additional Capacity on Prices.  Additions to the sup-
ply of meat and milk due to the project will approximate 1-1/2% of the total
supply. However, because the rate of increase in consumer demand cannot be
estimated with any accuracy, the quantitative effect on prices is unknown.
It may be concluded, however, that this additional capacity will have a
dampening effect on prices, and will be beneficial in a country where meat
and milk prices are high relative to consumer income.
16.       The poultry output may be expected to result in low,er prices in
certain markets such as Taiz, where economics of scale should enable poultry
meat to be sold at a lower price per kg than pre-project prices.



ANNEX 14
Page 7
17.       Economic Rates of Return by Component.  The overall rate of re-
turn to the Project is 27%. Sensitivity testing for 10% increase in invest-
ment costs, and separately for 10% increase in operating costs and a 10%
decrease in benefits both for the Project as a whole and for the individual
components (Table 1) reveals that reductions in rates of return do not fall
below 22% for the project. The animal health program has a very high return,
as might be expeced from the initiation of effective measures where little has
been done in the past. The public health effects of the new abattoirs and
food markets have not been possible to quantify and setting their entire costs
against the quantifiable benefits arising from improved hides and skins income
results in a very low economic rate of return.

ANNEX 15
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
LIVESTOCK CREDIT AND PROCESSING PROJECT
YEMEN ARAB REPUBLIC
Project Monitoring and Evaluation
1.        It is essential to establish from project initiation efficient sys-
tems for (a) monitoring progress of project implementation, and (b) continuous
evaluation of the impact of the project on institutions, on livestock and
poultry development and on participating beneficiaries. The monitoring system
would record progress in relationship to project objectives, goals and sched-
ules established for each project component. It would enable modifications
and improvements to be made to the project as well as to provide an early
warning of project implementation problems. The evaluation system would en-
able project management and policy makers to make necessary adjustments in
policies, objectives, institutional arrangements and resources in order that
the project can achieve its objectives. It would make possible to use experi-
ence gained in the early stages of project implementation to be applied appro-
priately to subsequent stages of the project and importantly to repeater
projects.
2.        Project monitoring and evaluation would be carried out by the PU, LDC
and ACF during project implementation. Overall responsibility would be with
the Project Unit Manager who will work closely with the division managers.
The Project Unit Manager would establish an Evaluation Unit made up of repre-
sentatives of the various Divisions, LDC, and ACF and employ short-term field
staff and specialists as necessary. The functions of the Evaluation Unit
would include:
(a) Monitoring of the overall progress of the project in relation
to the implementation schedule and identification of likely
problems.
(b) Carrying out studies and surveys to determine the impact of
the project on agricultural and livestock production, on
the institutions and on the beneficiaries, using the services
of the local staff supplemented by specialists when necessary.
Information collected will be kept to the minimum necessary
to prepare quarterly progress reports and annual evaluation
reports.
(c) Conducting special surveys as necessary.
3.        Objectives, and factors to monitor and evaluate for each project
component are illustrated in the following paragraphs. During initial super-
vision the Project Unit Manager and IDA would develop key indicators for
meaningful monitoring and evaluation.



ANNEX 15
Page 2
4.        Livestock Development Corporation.  The three divisions - Dairy
Farms, Livestock Finishing Farm, and Poultry/Feed Mill Division -- have as
their objectives (a) production of meat, milk, and eggs, (b) demonstration of
techniques, (c) improvements in the marketing system. The LDC Manager would
monitor (a) output figures, (b) activities to promote demonstration, and any
known implementation resulting therefrom, (c) data on distribution costs and
retail prices with and without project and effect of diverting land to project
component use.
5.        Slaughterhouses and Meat Markets.  The objectives are (a) improved
public hygiene, (b) improved retail meat quality, and (c) increased export
earnings from hides and skins. Accordingly, the MSMD Manager will monitor
(a) nature and results of inspections of the facilities concerned (b) distri-
bution costs and retail prices (c) output and grade of hides and skins from
the facilities, if possible with reference to project impact on quality im-
provement.
6.        Supervised Credit.  The objectives of on-farm programs are (a) in-
creased production of food products and increased income to producer families
(b) improved techniques and management practices at the farm level, and (c)
more efficient retail meat marketing. In line with these objectives, the
General Manager of ACF will monitor, (a) output and income figures for bene-
ficiaries of on-farm credit (based on sample surveys; and attempting to
analyze impact with and without project), (b) actual observed benefits arising
from improved meat retailing, and (c) distribution of credit.
7.        Livestock Range Improvement and Village Development Association.
The objectives of these programs are (a) to improve carrying capacity through
applied research, (b) to communicate these methods through demonstration and
extension (c) to promote livestock improvement practices, and (d) to increase
income in ten "Village Development Associations," each composed of about five
sub-villages. Accordingly, the LRID Manager would monitor similar character-
istics as those detailed under "Supervised Credit" above.
8.        Animal Health.  The objectives of the animal health program include
controlling disease, and reducing mortality, with consequent increased pro-
ductivity of livestock in their functions as draft animals and providers of
food and hyproducts. The AHD Manager would monitor (a) the disease control
program, (b) quarantine effectiveness of imported livestock, and (c) incidence
of disease.
9.        Training.  Training of Yemeni counterparts is an important objective
of the Project. Division Managers will monitor the response of trainees, and
report to the Project Manager.
10.       Overall Objectives of Program.  In addition to the objectives listed
above under the various project components, there are the following overall
objectives (a) institution building, (b) import substitution, (c) upgrading
nutrition, (d) extension of credit to target groups.



ANNEX 15
Page 3
11.       Evaluation.  The on-going evaluation of the Project by the Project
Manager will arise from analysis of the monitoring functions performed at divi-
sional level. This will be performed within the context of macroeconomic and
other considerations. The output of the evaluation will enable the Project
Manager to adjust management emphasis and deploy resources as necessary as
well as to recommend policy changes in his annual report to the investors.
